"Morning, Mrs Harrow," he called, not looking up. His cap was pulled down to his eyebrows and one sleeve had been darned with green wool that did not match the coat. "You heading far?"

"Depot first," she said. "You seen Tom?"

He shook his head and leaned on the shovel for air. "Haven't seen my own son since Sunday and he lives two streets over. If you've got someone indoors, keep him there. This lot's got a nasty mind to it."

That was as close to sympathy as Henshaw ever gave anyone. Elsie nodded and kept moving.

Mrs Bhatti's corner shop was dark except for the back room, where a strip of yellow light lay across stacked crates of onions. The front shutter was down to shoulder height. Elsie bent and ducked under.

Mrs Bhatti stood at the till in fingerless gloves, ledger open, pencil tucked into the knot of her braid like a hairpin. She had tied her sari hem up over her ankles for traction and was still in her house slippers, the felt soaked at the toes. A kettle steamed on a single gas ring near the biscuits.

"You're out in this?" Mrs Bhatti said. "You should be at home with your feet up."

"Have you seen Tom this morning?"

"Not since last night, and he was not buying groceries then either." She snapped the ledger shut with two fingers and studied Elsie's face, not her belly. "He was with Blackwell and that little ferret from the tyre place. Smelled like stale stout and damp dogs. Tried to put Players and a flask on your tab while he was at it."

Elsie felt the answer land low in her stomach. "On my tab?"

"Aye. Said you'd cover it Thursday after the pram people took their pound. I told him he could pay cash or freeze." Mrs Bhatti poured tea into a chipped mug and pushed it across the counter. "He said he was off to the depot to squeeze Doyle for wages first."

"I can't stay."

"Then take it with you." Mrs Bhatti lowered her voice. "My eldest rang from Bradford at dawn. Lorries are folded across the road there and nobody's settling accounts on time today. If Tom's chasing cash, catch him before weather catches you."

Elsie wrapped both hands around the mug anyway, letting the heat bite her palms for three breaths before she set it down. "If he made it to the depot, he'll have to be there."

Mrs Bhatti gave a short, irritated hum, the sound she made when a customer asked for credit she could not afford to extend. "If you pass back this way, knock hard. I'll be in the storeroom counting tins and pretending I trust people to pay me in spring."

That, too, was kindness.

The bus depot sat behind a wire fence at the edge of town, a yard usually loud with engines, conductors shouting routes, men smoking in clumps by the gatehouse. Today it was muffled and wrong. Three buses stood angled in the snow like grounded boats, windscreen wipers lifted, windows furred white. The big doors were chained. No diesel tang, no roar, only the clatter of a loose sign on one hinge.

A man in a railway coat emerged from the gatehouse when she rattled the chain. She recognized him after a second: Stan Doyle, dispatch office, a narrow face and the same nicotine cough as Tom but deeper in the chest. He wore his scarf twice round and still kept pressing two fingers at his throat between sentences.

"Mrs Harrow?" he said. "Shouldn't be out."

"I'm looking for Tom. He was on early shift."

Stan's eyes flicked away toward the frozen yard, then back. "No one had a shift after seven. They pulled all runs."

"He signed in before that?"

Stan moved his weight, boots crunching. "Didn't sign in at all."

For a moment she thought she had misheard him because of the wind. "Check again."

"I checked board, locker, and wage room." He hesitated, then reached into his coat and unfolded a damp pink duplicate slip. "Night clerk logged this at quarter to midnight. Harrow and Blackwell signed out a depot storm lamp for mill bend. Lamp never came back."

She stared at the blurred names, Tom's surname cramped and slanted. "Who authorized that?"

"No one sensible." Stan coughed into his fist. "Rail police rang before the exchange died. Said they'd pulled an unidentified depot man off the viaduct side and wanted payroll numbers. Couldn't give a name."

She gripped the chain so hard the links imprinted her palm through the glove. "Who was he with yesterday?"

"Could've been anyone." Stan spat into the snow, then seemed to remember who he was speaking to and wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. "Blackwell was bragging about cards at the Crown before they went out. Peggy will know who else was there."

"After wages were late again."

He gave a bleak little half-smile. "Aye. Hard to spend what you haven't got. Doesn't stop them trying."

She watched him, waiting for him to add something else, something useful, some scrap that would let her walk in one direction instead of all of them. He only pulled his coat tighter and glanced toward the office door where a telephone sat on a shelf inside. She followed his look.

"Can I use your line?" she asked.

"Dead since nine. Whole exchange keeps dropping."

He said it quickly, like apology and warning together. Elsie nodded once and headed for the high street.

By the time she reached Calderbank High Street the weather had thickened into white grit. She kept one hand on walls when she could, reading shopfronts by memory because signs blurred out at ten feet. The butcher had sandbags piled against his door. The pharmacy window had been boarded from inside. A boy no older than thirteen skidded past hauling a sack of coal on a pram chassis and grinned at her, face red with cold and triumph.

The Crown and Shuttle had half its windows steamed over and the other half filmed with frost. Someone had spread sawdust across the threshold to keep people from breaking their necks on the flagstones. Elsie pushed in and the warmth hit her hard enough to make her eyes water.

The room smelled of wet wool, pipe smoke, and spilled bitter gone sticky at the edges. Men were lined three deep at the bar though it was not yet midday, coats on, hats on, all of them listening with one ear to the radio propped near the optics. No one laughed. Even the regular domino table was empty.

Behind the taps, Peggy Lomas was polishing the same glass in furious circles. Peggy had broad wrists, a silver St Christopher around her neck, and a way of seeing trouble before it crossed the room. She looked up, took in Elsie's coat and belly and white face, and came round the bar without being asked.

"You all right, love?"

"I'm looking for Tom Harrow. He was in last night."

Peggy's mouth tightened. "He was. Came in loud and left louder."

"When did he leave?"

"Half eleven with Blackwell and two fitters. Went on about cards in the old rail sheds."

"With who?"

Peggy glanced at the men nearest the door, then back at Elsie. "Not here."

"Not here where?"

"Not this side of the room." Peggy took Elsie by the elbow and steered her toward the corridor by the gents where the noise dropped by half. "You don't need a lot of eyes on this."

Elsie pulled free, not hard. "I need to know where my husband is."

"And I need this bar kept on its feet." Peggy lowered her voice until it was almost a growl. "He came back once just before close, left sleeve soaked and one side of his face swelling. Asked for salt, a rag, and brandy like I'd turned into casualty. Said Blackwell had glassed him over a card pot, then bolted back out toward mill bend."

"He was bleeding?"

"From somebody's face, maybe his. Couldn't tell in that light." Peggy's expression changed, softened one inch. "If there was blood and police talk, the bridge post will have a log. Start there before full closure. Council rang before the line went to hell."

A man from the bar called out, "Peg, you serving or sermonizing?" and got a few low chuckles for his trouble.

Peggy didn't turn. "You want another pint, you can wait till my hands are free." Then to Elsie, quieter: "If he stumbles in, I'll send someone straight down Barrow Lane."

Elsie heard herself ask the stupid question anyway. "He wasn't dying?"

"Not when he left the second time."

That was not comfort; it was a narrow strip of floor over a deep place. Elsie nodded, thanked her, and left before the room could start watching again.

The old rail sheds were a blind idea and she knew it, but she walked halfway there before the wind beat her back. Snow had drifted thigh-high against the embankment. A constable waved her off from the bridge, face wrapped in a scarf, eyes streaming.

"Road's closed past this point," he shouted. "Turn around now."

"My husband's missing. Tom Harrow. Depot."

The constable swore into his scarf and squinted through blowing snow. "We dragged one depot bloke out from under mill bend after one. Head cut to the bone, half-frozen, no wallet. First-aid at Town Hall, then casualty wagon to Calderbank Infirmary."

Her breath snagged. "Was it Tom?"

"Couldn't tell you. Face was swollen and he wouldn't wake." He pointed his baton toward town. "Casualty desk opens at first light if staff can get in. You check there. Right now you go home before I end up carrying two people in the drifts."

He said it like a man reciting orders off wet paper. She hated him for being right.

On Barrow Lane the snowplough came through once and gave up, leaving a ridge of compacted ice down the middle. Doors were shut. Curtains twitched and fell still. At number three, Mrs Caulfield opened her top window an inch and leaned out in a maroon dressing gown with a fox-fur collar rubbed thin at the tips.

"Elsie!" she called. "You found him?"

"No."

"Try his brother in Hebden. Men always go to brothers when they've done something stupid."

"I don't have that number."

Mrs Caulfield sniffed and drew her gown tighter. "Neither do I. Peter Walsh might. He keeps a little black book with all his useful people in it."

The window shut. Elsie stood a second in blowing snow, wanting to throw something at the glass and having nothing in her hands.

She let herself into Harrow House with fingers so numb she had to shoulder the door twice before the latch caught. Indoors felt colder than the street. The fire had slumped to dull red and a film of ice feathered the inside of the scullery tap. She bent to rake the grate, then had to sit on the kitchen chair with the poker still in her hand because the baby had shifted heavy and low and her back had gone into a hard, warning ache.

It eased after a minute. She breathed through her nose until she could stand again.

On the back of the torn envelope she'd left under the sugar tin, she wrote in pencil with her hand still shaking: Dawn if no Tom. Noreen to use Peter's line for Calderbank casualty and Tom's brother in Hebden if the exchange came back. Elsie to Town Hall first-aid board, then bridge police log. Ask Henshaw for viaduct ditches only if no hospital record. She folded the scrap and shoved it into her coat pocket. Random knocking had got her nowhere; in the morning they would work from names and ledgers, not rumor.

The telephone at the Walshes was the only private line on the row. Peter had it because Peter had forms and authority and friends who mattered after office hours. Elsie hated needing his things, but need was not interested in her pride. She crossed the yard, knocked, and waited with snow melting down her collar.

Noreen opened in a cardigan over her dress, hair pinned up any old way, flour still on one wrist. She had been baking, probably because kneading gave her something to push against that wasn't her own life. The house behind her smelled of yeast and onions and paraffin.

"Christ, look at you," Noreen said, pulling her in by the sleeve. "You're frozen through."

"Can I use your phone?"

"If it works." Noreen shut the door with her hip. "Peter rang at dinner to say council might keep him in town overnight if the roads close. Then the line started crackling in sharp, greasy bursts."

Elsie wiped her boots and followed her to the hall table where the black phone sat under a framed wedding photo turned slightly askew. She lifted the receiver. No dial tone. Only a faint rushing hiss, thin and hollow in her ear.

She tried the operator anyway. Then depot. Then the Crown and Shuttle. The same dead hiss, then nothing.

"Any luck?" Noreen asked.

Elsie set the receiver down too hard and the bell gave a small, useless jangle. "Line's gone."

"You're shaking. Sit down and I'll get tea."

"I should keep trying."

"With what, telepathy? Sit."

Noreen's humor usually came with a grin. This one did not. Elsie sat.

They took tea at the kitchen table with the lamp already lit though it was barely four. The snow outside reflected enough light to make the room look underlit from below, all hard shadows and pale walls. Noreen slid a plate over with two slices of soda bread spread with dripping.

"Eat," she said. "You haven't all day, have you?"

Elsie opened her mouth to deny it and stopped. The lie was pointless between them. She ate, because the baby kicked when she did not.

"Where've you checked?" Noreen asked.

"Mrs Bhatti said he tried to run drink on my account and went chasing wages at the depot. Depot says he never signed in, but they logged him and Blackwell taking a storm lamp to mill bend. Peggy says he came back bleeding, then ran out again. Bridge constable says an unidentified depot man went from Town Hall first-aid to Calderbank casualty after one." Elsie wiped grease from her thumb with the edge of her napkin. "Could be him. Could be someone else."

Noreen listened the way she always did, chin tipped down, eyes on Elsie's hands more than her face. "So we start with casualty desks and police books at dawn. Not every ditch in Calderbank."

"That's your comfort?"

"No." Noreen blew over her mug. "That's the shape of it. Comfort can come later if we find any."

Outside, something heavy scraped along the lane. They both turned. A council lorry crawled past with a loudspeaker lashed to the roof. Static cracked, then a man's voice broke through.

"Emergency notice for Calderbank district. All bus routes suspended with immediate effect. Main roads closed to private traffic until further instruction. Residents are advised to remain indoors after dark. Repeat, remain indoors after dark."

The voice faded and came back as the lorry moved away, and the words carried between houses in pieces: suspended, closed, indoors.

Elsie stared at the steam rising from her tea and did the sums she could not stop doing. No buses meant no depot shift. No shift meant no pay packet. No pay packet meant coal first, food second, pram arrears letter still waiting at the bottom of the tin where she had hidden it. She pressed two fingers into the wood grain of the table to stop herself listing the rest out loud.

Noreen broke the silence. "You're not going back over there tonight."

"I'm going. If he comes home and I'm not there—"

"If he comes home, he'll find a note and two feet of common sense between his ears." Noreen set her mug down hard enough to slosh tea. "Elsie, don't be dense for once in your life. The yard at yours is a slip of ice and your fire's barely going. You stay here."

"Peter won't like it."

"Peter can write a memo to God about it." Noreen stood, already reaching for paper. "We'll leave him one as well, for his precious records."

She wrote quickly, her handwriting round and neat: Elsie staying due closure and failed line. Tom missing since last night. Back in morning if roads clear. She pinned it to the hall mirror with Peter's brass cufflink, then laughed once at the impropriety of that and did not bother apologizing.

As dusk thickened, they crossed to Harrow House together to collect what Elsie needed. Wind shoved at them in the yard. Snow had crusted over the coal scuttle by the back step. Indoors, Elsie moved fast, yanking drawers open and shutting them again before she seemed to register what was inside.

She dropped the tonic bottle into a market bag, stood for a beat with Tom's spare shirt bunched in her fist, then went back for thick socks and a nightdress like she was relearning how to pack in her own kitchen.

Noreen watched without interrupting until Elsie opened the pantry and stood looking at two onions, half a bag of flour, and three tins as if she could shame them into multiplying.

"Pack it in, Elsie," Noreen said, quick now. "Leave the pantry. You'll count the spoons next and freeze doing it."

"Tomorrow I might be digging him out of a drift."

"Or tomorrow he'll crawl back stinking of stout and cards and you'll skin him with the bread knife. Both options can wait till morning."

The joke landed better this time. Elsie almost smiled.

They banked the Harrow fire deep, checked the back latch twice, and left the kitchen clock wound on the mantel where Tom could see it if he came in. Elsie wrote a second note and propped it in front of the sugar tin: At Noreen's. Lines down. Knock hard.

By seven the power failed.

Power died in a single snap. The bulb over Noreen's stairs cut out mid-flicker so that one moment they were two women in a narrow hallway and the next they were shadows with matches. Wind thudded in the chimney. Somewhere down the lane a child started crying, then was hushed.

Noreen lit two candles, one in a saucer on the table and one in a jam jar by the sink. "There," she said. "Grand hotel." She pulled the curtain tighter against the window and fed paraffin into the heater with careful hands. "We'll keep one room warm and shut the rest."

The kitchen shrank around the flame. Every sound from outside arrived sharper for the lack of electricity: slate rattling, the gate chain tapping, the far bark of a dog that would not settle. Elsie sat with her back to the range and both hands around a hot-water bottle wrapped in a tea towel. Heat spread slowly through her coat and stopped at her knees.

Noreen was never still for long. She kneaded dough for morning rolls, wiped flour from the table with the side of her hand, checked the paraffin, rewrapped the kettle lid with cloth to keep the heat in. She talked as she moved, little practical things about whose pipes had burst last year and how to keep oranges from freezing if you buried them in flour, like she was laying planks over a hole.

Elsie let her. Listening was easier than speaking.

Near nine there was a knock so sudden and loud they both flinched. Noreen opened the door to a gust of snow and a young postman in the wrong coat, one sleeve pinned up where his left arm should have been. He held out two telegram envelopes in his gloved hand and looked embarrassed before he spoke.

"Not for this number," he said. "For next street, but I can't make that lane now. Can you hold them till morning?"

Noreen took the envelopes. "Of course. Come in and warm yourself."

"Can't. They've got me doubling route and I still need to clear three roads." He shifted his satchel higher with his shoulder stump in one practiced motion. "You ladies stay inside, yeah? They've closed the viaduct. One bus already slid sideways near mill bend."

He gave a brief, almost shy nod and stepped back into the storm before either woman could answer.

Noreen shut the door slowly. "I thought we'd heard all the bad news there was. Turns out weather finds new pockets."

They ate stew from the pot, thin but hot. Elsie tasted mostly pepper and tin. Halfway through, the baby rolled hard enough to make her suck air through her teeth.

"Pain?" Noreen asked at once.

"No. Just elbowing for room."

"Cheeky little thing." Noreen's smile was brief, then gone. "You're not bleeding?"

Elsie shook her head.

Noreen looked relieved and then guilty for showing it. "Sorry. I ask everyone that now. I asked Mrs Caulfield that this morning when she said she'd slipped in her yard. Woman's sixty-two and I asked if she was bleeding like she was one of the clinic mothers."

"You can ask me." Elsie scraped her spoon around the bowl. "I'd rather that than people saying, 'You'll be fine,' when they don't know."

Noreen sat down across from her with her own bowl and did not eat yet. Candlelight sharpened her face, turning her mouth serious. "Did nobody ask you last time?"

The question was simple and hit with the blunt shock of metal on tile.

Elsie looked at the candle, then at the spoon in her hand, then at nothing. She could have said, There isn't a last time. She could have said, It was years ago and not worth dragging through this kitchen. She could have got up and stacked plates and made herself useful until midnight. Instead she heard herself say, "No. They asked Tom if he wanted tea while I was still pushing."

Noreen's fingers stilled around her bowl.

Elsie set the spoon down carefully. Her palms had started sweating against the pottery. "I was twenty-four. First baby. We were in rooms off Myrtle Street then, above a butcher's, and I thought the pain meant I was doing it right because everyone said pain was the job. We waited too long to call anyone because Tom kept saying first labors take ages and I was making a fuss. By the time the district nurse came my waters had gone green and she swore straight at him before she even got her coat off."

The room pressed closer. Wind shoved at the window and the frame answered with a thin creak.

"They got me to St Luke's in a taxi with no heater," Elsie said. "I remember the vinyl seat sticking to my legs and the driver opening his little flap every five minutes to ask if I was done yet like he had another fare waiting. I pushed for... I don't know. Long enough that the clock changed shifts and I heard two sets of cleaners in the corridor." Her breath snagged. She pressed the heel of her hand to her sternum and swallowed before she could keep going. "I remember everything useless. The pattern on the curtain. The nurse's lipstick bleeding at the corners. The way the metal stirrup was colder than outside."

Noreen did not move.

"When he came out he did not cry," Elsie said, voice flat now, because flat was all she could manage. "He was heavy enough, dark hair, fingers like Tom's mother. He just... did not start. They rubbed him. They put that little mask on and pumped and one of them counted out loud like numbers might bully him into it. Then one nurse put him under a blanket and looked at the wall instead of me."

Elsie dragged her thumb through a ring of spilled stew on the table and watched it smear. "They told Tom first. He stood at the end of the bed with his coat still on and said, 'Right, well, we'll have another,' like he'd missed a bus and was planning the next one."

The shame she had kept boxed for seven years came up not as tears at first but as heat, a dry furious heat behind her eyes. "I asked to hold him and a doctor said it might be better if I remembered him peaceful. Peaceful. Like I was asking for a photograph, not my own child."

Noreen made a small sound in her throat, not a word.

"They gave me pills to dry my milk," Elsie went on, because stopping now would make all of it pointless. "It came in anyway. Through my nightdress, through the sheet, soaking everything. Every woman in that ward with a living baby had a bell by her bed and all night I listened to bells and crying and nurses saying, 'Good girl, latch him,' while my breasts swelled till my skin shone and there was no mouth to put to them."

She could hear that ward now as clearly as if the storm had stripped the years away: metal trolleys, rubber soles, infants rooting and failing and finding. She had never told this whole of it to anyone. Not her mother before she died, not the clinic midwife at checkups, not the women who said sad thing and touched her elbow in the queue.

"I bled for three weeks," she said. "Tom couldn't stand the sight of the cloths drying by the range. Said it put him off his tea. On New Year's Eve he went out and came back singing and I scrubbed the chamber pot while he slept. We never named the baby because Tom said names make a thing harder to forget."

There it was: the ugliest part, the one that still made her feel disloyal to the dead.

Noreen stood and came around the table. For a second Elsie thought she might hug her and stiffened on instinct. Noreen did not. She set a folded dish towel in Elsie's hands first, something to grip, then crouched so they were level.

"He was your son," she said, quick and low. "Named or not. Look at me."

Elsie looked at her. Candlelight trembled over Noreen's cheekbone and caught in the damp at her lash line.

"I never say him out loud," Elsie whispered. "I do. I start to, and my jaw locks. My breasts start aching like that ward is back in my nightdress, wet to the waist. If I say him, it feels like I'm stealing."

"No. Listen to me." Noreen's voice roughened into command. "You're not stealing. They left you to drown in milk and blood and bells, and that was the theft. You saying him is you keeping him."

That cracked whatever was left of Elsie's control. She bent over, elbows on knees, and cried into the towel with her mouth open because the sound had to go somewhere and she could not bear to wake the lane with it. The first sob tore her throat. The second folded her farther down until her forehead hit her wrists. Snot and tears ran together into the cloth; she gagged once on her own breath and coughed without getting air. Noreen kept one hand between her shoulders, steady pressure, nothing theatrical.

When the wave passed, it left hiccups knocking through her chest and a fine tremor in both forearms. She tried to speak and only got, "I-" before her voice split into a rasp. Noreen fetched water and held the mug steady until Elsie's fingers stopped skidding on the handle.

The kitchen sat in an awkward, honest quiet: candle spit, wind at the sash, spoon ticking where Elsie had dropped it against the bowl. Elsie wiped her nose on the dry corner of the towel and tried again. "I shouldn't have. I shouldn't have dumped all that on you."

Noreen waited until the whole sentence was out. Then she wiped the table in slow circles with the hem of her apron because there were no clean cloths left and housekeeping was how she made mercy practical.

"I'd rather you drop truth on me than feed me polite lies like stale cake," she said. Her voice stayed rough, not soft. "So stop apologizing and drink your water."

The candle burned low. They banked the range and carried blankets into the front room where the chimney drew better. Noreen made up the sofa with two quilts, then added her own wool throw without comment. Elsie took off her boots and sat, rubbing warmth back into her toes. The baby shifted again, gentler this time.

"You want me in here with you?" Noreen asked.

"You'll freeze in your own bed if the fire drops."

"Answer the question."

Elsie considered the dark ceiling, the sound of wind finding every crack in the frames, the knowledge that Tom might be in a ditch or in another pub or under another story she would never verify. "Stay till I sleep," she said.

Noreen nodded and sat on the floor with her back against the sofa, quilt wrapped around both of them where it trailed. They listened to the storm settle into its late rhythm, less gust now, more continuous shove. Once, from the other side of the wall, came the deep cough of Mr Henshaw followed by a muttered oath. Ordinary noise. Proof of other bodies enduring the same night.

After a while Noreen said, "At first light we'll start at the infirmary desk. If it's not him, we work outward. Bridge book, sheds, brother, all of it."

"We?"

"Aye, we. Don't make that face. I can still walk and shout and frighten men."

Despite everything, Elsie laughed once into the quilt. It hurt, but it was a laugh.

"Sleep," Noreen said, and squeezed Elsie's ankle through the blanket, brief and matter-of-fact.

Elsie drifted in and out with the candle guttering by the hearth. In one waking moment she heard Noreen in the kitchen, writing the incident details from memory so they would not lose them in the morning. In another she heard the front latch rattle and held her breath until the scrape stretched into the loose, uneven rhythm of wind worrying the hook.

Near dawn the storm eased enough that she could hear individual flakes striking the window in dry ticks. The house had gone cold at the edges, but the center held. Noreen had fallen asleep with her head against the sofa, mouth slightly open, one hand still hooked in the quilt as if holding a rope.

Three hard knocks hit the front door, quick and certain. Noreen jerked awake and swore softly, then padded to the hall and opened up to an ARP warden white with drift. He held out a damp carbon slip and kept his voice at doorway level.

"Mrs Harrow here? Casualty office sent runners where the phones are dead. Thomas Harrow, depot badge 47C, admitted at two ten from mill bend. Scalp laceration, suspected rib fracture, under police hold till he can give a statement."

Elsie was already on her feet. "He's alive?"

"Alive and loud, according to the porter." The warden tapped the number at the bottom of the slip. "Take this to Calderbank Infirmary front desk. They'll move faster with the incident number."

He moved on to the next house before thanks could catch him. Elsie watched him disappear into blowing white, then reached down and tucked the blanket higher over Noreen's shoulder with hands that would not stay steady.

Tom was not in a ditch. Tom was in a bed with his name on a chart and a constable at the door.

Elsie took Noreen's pencil and wrote Tom's incident number on the back of a telegram envelope, then pressed hard enough to score the paper when she wrote his name under it. In the kitchen doorway, yesterday's roasting tin sat in grey dishwater, fat cooled to a pale film. She folded a clean shirt over her arm, touched the slip to make sure it was still there, and did not lie down again. She watched the window lighten and waited to move.


# Chapter 5

Pain hit before Noreen could rinse yesterday's fat from the roasting tin. It was not the usual pulling ache she had lived with for weeks. This was a hard band, hot and mean, cinching from spine to pubic bone as if somebody had looped wire around her and started turning a handle. By evening, the same storm that had sent runners at dawn was still trying to bury the street lamps alive.

She stayed bent over the sink until it eased. Meltwater from her sleeves dripped onto the flagstones. The window above the tap was crusted white from the inside, and the pane rattled under wind that sounded like sand thrown by the handful.

"Peter," she called.

No answer. He was in the front room with the gas fire turned low to save coins, working through council papers he had brought home because he did not trust the junior clerk with totals. She could picture him without seeing him: wire-rim glasses sliding down his nose, cuffs protected by old shirt garters, neat stacks squared at exact right angles on the table, straight paper enlisted to force straight life.

She rinsed her hands, wiped them on her apron, and took two steps toward the hall before another contraction caught her harder than the first. She grabbed the dresser and waited through it, breath hissing through her teeth. When it passed, warmth spread between her thighs.

For a second she told herself it was urine. Women leaked. Women were not machines. Then she looked down.

Blood darkened the inside seam of her underwear and crawled in a thin line along her left stocking. Not bright, not a clean cut. Brown-red, clotted where it met the wool.

She shouted his name again, louder. "Peter. Now."

He came to the doorway frowning, one finger still hooked inside a ledger page to keep his place. "You do not have to shout through the house."

"I'm bleeding."

He blinked once, more irritated than alarmed. "You have had spotting before. Mrs Keane said strain can cause it. Sit down."

"This isn't spotting." She pressed her palm against her lower belly as another grip of pain rose. "Get Elsie. Get the phone."

"You are upset because of the weather. Everyone is upset because of the weather."

Outside, wind drove snow against the door in dry rattling bursts. The lane had disappeared by afternoon; from the upstairs bedroom she had watched the top of Mrs Bhatti's sign vanish first, then the black iron rail, then half the front gate. The radio had said no buses since noon and no guarantee by morning.

"Peter, for God's sake."

He sighed the way he did when figures failed to balance. "You are not due for weeks. If I wake half the lane and this is nerves, you will have made a spectacle of yourself."

She laughed once, a sharp, ugly bark that hurt her ribs. "I don't care about spectacle. I care about not dropping this baby in the kitchen."

The third contraction bent her. She slid down the dresser to one knee, fingers slipping on the worn varnish. Peter set the ledger on the sideboard with careful hands, still protecting the binding, and crouched beside her without touching.

"Can you stand?"

"Get Elsie."

"I asked whether you can stand."

She grabbed his sleeve and pulled him close enough to smell shaving soap and the cold tobacco he pretended he did not smoke anymore. "If you make me ask again, I will crawl next door and wake her myself."

That, finally, moved him. He straightened, jaw tight. "Stay in here. Do not walk about." He took his coat from the peg, then paused to button every button to the throat before opening the door to the storm.

She listened to him fight the drift on the step, boots scraping, then the short, muffled thud of his fist against the Harrows' wall door.

By the time Elsie came in, Noreen had made it to the kitchen chair and bled through one folded tea towel. She held the towel between her legs and stared at the range, willing the fire not to die.

Elsie arrived half-laced, hair shoved under a wool scarf that had once been navy and now lived somewhere between slate and ash. She had thrown Tom's old depot coat over her nightdress and forgotten to wipe flour from one forearm. A stub of carpenter's pencil rode behind her ear, the one she used for marking ration sums on the inside of cupboard doors where men never looked.

"Show me," Elsie said.

Peter stepped between them. "There is no need for theatrics. She has had a fright."

Elsie did not raise her voice. "Move, Peter."

He held her stare for two beats too long, then shifted a fraction. She passed him anyway, knelt in front of Noreen, and lifted the soaked towel with efficient fingers.

Noreen watched Elsie's face as she checked the blood. Elsie's mouth tightened, then flattened into the polite calm she wore with landlords and matrons.

"When did it start?"

"Fifteen, twenty minutes. Maybe." Noreen swallowed. "Pain's coming in waves."

Elsie took the little clock from her coat pocket, cracked plastic face held together with two strips of plaster, and set it on the table. "We'll count proper. Next one, tell me the second it grabs."

"This is absurd," Peter said. "You are encouraging panic."

"No," Elsie said. "I'm measuring it so we know if she's in labor or not."

"She is not in labor."

"Maybe she isn't. Maybe she is. We don't get to pick by saying it harder."

Noreen felt the next contraction build like a wave under floorboards. "Now," she said.

Elsie clicked the clock. Peter paced two steps toward the hall, then back, then reached for the kettle and set it to boil with abrupt force.

"Fine," he said. "You may time your experiment. Then we can stop this nonsense."

Noreen's pain peaked and broke. She breathed through her mouth and tasted coal smoke. Elsie wrote a number with the pencil stub on the back of an old envelope: `7:14`.

"Seven minutes since the last by your guess," Elsie said. "We'll see the next. Peter, I need clean towels, not the ones by the sink. Top shelf airing cupboard. And bring the paraffin heater through."

"The paraffin is rationed."

"So is blood," Elsie said.

For the first time that night, Peter lost his footing in the argument. The stain kept widening on the chair cushion while he scrubbed his thumb against the ink on his forefinger until it squeaked. Then he went upstairs without answering.

The house settled around them with winter noises: the range ticking, wind under the door, muffled cough from next door through the shared wall. Noreen held Elsie's wrist when the next contraction hit and felt how steady Elsie's pulse stayed.

"I'm sorry," Noreen whispered when it eased. "I dragged you out of bed."

Elsie shook her head. "Don't be thick. You did right."

Noreen tried to joke and could not. "If this baby's daft enough to come now, tell it to wait till after payday."

A shadow of a smile passed over Elsie's face and was gone. "You can negotiate with it yourself. I'm just the clerk."

Peter came back with towels and the heater, set both down too hard, and announced to the room rather than to either woman, "Roads are closed from the roundabout. I saw no lights on the lane except Bhatti's back room."

"Phone the clinic," Elsie said.

"The line has been unreliable all day."

"Try anyway."

He did not move.

Noreen felt another cramp begin, slower this time but deeper, starting in bone. She looked at her husband and saw what had once passed for steadiness: his refusal to waste effort, his certainty that panic killed faster than illness. Tonight it looked like something else. It looked like a man defending his own idea of order while her body leaked through his categories.

"Peter," she said softly, because soft was what reached him when shouting failed. "Ring them."

He went to the hall at last.

Elsie marked `7:23` on the envelope. "Still regular enough to worry." She pressed a folded towel into Noreen's hands. "Squeeze this when it bites. Don't lock your legs."

In the hall, the rotary dial clicked and spun, clicked and spun. Peter's voice came sharp, formal, clipped into the mouthpiece.

"Calderbank exchange? I require maternity... yes, immediately... no, this is not social."

Noreen closed her eyes. Snow hissed against the window. The towel in her hands smelled of sunlight from summer lines long gone.

By the time Peter returned, the line had cut twice and her contractions were five to six minutes apart.

"No answer at clinic," he said. "Exchange says calls are queued because half the district has no power." He aimed the last question at Elsie like a charge sheet. "Are you satisfied?"

Elsie stood. "No. I'm going to ring Keane's number direct."

"You are not turning my hallway into a public office."

Noreen pushed herself upright on shaking legs. "It is not your hallway if I'm the one bleeding in it."

The words surprised all three of them, most of all her. Peter's mouth opened, then closed. Elsie took the chance, crossed to the phone, and began dialing before he could recover.

Outside, snow kept falling, determined to erase every track by morning.

***

The next hour stretched thin as wire.

The first direct call to Keane's flat rang into static. Elsie swore, dialed exchange instead, and came back with clipped instructions from a woman who would not give her name: get Noreen on her left side now, change towels on a clock not a guess, and log every contraction. Elsie moved her to the settee and wedged a folded blanket under her hip before Peter could argue.

The second call reached Keane's downstairs neighbor, breathless and annoyed. Keane had gone to an emergency near the mills and might not get back before midnight. Elsie tore a page from Peter's blotter, wrote Noreen's symptoms and their address in block letters, and shoved it into his hand. "If this towel fills in under half an hour, you walk that to the police box. No debate." Peter started to set the page aside. Elsie caught his wrist, put the pencil in his fingers, and said, "Write the trigger time in your own hand." He stared at her, then wrote `30 minutes` under her line, each letter pressed so hard the nib scored through the paper. After that he pulled on scarf and gloves and used the coal shovel to cut a narrow path from the door to the gate.

The third call connected for less than a minute. Elsie got out, "Dark blood, contractions five to six," before the line screamed and dropped. She set Peter's lamp and greatcoat by the threshold and moved his boots there with them. Peter said, "You are not ordering me into that storm on speculation." Noreen answered through clenched teeth, "If it comes to that, you go. I'm done asking."

Noreen lay on her left side on the settee with a blanket folded under her hips, as instructed by a nurse at the exchange who might not have been a nurse at all. Her knees were drawn up because that hurt least. The paraffin heater breathed hot metal and stale fuel into the room. Each contraction rolled through her in a shape she learned and hated: low warning ache, hard squeeze, knife line down the groin, then shaky relief.

By then the settee cushion had gone damp and cold under Noreen's hip, and each new wave left a finer tremor in her thighs. Elsie still kept the envelope, but she only marked it when the pattern shifted; the rest of her attention stayed on Noreen's breath and the color in her mouth. She had moved her own clock and Noreen's wedding photo to the mantel, clearing space like a woman setting out instruments. Nothing wasted, nothing theatrical, every motion exact.

More frightening than the pain was the way Peter had shifted from husband to clerk. He stood near the doorway with his coat still on, wet shoulders shining in lamplight, polishing his glasses again and again as if clean lenses could turn blood into paperwork. The gesture was almost tender in ordinary hours; now it made Noreen want to throw something heavy.

"You should conserve paraffin," he said after one contraction passed. "We may need it for days."

Elsie kept her pencil moving. "If she goes into full labor this early, fuel won't be your worst problem."

"You are not qualified to make that judgment."

"Neither are you."

Noreen laughed again, thinner this time. "If we die, at least you'll have the argument won."

Peter flinched. He moved to the settee and touched her shoulder at last, fingertips light, uncertain. "You are not dying. Do not say idiotic things."

She almost leaned into his hand from habit. Then another contraction hit and she gripped the blanket so hard her nails tore threads loose.

Elsie counted against the panic rising in the room. "One, two, three, breathe. In through your nose, out through your mouth. Stay with me."

"Don't boss me," Noreen said through her teeth.

"Then boss yourself." Elsie met her eyes. "But breathe."

Noreen breathed.

At half eight, the phone rang.

Peter got there first. "Walsh speaking." His back straightened. "Mrs Keane, yes. She has had bleeding and spasmodic pain for approximately--for about an hour." He cupped the mouthpiece with his palm as if the room were hostile, and delivered the next words in the tone of a man reading a complaint. "I am not saying it is nothing. I am saying two women here are amplifying each other."

Elsie was on him before Noreen could curse. She took the receiver from his hand with a force that made the cord slap the skirting board.

"Mrs Keane? It's Elsie Harrow, from next door. Dark blood, not bright. One cloth soaked through, now it's slower but still coming. Pains were seven apart, now six, one hit at five. Started around seven o'clock." She listened, nodding. "No waters. Skin is warm but not hot. Baby moved after the first rough hour, yes."

Peter reached for the phone. Elsie turned her shoulder and blocked him without ceremony.

Noreen heard only Elsie's side. The missing half scraped at her nerves; each pause opened space for the worst picture her mind could make.

"Yes, she's on her left. Blanket under the hip."

"Kettle's boiled. Towels are clean."

"No, lane is blocked right to the gate. We can't get a motor in."

"Right. I'm writing that now."

She rummaged for a pen, found none, snapped Peter's fountain pen from the mantel with a muttered sorry that was not sorry at all, and wrote on the back of a gas bill.

Noreen kept one hand on her belly and did the sums in her head: one towel soaked, then two, then Peter sent out on foot whether he approved or not.

"Fresh folded towel every half hour," Elsie said into the phone. "If one floods sooner, he goes to the police box on foot."

Peter straightened. "Do not volunteer me like a parcel."

Noreen cut in before Elsie could answer. "Get your boots by the door and shut up."

Elsie listened, nodding once. "Right. If the pains spread out and lose that knife edge, we keep her still and keep watching."

Peter opened his mouth again. Elsie turned away from him and spoke harder into the receiver. "No, we're not putting fingers in. We're not guessing."

"Put that in his ledger," Noreen said, then breathed through another grip of pain.

"Sweet tea in sips, keep her warm but not sweating," Elsie said, writing fast.

She pressed the pen harder as Keane continued. "If blood turns fresh red, or the baby goes quiet, we ring back at once. Yes. I have it."

Elsie handed the receiver to Noreen. "She wants your voice."

Noreen pressed cold Bakelite to her ear. Keane's voice came rough with line noise and exhaustion, brisk enough to cut through fear.

"Nora, listen to me. I can't get a car up your road tonight. Nobody can. That does not mean you are abandoned. It means we manage risk where you are. I need your agreement on three triggers: fresh red blood, no movement, or a cloth soaked in under thirty minutes. If any one of those happens, you send for help on foot at once. Can you do that?"

"I can," Noreen said, and surprised herself by meaning it.

"Good woman. Is the baby moving now?"

"A little flutter, then a kick after the last one."

"That's what I wanted to hear. I'm flagging your name at the top of my call sheet. If lines stay up, I'll ring you in an hour. If lines fail, keep logging and ring exchange every thirty minutes. If you need a runner, put your husband on and keep yourself still. Do I have your yes?"

There was a pause, static hissing. Then, softer: "And Nora, no heroics. Bleeding in pregnancy is never nothing. Anyone tells you different, send them to me."

Noreen answered into the receiver and let the words land on Peter. "Right. Thank you."

When she hung up, the room seemed briefly too quiet, as if all three had been holding breath for permission.

Peter reclaimed his pen from the table and wiped the nib on a handkerchief. "She did not say it is labor."

Elsie folded another towel into a thick pad. "She didn't say it's nothing either."

"Words matter."

"So does blood on your chair."

Noreen wanted to laugh and hit and sleep, all in one motion. She took the new towel, shifted her hips, and bit down on a groan when the next contraction arrived. It had changed: broader, less knife, more pressure. Still frightening. Still real.

Time stopped feeling like numbers and turned into damage control.

She got down two swallows of tea before nausea climbed her throat. She pissed in the chamber pot because standing sent sparks down her thighs.

Each wave stole her voice for a few seconds, so she squeezed Elsie's wrist when it started and tapped twice when it released. Elsie adjusted towels, shifted blankets, and remade the spare bed with old sheets in case they needed more room than the settee.

Peter fed the range and watched the fire as if heat alone could settle the argument. Every clang of the iron door went through Noreen's teeth.

At ten, she passed a clot the size of a plum pit and started crying in soundless bursts she could not stop. Elsie wiped her between contractions with warm water and did not pretend there was dignity in it. Peter stood at the foot of the settee, white-faced, reciting practicalities as if order on paper could stop blood in a body.

The tone hurt worse than the cramp. It told Noreen he was already drafting tomorrow's story, one where this night could be filed as nerves.

"If this stabilizes by midnight, we all need sleep. We can reassess at first light."

Noreen spoke into the blanket. "My body's not one of your tidy plans, Peter."

"I am trying to keep this house in one piece."

"Keep it in one piece somewhere else."

He went to the kitchen and came back with a dry dishcloth bunched in both hands, then seemed to realize what he was doing and dropped it on the side table, ashamed and angry in equal parts.

Around eleven, the contractions began to drift apart. Six minutes became eight, then ten. The pain dulled to a heavy drag across her lower back. Bleeding slowed from soak to smear. She felt three distinct kicks in twenty minutes, each one a blunt thud under skin that made her want to sob with relief.

Elsie did not trust it yet. She kept timing. She kept writing. She changed towels, wrung stained cloths into the bucket, and fed the clock with her eyes like it might betray them if she looked away.

Noreen watched her and thought of last month, of Elsie standing in borrowed slippers at the back door pretending she had come for sugar when she had really come because the silence in her own house was too loud. People said women like Elsie were careful, decent, not made for crisis. They were wrong in the way people were wrong when they mistook quiet for passivity.

Near midnight the phone rang once, then died. No second ring. Dead line.

Peter lifted the receiver, listened to nothing, and replaced it with surgical precision.

"Out," he said.

"Of course it's out," Noreen muttered.

Elsie rose and checked the front latch. "Then we change it. Door on single catch, lamp at the step, note in your coat. If I say run, you run. Nobody waits for a dial tone."

"The bolt stays across at night," Peter said.

Noreen turned her head toward him. "Not tonight. Slide it back."

He held her eyes, then dragged the bolt open with a scrape that sounded like something tearing.

Elsie tucked a hot-water bottle against Noreen's spine and sat on the floor with her back against the settee, knees up, clock in her lap. She looked suddenly older than thirty-one, not in face but in posture: the posture of someone who had learned how much work terror required and had chosen to do it anyway.

"I'm staying till morning," she said.

Peter opened his mouth.

Noreen cut him off. "She's staying."

He held still for a long moment, jaw working as he measured the cost. Then he nodded once. "Very well. But this does not become habit."

Noreen turned her face to the cushion so he would not see the expression she could not smooth in time.

Habit, she thought. As if dependence had not already become habit in this lane, in this winter, in both their marriages.

At twenty past one, a contraction hit that did not crest and ease. It clamped and stayed. Warmth spilled sudden and bright between Noreen's thighs.

Elsie stripped back the blanket, saw the fresh red, and said Peter's name in a voice that made him run.

"The note. Now."

Peter turned toward the window where snow struck sideways. "I cannot leave you two in this condition. Not with her like this."

"You can and you will," Noreen said. "Police box. Bring whoever has boots."

He hesitated one beat too long. Elsie shoved the folded symptom note into his coat and pushed the door open. Cold punched the room. Peter went, lantern swinging.

The house went very quiet after he left, except for Noreen's breathing. Another contraction gathered in her spine before the first fear had drained. It came as a deep pull under her ribs, then dropped hard into her pelvis and locked there. She dug both hands under the settee cushion, knuckles grinding wood, and heard herself making a low animal sound she had never made before.

"Stay with me," Elsie said. "Don't bear down. Breathe to the count."

The pain sharpened until her vision narrowed to the brass tack heads along the settee arm. She tasted pennies. Sweat ran cold from her neck to her shoulder blades. At the top of it she felt pressure so fierce she thought her bones might split, then the grip began to peel away in ragged increments.

When it finally released, she shook so hard her teeth clicked.

Elsie lifted the towel and checked in the lamplight. "Fresh red streak across the middle. Not flooding right now. Darker at the edges. No new clot." She folded a clean towel into place and marked the envelope with a blunt, angry jab of pencil: `1:27 fresh red`.

Noreen lay rigid, waiting for movement. For five minutes the baby stayed still, and by seven her mouth filled with the taste of metal. Elsie kept one palm on her belly and counted aloud anyway, steady as a metronome.

At nine minutes a heel dragged under Noreen's ribs, slow and stubborn. She cried hard enough to choke.

Peter came back thirty minutes later with snow clotted on his eyebrows and two strips of skin peeled raw from his knuckles where the shovel had slipped. A constable at the box had phoned the station: no ambulance could clear Barrow Lane before dawn, but the address was logged for the first team that got free.

Elsie did not let the sentence settle. "We change protocol now. No waiting for calls. Peter, you go again at three with the updated times and bleed note. If one towel floods inside twenty minutes, you don't argue, you run."

"I have already gone once through that lane."

"And you'll go again," Noreen said. "Or you can explain to Keane why you chose your sleep over my blood."

His jaw flexed. "Very well. Three o'clock."

At two ten, another contraction came sharp and short. It started as a dull drag across Noreen's back, tightened to a vise that made her curl around her own belly, then broke with a wet gasp and a tremor in her thighs. Elsie checked again: rust-brown smear over bright threads, less than before, cloth still mostly dry at the corners. She wrote `2:14 less volume` and held the envelope up for Peter to see.

At three, Peter put the coat back on without being asked and went out with the note, lantern, and his wounded knuckles wrapped in dishcloth strips.

He returned before four with no ambulance and no comfort, only word that the station had repeated her address twice and would send whoever got a vehicle free first. Noreen did not sleep after that. She listened to wind hammer the chimney, to Elsie murmuring numbers over each tightening, to Peter at the table under the weak lamp writing a list of who might talk if they had seen him leave at night while her bloody towels soaked in a bucket beside his shoe.

She stopped expecting him to look up.

***

Morning arrived as a diluted grey through frosted glass.

The storm had not broken, but the wind had dropped to a lower, steadier moan. Snow stood shoulder-high against the front wall. The lane was a white trench; any tracks from the night had already blown flat, and only the top half of Mrs Bhatti's sign peered over a drift like a half-sunk boat.

Noreen woke with a dry mouth and a back that felt beaten. For three full minutes, nothing tightened. Then a mild cramp came and went, more warning than attack.

Elsie was already up, rinsing cloths in the scullery sink with water gone pink to rust in the enamel basin. Her sleeves were rolled, exposing the pale grooves where soap had cracked the skin along her wrists. Peter stood at the range buttering bread with careful economy, slicing each piece exactly in two as though symmetry could count for nourishment.

"How far apart now?" Noreen asked.

Elsie checked the envelope. Ink and pencil marks crossed each other in cramped lines. "Last proper one was twenty-two minutes. Before that, eighteen. Bleeding's slowed right down."

"Baby moved?" Peter asked.

She put both hands on her belly and waited. A moment later, a solid heel pressed under her ribs and rolled away. She exhaled through her nose. "Yes."

He nodded like she had passed an exam.

Relief came first and hard enough to make her dizzy. It was not labor. Not yet. She had not lost the baby in the night. The house still stood. She could breathe without counting.

Then anger flooded in behind it, just as strong.

Peter set a plate on her lap. "There, you see. False alarm. Your body responded to stress, and both of you--both of you escalated it."

Elsie wrung out a towel and hung it over the chair back. "Keane called it threatened labor. Not alarm."

"Keane has to assume the worst," Peter said. "It is her profession."

Noreen took one bite of bread and could not swallow it. "Worst case was me bleeding through two towels while you argued about paraffin."

"And yet you are here, stable, exactly as I predicted."

"You did not predict anything. You delayed."

His face sharpened. "I prevented chaos."

"You prevented being embarrassed," she said. "Different thing."

He put down the knife with deliberate care. "I will not be spoken to in this tone in my own house."

Noreen barked a laugh that sent a twinge through her back. "Your own house? I cleaned your own house yesterday on ankles like boiled sausages. I bled in your own chair all night. Spare me ownership for five minutes."

A flush rose under his skin. For a second she thought he might shout. Instead he went colder, grammar stiffening the way it did when he wanted to punish without volume.

"You are exhausted and irrational. I shall overlook this conversation. What I will not--cannot--overlook is Mrs Harrow inserting herself into private family matters and inflaming your nerves."

Elsie stopped wringing the cloth halfway through a twist. Pink water ran off her knuckles into the basin. "If you'd like to call keeping her and the baby alive inflaming, go ahead."

"Do not tell me what kept my wife alive."

"No," Elsie said. "You keep telling us that yourself."

Silence dropped, dense as wet wool.

From the settee, Noreen could hear the wall clock in the front room and, faint beyond it, someone in the Harrow house coughing a smoker's cough that had to be Tom, back at last. The lane would wake soon. Doors would scrape open. News would travel by kettle and coal scuttle whether she approved or not.

Peter straightened his tie, though he was not going anywhere. "This discussion is over. Mrs Harrow, thank you for your assistance. You should return home before rumors begin." He took the front-door key from the hook and slid it into his trouser pocket.

Elsie turned her body toward Noreen and kept her shoulder to him. "You want me to stay till noon?"

Peter answered first. "That is unnecessary."

Noreen held Elsie's gaze. The question was not really about noon. It was about who she called next time, who she trusted to hear pain without calling it hysteria.

"Go get some sleep," Noreen said. "Then come back with your chalk and we'll do sums for food and fuel."

Peter frowned. "Sums can wait."

"No," she said. "They can't."

Elsie gave a short nod. "I'll be back in an hour." She stripped off her apron, pulled Tom's oversized coat tight over her belly, and paused by the door. "Lie on your side. Keep drinking. If bleeding picks up, I don't care what anyone says, we send for Keane again."

"I know," Noreen said.

After Elsie left, cold air and snow crystals still spinning in her wake, the house seemed to shrink around Peter's displeasure.

He gathered the stained towels into a bucket and carried them to the scullery with pinched fingers, handling them the way a clerk handles evidence. "You have made this household look foolish," he said without turning. "Bhatti will have seen lights all night. Half the lane will hear of it by tea."

Noreen watched him from the settee. His shoulders were neat inside his sweater. His hair stayed combed even after no sleep. He was the same man who remembered due dates for every bill, who had once rubbed her swollen feet while reciting a ridiculous poem from school to make her laugh. That man still existed somewhere inside this one. She could see him and no longer rely on him.

"Let them hear," she said.

He turned, startled.

"If gossip keeps me alive," she went on, "I'll buy it a biscuit." She shifted, wincing, and settled her palm over the place where the baby had kicked. "You can be angry about the scene if you want. I'm angry I had to beg you to take me seriously while I bled."

"I took you seriously."

"No. You took your pride seriously."

He opened his mouth, closed it, and turned away to scrub blood from the chair cushion with a ferocity he would later call diligence.

Noreen let him scrub. She let him mutter about soap flakes and ruined upholstery and paraffin usage and the price of replacements, hoping numbers might reassert moral order.

Under all of it, a quieter set of rules began in her own head.

She asked for the gas bill Elsie had written on, flattened it over her knee, and printed in thick pencil:

If pain returned, call Elsie first.

If road cleared, see Keane whether Peter approved or not.

If bleeding brightened, send for help before any argument could start.

She tore the paper in two, tucked one half under the clock on the mantel, and folded the other into her bra.

By noon the contractions had faded to occasional cramps, and the bleeding was only a rust mark on folded cloth. Keane could not get through by phone, but a note came by boy from the exchange saying she would visit as soon as weather allowed and that Noreen was to stay in bed, no lifting, no stairs unless absolutely necessary.

Peter read the note twice, folded it into a rectangle, and tucked it into his pocketbook beside wage slips.

"There," he said. "Official instruction. Rest for a day and this will pass."

Noreen held out her hand. "Give me that."

He stared at her, then gave her the note.

She smoothed it flat and pinned it beside the phone with the bread knife, right over the wallpaper seam where anyone could see it. Under it she pinned her own penciled list.

Elsie came back before dinner with chalk in her coat pocket and a jar of broth wrapped in newspaper. Noreen opened the dresser drawer, took out the spare front-door key, and pressed it into Elsie's palm.

"If this starts again and he says wait, you don't wait," Noreen said. "You come in and ring Keane."

Peter stiffened. "You are not distributing keys from this house."

"I just did," Noreen said.

When Elsie left again, Peter sat at the table with his jaw tight and his hands flat on the wood, not writing anything.

Noreen listened to the quiet and felt no comfort in that either.

The baby shifted once, slow as a fish turning under dark water. Noreen placed her hand there and counted, not contractions this time, but distances: from chair to phone, from phone to door, from door to Elsie's wall, and from that wall to first light, when Elsie would already be at her table doing sums before the lane fully woke.


# Chapter 6

Upstairs, the inside of the bedroom window had gone white as milk glass. Elsie took her thumb to it and made a coin-sized clear patch. Outside she saw no color at all, only coal smoke held low in the air and the terrace roofs wearing a hard skin of dirty snow. It had not thawed in days. Nothing gave. Nothing drained. The lane had become a narrow trench of boot prints, frozen and refrozen until each step caught the ankle if you took it wrong. A bus went past at the top of the lane, slow and grinding, chains clinking on the rear wheels like loose cutlery.

She came down early because the cold had teeth again; it bit through blanket and cardigan alike, into the sore points in her hips where she had slept on one side too long. The flour tin met the kitchen table with a dull iron thud, then sugar, tea, oats, and dripping in quick succession. She paused with one hand under the weight of her belly and listened before she reached for the next tin: the soft tick of pipes trying not to freeze, the faint rattle of the bedroom sash where the putty had cracked, Tom upstairs breathing through his mouth with that smoker's burr in it.

In the kitchen she lit the range with yesterday's newspaper and three careful lumps of coal, not four. She watched how long the kindling took and wrote the time down on the back page of an old school exercise book with Jack and Jill still printed at the top in faded blue. She had started the book in the week before Christmas. She had written meals, weather, what they spent, what they owed Mrs Bhatti, what came into the house that could be counted in ounces or pints. She had stopped writing Tom's promises because promises did not stay still long enough to measure.

She set everything out on the table in a straight, merciless row, the way the dead were laid out before somebody confirmed the name. She lined up the flour tin, sugar tin, tea caddy, oat jar, and dripping pot. She added two onions soft at the neck, nine potatoes still hard and six that had started to eye and sink, and a heel of bread gone stale enough to knock on wood. She put down one strip of bacon fat wrapped in greaseproof, three eggs, all small, with one cracked shell she would need to use this morning, and four tins: corned beef, marrowfat peas, tomato soup, and condensed milk. She finished with half a packet of barley, a paper twist of pepper, and mustard clinging in the bottom corners of a jar.

She kept going. She opened the scullery door and counted coal sacks by touch because the glass in there had iced over too thick to see cleanly. One full sack was tied at the neck, one half sack slumped and leaked dust, and a bucket of slack sat by the wall. In the cupboard under the sink she found one and a quarter bottles of paraffin, if she counted the heel in the lamp and dared tip it back, soap shaved down to a crescent, and sanitary towels folded from old flour sacks and boiled until the threads looked tired.

When she came back in, Tom had his elbows on the table and her tea caddy open under his nose, pinching leaves and sniffing each pinch before he dropped it back. He wore his vest and braces and had not shaved. He had one sock on and one heel on the bare stone, rubbing at the cold with his palm.

"What are you doing with all that?" he asked. "Looks like a raid."

"I'm making a two-week plan," Elsie said. "If this weather holds, we can't buy like we used to."

He took a pinch of tea leaves between finger and thumb, rolled them, and dropped them back. "We never bought like rich folk, El. You've always managed."

"That was before the roads kept shutting and before the shop started rationing by queue." She held out her hand for the caddy. "Leave that. I need to know exact amounts."

Tom gave it back with a little smile that was meant to be affectionate and landed as dismissal. "Exact amounts. Listen to you. You sound like old Walsh with his office stamps."

Elsie opened to a fresh page. She wrote at the top: _Fourteen days_. Beneath it she wrote _coal_, _paraffin_, _food_, then drew lines with the edge of a ruler so cracked that it bowed. She did the sums aloud because if she kept them inside her head he would treat them as mood.

"Three lumps to light, three at noon, four to bank," she said. "That's ten a day if we keep one room heated and close off the front. We've maybe one hundred and thirty lumps worth if the half sack is better than it looks. That gives us thirteen days if we don't waste any."

"You count lumps now?"

"Yes."

"Christ." He leaned back, stretched his shoulders, and winced at the pull. "All right then, count lumps. I'll bring more in."

"With what money?"

He did not answer at once. He reached for the enamel mug, drank cold tea, and made a face. "Wages."

"How much this week?"

"You know what it comes to."

"I don't."

He put the mug down too hard. "Don't start in on me before breakfast."

The word sat between them like a nail dropped on tile. The partition cupboard gave a small shudder as a chair dragged next door, then came the low cough of someone trying not to wake a house. Peter's voice followed, crisp enough to tighten her shoulders.

"You are to stay in bed," Peter said. "You frightened everybody enough on Saturday."

Noreen answered, her voice thinned but still carrying that quick lift at the end of words that could make a complaint sound like a joke if she wanted it to. "I'm not made of spun sugar, Peter."

Elsie looked at Tom. He gave a small shrug toward the wall, the usual verdict that women turned to fuss once babies came near. Then he stood, took his second sock from the range rail, and pulled it on.

"I've got an early turn," he said. "And they're cutting hours if men come in late. Keep the fire low till I get back."

"Tom. How much in wages?"

He found his shirt from the peg and shoved his arms through without looking at her. "Enough."

"Enough for what?"

"For this house." He buttoned wrong, cursed, unbuttoned, started again. "For us. Stop pecking at me like a crow."

"I'm carrying this house with figures because the figures are all that hold still," she said.

He bent for his boots. "You're carrying a baby. Leave the numbers to me."

That was the line. Leave the numbers to me. He said it while she was the one scraping paraffin heels from lamp glass and slicing mold off potatoes and deciding whether she could afford to boil nappies or only rinse them. He said it with his hand already out for the last heel of toast.

She let him eat. She let him take the thick end and she did not say anything because saying anything at that minute would have spent fuel she needed later. When he left, he kissed the top of her head like a man exiting a train carriage. The door opened. Cold rolled in. The door shut. His steps crunched down the flagged path.

Elsie counted to fifty. Then she took down the tobacco tin where she kept housekeeping cash. Two shillings and fourpence in coins. A tram token. A bent hairpin. She wrote it all down. She opened Tom's coat pocket where it hung by the door and found nothing but a matchbook from the Railway Arms and a folded betting card for dogs run three weeks ago, circled in blue pencil. The dates had gone soggy from melted snow and dried warped.

By ten the range had settled into a red eye and the room smelled of damp wool and boiled barley water. Elsie put the cracked egg into a pan with chopped onion and let it go soft, then ate standing with her hand braced on the table when a deep drag pulled across her pelvis and into her groin, as if someone had tied weight to her bones. She stayed still until it unclenched. She listened to Noreen's house.

There was no singing that morning. Noreen usually sang under her breath when she cleaned, bits of hymns folded into dance tunes and pop songs from the radio, whatever came first. Elsie heard only short movements: cup to saucer, cupboard door, then quiet. Quiet in that terrace was a warning louder than shouting.

She wrapped a scarf over her hair and carried over the washing basket with two damp aprons laid on top. Under the aprons she put the corned beef tin, the condensed milk, half the barley, and the better of the onions. She added six coal lumps in a flour sack and tied it tight. She did not take paraffin yet because the bottle clinked and smell traveled.

Noreen answered in Peter's old cardigan belted over her nightdress, the hem dark where it had brushed wet stone. She had her hair pinned up with two darning needles crossed like chopsticks, and she held a baby's cardigan sleeve in her hand, mustard wool unraveling from the cuff.

"Don't laugh," Noreen said before Elsie had spoken. She lifted the sleeve. "I've knitted this same inch six bloody times. The wool scratches my fingers and then I imagine it scratching his neck, and out it comes again."

"I didn't laugh," Elsie said. "You're up too long."

"I'm up because if I lie down while he's at work, I hear every drop in the pipes and think one of them is blood." Noreen stepped back to let her in. "Come in before you freeze solid."

The Walsh kitchen felt warmer by maybe two degrees and smelled of cloves and damp plaster. Noreen kept orange peel drying on a saucer behind the range, even now, because she said the house needed reminding that seasons changed. Today the peel had gone hard as shell.

On the table sat a cup of tea gone cold and a saucer full of pills cut in half with a bread knife. Elsie did not recognize the tablets. Noreen saw her looking.

"Aspirin from Mrs Donnelly," she said. "Peter says they're harmless and then tells me not to take anything without asking him. He likes both sides when both sides leave him right."

Elsie set down the basket. "How is the pain?"

Noreen pulled a face and then smoothed it. "Like somebody tied wire around my lower back and forgot to untwist it. Better than Saturday. Worse than I say out loud."

"Any more bleeding?"

"Only spotting."

Elsie nodded. She did not ask if Peter knew. She did not want the answer because she had already decided what she was doing and answers could make a woman hesitate.

She peeled back the aprons. Noreen stared at the tins.

"No," she said immediately. "Absolutely no."

"Yes," Elsie said. "These are staying."

"El, I can't take your food."

"You can and you will."

"Tom will notice."

"Tom notices when it suits him."

Noreen gave her a long look, one eyebrow lifted. "That sounds like a woman who hasn't slept."

"That sounds like arithmetic." Elsie took the tins and moved to the pantry cupboard under the stairs. It smelled of onions and wet cardboard. She put the corned beef behind a sack of flour and set the condensed milk inside an old biscuit tin with cracked family photos on top. She poured barley into a jar marked _Sago_ in blue paint and turned the label inward.

Noreen leaned against the doorframe, rubbing the bridge of her nose with finger and thumb. "If Peter finds it, he'll ask questions."

"Then tell him Mrs Bhatti overcounted and sent you home with charity. He likes the idea of other people making clerical mistakes."

Noreen laughed once, sharp. "That's cruel."

"It's useful."

"Tom will still ask."

Elsie straightened slowly, hand to the small of her back. "Let him. I'm not leaving you to boil wallpaper paste and call it broth because the men need to feel in charge."

Noreen's face softened and then hardened again in the same breath. "I hate this," she said quietly. "I hate needing."

"I know." Elsie reached for her wrist and felt how cold it was. "I hate it too."

They stood like that for a second, not embracing, just palms on wrists like they were checking each other for pulse. Through the front room came the dull click of the Walsh wireless turning on by itself as the power dipped and returned, that old set with the loose knob Peter refused to fix because he called replacement parts indulgence. A dance orchestra swelled, then fell into static.

"Wait," Noreen said. She went to the drawer by the sink, took out a folded paper parcel, and pressed it into Elsie's hand. "Tea. Real tea. Not the sweepings. My aunt sent it at Christmas and Peter thinks I'm saving it for after the baby."

Elsie closed her fingers around it and pushed it back. "Keep it."

"Take half."

"No."

"Take half or I'll put your tins back in your basket and carry them over myself, and then the lane's going to have a show." Noreen's mouth twitched. "Don't test me when I'm in a state and Irish."

Elsie gave in. They split the parcel over the sink with a spoon, each watching the other count. It was absurd and intimate and necessary.

When Elsie left, she carried no visible weight. The basket held only aprons now, and on top Noreen had thrown in the unraveling cardigan sleeve, a prop for anyone watching from the lane. On the path between houses, Elsie paused by the coal shed. She looked over the low wall toward the lane. Nobody was there. She lifted six more lumps into her apron, tied the corners, and crossed back to Noreen's scullery door. She did this twice. On the second trip a cramp knotted across the base of her spine and sent heat down both thighs. She had to brace on the shed door until the lane stopped tilting, coal dust bleeding black through the apron cloth onto her coat.

By noon she had rewritten the household list three times. One list gave everyone in the house equal portions. A second gave Tom the largest share, because he took it. A third stretched soup with barley water and left her the crusts. Only the third list lasted fourteen days.

She copied that one neatly, then tore it in half and burned the top section with Tom's name against the grate until it curled in on itself and vanished.

She spent the afternoon scrubbing nappies in water that filmed with grease before it reached a boil. She hung them indoors on string by the range so they dripped on her shoes while she peeled potatoes and skimmed mold from jam and patched Tom's shirt cuff with thread darker than the cloth. Every task asked for her hands and gave back a small proof that she had postponed failure by another hour.

At four she put on coat and boots and walked to Mrs Bhatti's shop with the empty paraffin bottle wrapped in a towel. The queue reached the door and bent along the shelves past tins of peaches nobody could afford. Mrs Bhatti stood on a stool by the counter ledger, glasses low on her nose, hair pinned under a scarf printed with tiny red chilies. She was adding up columns with her lips moving and her left foot tapping the rung in time.

"Mrs Harrow," she called without looking up. "You've become very thin in your face. That means your husband is either useless or in love. Which one?"

"The first one today," Elsie said.

Mrs Bhatti barked a laugh, then glanced up and softened. "I can do one pint paraffin, no more. Delivery hasn't come. Coal lorry turned back at the bridge again."

"One pint is fine."

"Your account isn't fine." Mrs Bhatti turned the ledger so Elsie could see the line under her name. "You're one week and three days behind. I'm not pushing you. I'm telling you what ink says."

Elsie slid two shillings across and watched the woman's thumb weigh each coin before she wrote the credit down. Shame was this: the scrape of a coin over wood, the pause before somebody chose whether to trust your face.

On the way home, Elsie stopped outside Noreen's back gate and tucked the paraffin bottle into the milk crate under a tea towel. She had just raised her hand to knock when Mrs Donnelly came out with an ash pan and squinted across the yards.

"Is that your bottle or hers?" Mrs Donnelly called. "Peter was asking this morning who keeps feeding half the row."

Elsie's heart kicked once, hard. If she turned and walked away, Noreen would spend the night with candle stubs. If she stayed silent, the lane would write its own answer and hand it to Tom by supper.

"Mine," Elsie said, loud enough for both houses. "She left it at ours after Saturday. I'm returning what isn't mine."

She knocked once and waited. Noreen opened the door a hand's width.

"Your bottle," Elsie said for the lane, then under her breath: "Hide it under the sink before he gets in."

Noreen took it without blinking and shut the door.

By the time dark fell, the house had the close, stale warmth of people trying to make one fire do the work of two, and Elsie could still feel Mrs Donnelly's eyes between the back doors.

*

Tom came in just after seven with snow on his cap brim and ale on his breath. He stamped his boots, swore at the cold, kissed Elsie on the cheek with distracted lips, and held his hands to the range in the old entitled way, claiming heat first. He had brought no coal. He had brought a packet of Woodbines and half a loaf of white bread already cut.

"Good news," he said. "They're talking overtime Saturday if buses keep running."

"Talking is free," Elsie said. She set stew on the table: potato, onion, a little bacon fat melted through, thickened with barley so it looked richer than it was.

"You're in one tonight," he said, trying for humor. "Did somebody steal your halo?"

"Eat before it cools."
