He ate fast, spoon scraping enamel in hard circles. Halfway through his second helping he paused and glanced at the pantry. It was a quick movement, but Elsie saw it. After supper he stayed on his feet, opened the cupboard above the sink, then the pantry.

"Where'd the corned beef and milk tin go?" he asked.

Elsie kept washing bowls. "Food goes where food goes."

He shut the pantry door hard enough to rattle the hinge. "Don't get smart with me. You've been carrying it next door, haven't you?"

The bowl in Elsie's hand slipped and clanged in the sink. She set both palms on the draining board until the metal stopped buzzing.

"Mind your mouth," she said.

Tom stood. "That's an answer."

"No, that's a warning."

"I knew it." He laughed once, no humor in it. "I come home and I'm the idiot with empty shelves while Mrs Walsh eats off my wages."

"Your wages don't fill these shelves," Elsie said. "My frozen fingers do. My hours in Bhatti's queue do. My hands do."

"Your hands?" He crossed the room and caught her wrist before she could pull back. His thumb drove into the soft place above her pulse until her fingers opened. "You think I'm out for a stroll all day?"

Pain shot up her forearm and bloomed at the pulse point. Her fingers went numb, then prickled back in hard pins that made her grip useless for a beat. The baby locked hard under her ribs, and the first breath she pulled in snagged high in her chest. She jerked free and backed into the sink hard enough to rattle the bowls. The bulb over the sink dipped and surged; next door the wireless died mid-song. Peter started in, sharp as chalk.

"You will not be up those stairs again tonight," Peter said. "Doctor's instruction."

Noreen answered low and fast, too blurred to catch.

If she shouted, he'd shout louder. If he stormed next door tonight, he'd turn Walsh's pantry out and make Noreen pay for every missing spoonful. She forced one full breath, then another, and chose the only ground he still respected: figures on paper.

"Then show me what actually comes in," Elsie said. She pressed her bruised wrist against her apron until the tremor eased. "Write it with dates."

Tom looked toward the dark window where their reflections floated over black glass. He yanked his bus ticket book from his coat pocket, flattened it on the table, and found a pencil from the mantel.

"Fine." He wrote the date and listed: _Tea. Sugar. Flour. Coal. Paraffin. Tins._ He walked to the scullery, came back with the coal shovel, and dropped fuel into the scuttle while he muttered under his breath. He tipped the paraffin lamp, and the sharp stink slicked across the room as a little ran over his knuckles. He opened the sugar tin, dragged a spoon flat across the top, and scored a pencil mark inside the rim.

"You marking tins like depot parcels now?" Elsie said.

"I'm marking what keeps slipping out of this house," Tom said. "One tin, half a jar, a scoop here and there. It keeps happening."

"Food gets eaten in winter. That's not vanishing."

"Don't dress it up." He snapped the book shut, then opened it again when she held his gaze. "Madden wants six pounds four shillings by Friday dinner. If I miss it, Doyle shouts my slate at the depot gate where every bastard can hear. Baby Goods still wants twelve pounds eight by month-end. There. That's dates. Give me till Saturday overtime and I can breathe."

The kitchen seemed to tip for one second. Six pounds four by Friday. Twelve pounds eight by month-end. Outside, a bin lid banged somewhere down the lane and the sound skated along the ice. The debts sat in the room like extra bodies.

Tom opened the tobacco tin, swept out the housekeeping coins, and dropped them into his trouser pocket with a hard clink. Then he took the pantry key off the nail, shut the pantry door, and turned the lock until it clicked. The click carried through the wall. He lifted four coal lumps out of the scuttle and dropped them back into the sack in the scullery.

"Three lumps till morning," he said. "No extras."

"Give me the key."

"No."

"I'm the one stretching stew with barley water and scraping mold off jam. Give me the key."

"You'll cook what I hand you." He tucked the key into his shirt pocket and tapped it once. "If one more tin moves, I'll go next door myself, turn Walsh's pantry out on the floor, and carry it back in front of their faces. Tomorrow I'll tell Mrs Bhatti not to put another ounce on our name for anybody."

It landed where he meant it to: on Noreen, not just on Elsie.

"Don't you dare go to her door," Elsie said.

"Then stop stocking her shelves with our food."

A stitch of pain cinched across her lower belly into her back. She caught the table edge and rode it out without bending. Tom watched her, jaw tight, then looked away first and drew fresh columns in the ticket book, pressing so hard the pencil tip snapped. He sharpened it with his pocketknife and kept writing.

When they came to bed, he turned his back to her and lay stiff as green timber. Elsie lay on her side facing Noreen's side of the terrace, her wrist throbbing where his thumb had been. Next door, a kettle hissed, then a cupboard door, then silence stretched thin.

Tom said into the dark, "We start proper now."

Elsie waited until his breathing thickened. Then she slid out from under the blanket, pulled on her coat over her nightdress, and went to the scullery. She tapped Noreen's back window twice, pause, once. Noreen cracked it open in cardigan and bare feet, eyes wide.

"Move the tins tonight," Elsie whispered. "He marked sugar, paraffin, all of it. At first light he'll count, and if one thing's off he'll be at Bhatti before breakfast."

Noreen nodded once. "I'll shift them now."

Elsie crossed back before Tom woke, fingers numb and belly dragging low, and lay down with cold still in her bones.

Frost feathered the bedroom glass again from the inside. From Noreen's pantry came the soft scrape of one last tin being shoved deep across the floor, and Elsie listened until it stopped.


# Chapter 7

The knock came before full light, three hard raps that did not wait for courtesy. Noreen had just shoved the last borrowed tin behind Peter's office files and gone to scrape frost from the inside of the pantry window with the back of a teaspoon. Elsie's two taps, pause, one at the scullery window still thudded in her wrists. She had the spoon in one hand and a damp tea towel in the other. The towel had frozen stiff where it hung over her shoulder. Peter was at the table behind her, penciling figures on the back of an old gas bill and adding up totals that looked cleaner than their life.

He did not look up. "Answer it."

He was still in shirtsleeves and waistcoat, spectacles low on his nose, the sort of neat that made her feel she'd smeared fingerprints on borrowed silver. He had shaved already. He always shaved before noon on weekdays even when the buses were off and half the town was walking on ice.

Noreen set the spoon down and flexed her fingers, the knuckles burning now that she had stopped moving. On the front step stood a young council clerk she had seen once at the clinic queue, and behind him a woman in a maroon coat with a clipboard tucked into the crook of her arm. The woman's lipstick had cracked into pale lines, dragged at the edges like paint laid down in a jolting lorry.

"Mrs Walsh?" the woman said. "Edith Jeffries, emergency office. We've an accommodation order for this address."

The clerk held out the paper like it was hot. A red stamp from Calderbank Borough Council covered the top half under the heading Emergency Billeting and Utility Support. The ink had run where melted snow had touched it and dried again.

"No," Peter said from the hallway behind Noreen, close enough that she felt his breath move her hair. "There is a mistake. My wife is expecting."

Miss Jeffries did not blink. "I am aware. We are placing electricity board personnel nearest failure corridors. Mr Arthur Hale, linesman, is assigned to this row. You have a spare room and a functioning grate."

"Functioning is generous," Peter said.

"You may file a complaint when roads open and offices resume full hours." She tapped the form with her pencil. "Today, this is not optional."

A man had been waiting by the gate, cap in his hands. He stepped in when Jeffries moved aside. He was lean enough that his coat seemed to hang from his shoulders rather than fit them, with a yellow-striped scarf wound twice around his throat, knitted too short to tuck in properly. One end kept slipping loose. Every few breaths he rubbed that loose end smooth with his thumb. His knuckles were raw and split white at the joints.

"Arthur Hale," he said. "Sorry to land on you."

Noreen recognized him then: the same board man from the Harrows' Christmas table, the one Tom had called Arthur Pike all evening through ale-thick politeness, though the council form in her hand said Hale in blunt block capitals.

His voice was plain Yorkshire with the edges worn off from years of talking over wind. He held a canvas tool bag and a small battered suitcase with one buckle mended in green thread. The thread was too bright for the case and too careful to be accidental.

Peter did not move to let him through. "How long?"

"Until restoration phase eases," Miss Jeffries said. "Could be days. Could be longer. You know what this weather's done."

She turned to Noreen, not Peter. "If you need paraffin priority because of pregnancy, report through your district midwife. She's listed on the back." She lowered her voice by half an inch. "Keep your hallway clear. They call out men at all hours."

Noreen took the paper because someone had to. Her fingers left damp marks at the corners. She knew the exact moment she stopped hoping this was negotiable. It was when Miss Jeffries signed the duplicate copy with a blunt pencil and tore it out for them with the calm of a woman who had done this door after door and heard every version of no.

Peter shifted his weight and gave Arthur a ruler-straight smile. "Mr Hale, of course. Public need takes priority. Come in. Mind the runner."

Arthur wiped his boots on the mat until the mat gave up taking dirt. "I'd rather leave the boots by the door if that's all right."

"You'll catch your death," Noreen said before Peter could answer.

Arthur's mouth twitched. "I've kept this lot attached through worse than wet socks."

He stepped inside in thick grey socks, steam lifting off his trousers as heat reached them. The hallway smelled suddenly of outside things: diesel, sleet, iron. He kept his bag close to his leg, hand hooked through the strap.

Miss Jeffries gave Noreen a final nod. "Spare room must be available by six. If he is not housed, we assign enforcement." She looked over Noreen's shoulder at Peter and did not soften. "Do not test that."

When they were gone, the house sounded bigger for a few seconds. Then Peter shut the door very carefully and said, "Get the room sorted."

Noreen did not move.

"We use that room," she said. "The linens are there. The baby bundle is there."

"Then move them."

Arthur stood in the hall holding his cap with both hands, eyes on the umbrella stand.

"I can wait outside while you clear it," he said. "Or in the yard. I'm no trouble."

"You'll do neither," Peter said. "Mrs Walsh has this in hand."

He turned to Noreen, that same smooth smile still on his face because there was now an audience. "You have this in hand."

The words sat on her skin like cold coin.

She carried the billeting form into the kitchen and laid it under the sugar tin to stop her own hand from tearing it. The spare room held two years of things she had not had the heart to throw out. It held her mother's rosary beads in a cracked blue box, a stack of mended infant vests from her sister in Hull, a jar of buttons sorted by color in old jam glasses, Peter's old account books tied with string, and the blanket she had started for this baby and abandoned halfway when the bleeding started in week three of the freeze and she was afraid to finish anything that could be folded around a body.

Arthur set his suitcase down by the stairs but did not follow. "Tell me where not to stand," he said.

Noreen looked at him then. Up close his eyelashes were pale with melted frost. There was a white crescent scar behind his left ear where skin had once split and healed badly. He was trying so hard not to be in their way that it made him clumsy.

"Just don't touch the top shelf," she said. "That's mine."

"I wouldn't dream of it."

Peter had gone back to the kitchen and his sums. Noreen could hear his pencil crossing and recrossing a number until the paper thinned. Every time she carried a box past, he looked up and counted her trips rather than the boxes.

By noon she had moved the baby things into the corner of her own bedroom, stacking them behind the wardrobe where Peter would not look unless he needed a reason. The spare room was small, with a bed narrow enough to make any grown man feel apologetic. The wallpaper had cabbage roses faded to bruised pink. She shook out the quilt, beat dust from it in the back yard, and watched the powder freeze mid-air before it reached the ground.

Arthur came to the doorframe and waited for her nod before stepping in. "Need a hand with the grate?"

"I'll do it."

"You are doing it," he said, not correcting her, just stating. "I can black the bars while you're elsewhere."

Noreen moved aside. He knelt, took a rag from his pocket, and polished the iron with quick practiced circles. He did not ask where to put his shirt, where to keep his tools, where to hang anything. When she brought him a chipped washbasin and a mug, he thanked her as if she had given him a room at a hotel.

At one point he took the yellow scarf off and folded it into a square, precise as a handkerchief, then tucked it under his pillow. He did not drape it over the chair or fling it with his other damp things. He kept it under the pillow, where no one would spill tea on it.

Peter appeared in the doorway in his clipped committee voice. "We keep breakfast at seven, supper at six-thirty. No smoking upstairs. Bathroom use before ten if possible to conserve coal."

Arthur stood. "Understood."

"If there is night call, do not wake my wife unless absolutely necessary. She needs regular rest."

Noreen laughed, thin and wrong in her own ears. Peter glanced at her once and she felt the warning in it.

Arthur nodded anyway. "I'll step lightly."

Peter turned to Noreen. "Tea."

Arthur said, "No need for fuss."

"It's no fuss," Peter said. "My wife is efficient."

Noreen went to boil the kettle with her jaw set so hard she felt the hinge grind. She could hear the men in the spare room discussing fuse loads and line collapse out near Stainforth Road, and she could hear, through the shared wall, Elsie coughing in her own kitchen and opening the coal scuttle. Sound traveled like gossip on Barrow Lane; it did not ask permission.

She wanted to knock three times on the back wall in the old pattern she and Elsie had started using during the false labor night, one for safe, two for come if you can, three for now. Her knuckles hovered, then dropped to her side. Arthur's bag was three feet from the wall and Peter was in listening distance.

At half two Elsie came by the front with an empty flour tin under her arm, cheeks chapped red from wind. She stopped dead when she saw Arthur at the hall table re-soldering the broken clasp on his own torch with a spirit lamp no bigger than an eggcup.

"Oh," Elsie said. "You've company."

Noreen saw her take in everything in a blink: the extra cup, Peter's posture, the billeting paper still under the sugar tin where the red stamp showed like a wound. Elsie's hand tightened on the tin handle.

"Council order," Noreen said. "Mr Hale's in the spare room."

Arthur stood so quickly he bumped his knee on the table. "Afternoon. Arthur Hale." He wiped his hand on his trouser seam before offering it. "Sorry if I'm in your way."

Elsie shook it. "Elsie Harrow. Next door."

"Your wall's warmer than ours," Arthur said, then gave a small embarrassed laugh. "Sorry. First thing I notice in a house is where the heat leaks. Bad habit."

"Could be worse habits," Elsie said.

Peter came in from the scullery, eyes going from Elsie to the flour tin to Noreen. "Mrs Harrow. If this is about another cup of sugar, we are all on strict rations this week."

Elsie lifted the tin. "It's your own flour from Tuesday. I'm returning it."

"How conscientious."

Noreen stepped in before the silence thickened. "I'll put the kettle back on."

Elsie's glance flicked to her, quick and bright with warning that this was not the place and not the moment.

When Noreen came back with tea, Elsie had moved to the hallway and was discussing paraffin queues with Arthur, both of them careful and ordinary, voices filed flat by caution. Peter sat at the table with his fingertips pressed together and tracked each sentence the way he checked postmarks before opening an envelope.

That evening, when Elsie left, she did not slip through the yard as she usually did. She buttoned her coat in full view of the front room window and walked home by the pavement, even though ice made it slower.

Noreen watched until her shape disappeared behind the hedge and understood, with a cold that had nothing to do with weather, that this was how it would be now.

She and Elsie would still exchange what needed exchanging, but never in the old easy line from scullery to scullery, never with backs turned, never without counting who had eyes.

Arthur unpacked after supper. He laid out one shirt, two wool jumpers, an oilskin rolled tight, spare socks darned at heel with mismatched thread, and a paperback detective novel swollen from damp. He set a framed photograph on the bedside table: a woman in a nurse's cape, smiling into sun that had no place in Calderbank. Then he noticed Noreen at the doorway and turned the frame face down without fuss, as if he did it every day.

"Sorry," he said quietly. "Didn't mean to make it personal."

"It's your room," she said, then corrected herself because it was not, because that was the point. "For now."

He gave her a look she could not read and said, "For now's what most of us get."

She lay awake that night listening to a stranger breathe on the other side of the landing and to Peter breathe beside her in clipped, controlled counts, in for four, out for four; he ran his own lungs like a drill.

The wall got no signal from her that night.

* * *

By the third day Arthur's boots had left two half-moons of dried mud near the door no matter how often he cleaned them, and Peter had started treating mud like a moral category.

"It is not difficult," he said at breakfast, buttering toast in exact edges, "to keep common areas orderly."

Arthur looked at the floor, then at Noreen with apology already there. "I'll scrub it before I go out."

"No, you will eat while it's hot," Noreen said.

Peter folded his napkin once. "Noreen, do not manage the guest by contradiction."

Arthur opened his mouth, then closed it. He chewed in silence.

Noreen passed him the marmalade anyway. The jar was nearly empty, rind and syrup clinging to glass like amber. Arthur took a teaspoon and scraped carefully, leaving enough that someone else could pretend there was still jam tomorrow. It was the kind of habit people learned from long years of not being first at a table.

Outside, the lane had narrowed to a black runnel between snowbanks gone grey with soot. Men from two streets over dragged a dead scooter to the corner and left it there like driftwood. Every afternoon someone shouted for paraffin and someone else shouted back that there was none. Every evening a baby cried from the Hodgsons' house at number six and stopped only when someone sang hymns off-key.

Inside, Peter performed generosity for Arthur and correction for Noreen in the same breath.

"Take another potato, Mr Hale."

"Noreen, thinner slices please, we're not feeding navvies."

"Mr Hale, do use the fire in your room, we insist."

"Noreen, cut tomorrow's coal and make tonight's burn cover it."

He did not raise his voice. He did not need to. The table itself carried him, the way it had been set and counted and paid for by him, that was his argument in wood and crockery.

Arthur answered with yes and thank you and I can manage. He never argued, never sympathized too quickly, never looked away when Peter's tone sharpened. That last part unsettled Noreen most. Most men looked politely past another man's house-rules. Arthur watched them land.

On Friday Noreen asked Peter for milk money before shop closing. He was at the sideboard with his wage envelope open, notes squared, coins sorted in stacks by denomination.

"How much?" he asked.

"One and eight. If she's got any left."

"For what quantity?"

"A pint and maybe a gill if she cuts me slack."

He lifted two shillings, held them, then put one back. "You had one and four yesterday."

"For bread and paraffin." She kept her voice flat. "You watched me pay."

"I watch many things. It does not follow that all are legitimate."

Arthur came in then from the yard with a coil of cable over his shoulder. He stopped, took in the open envelope, Peter's hand still above the coins, and set the cable down by the door as softly as if there was a sleeping child nearby.

"Wrong place?" he asked.

Peter smiled without warmth. "Not at all. Household order."

"Right." Arthur nodded once. "I've seen whole substations go down for less than a missing shilling."

Peter gave him the single shilling and three pennies, not enough. Noreen did not reach for them.

"One and eight," she said.

"You will make one and three stretch." He tilted his head toward Arthur. "Our arrangement now includes a third mouth."

Noreen took a breath that hurt along her ribs. "Your arrangement included your pub tab last Tuesday as well, and that stretched."

Peter's face did not change, but his voice turned formal enough to frost. "Do not address me in that tone in front of a guest."

Arthur said, "I can eat at depot when I'm out, truly. It's no burden."

"You are not the burden," Peter said.

For a second no one moved. Noreen heard, through the wall, Elsie dropping a pan and swearing low, the word clipped off before it carried.

Arthur cleared his throat. "Mrs Walsh, pass me that screwdriver, will you? I can reset the back-gate latch before dark if the screws still bite. Frost's swelling the wood."

He had shifted the room half an inch with one line. Peter was still standing over his coins, but now he had to decide whether to refuse a useful repair and look small, or concede the moment.

He set down another sixpence. "Very well."

Noreen picked up the coins and felt heat rise in her face, not gratitude, never that. Rage and relief often wore the same coat.

She went to Mrs Bhatti's with the shopping bag and the money damp in her glove. Elsie was at the flour bins arguing quietly about yeast. They did not stand together. They did not touch sleeves. Elsie said, "Morning," in the same voice she used for women she barely knew.

Noreen answered in kind.

At the till, with Mrs Bhatti's pencil tucked behind one ear and her radio coughing static near the cigarettes, Noreen slid her list over and said, "No credit this week. Cash only."

Mrs Bhatti lifted one eyebrow. "Since when?"

"Since now."

Elsie did not turn around, but Noreen saw her shoulders go tight under her coat.

On the way home Noreen took the longer route past the chapel rather than cut through Elsie's yard. The snow there had gone to slush over frozen stones, and her boots filled at the seams. She did it anyway. She could feel the shape of Arthur in the house now the way she felt the mantel with her hip in the dark: not always in sight, but always there.

When she got back, Arthur had fixed the back-gate latch. Peter tested it twice, then threaded stiff garden wire through the latch and tied it to a brass handbell hung on a scullery nail. Any lift of that gate would ring in their kitchen. "No casual traffic through our yard," he said, not looking at her while he spoke. "If Mrs Harrow needs anything, she comes by the front."

In the afternoon Elsie came by to return a potato masher and borrow a pan lid. She stood in the hallway with her gloves on.

"Can't stay," she said. "Tom's due."

"You all right?"

"Fine." Elsie looked past her at the stairs, where Arthur's coat hung on the peg below Peter's. "You?"

"Fine."

The lie passed between them smooth as soap.

Arthur came down carrying a cracked plug head and paused when he saw Elsie. "Mrs Harrow, your front room lamp, if it's still flickering, I can look after tea. No charge."

Elsie gave a quick smile. "It'll keep till tomorrow."

"Tomorrow might not keep," Arthur said, then seemed to hear himself sounding like a noticeboard and added, "Only if you want."

"Thanks," she said. "I'll send for you if it goes."

She left before Peter came in from the lavatory. Noreen watched her through the glass panel in the door, crossing the lane with shoulders tucked and each step measured to look ordinary. A woman who had learned exactly how quickly ordinary movement could be called suspicious.

That night at supper Peter asked Arthur about line failures around the estate and listened with the solemnity of a committee man.

"So if residents overloaded sockets with electric bars, they contributed to outages," Peter said.

Arthur buttered bread with the wrong side of the knife, absentminded. "Contributed, yes. But mostly it's ice load and old kit. People are cold. They do daft things when they're cold."

"Discipline prevents daftness."

"Sometimes," Arthur said. "Sometimes folk just need heat before they need lectures."

Peter gave a soft snort. "That is a generous philosophy for a utility worker."

"It's not philosophy," Arthur said. "It's what I see when I knock doors at two in the morning."

Noreen had not heard anyone answer Peter like that in his own house. A simple correction, level and unbowed, laid down like a plank over bad ground.

Peter turned to her. "More tea."

She stood and reached for the pot. Arthur stood at the same time and took it from her before she had to lean across the table.

"I've got it," he said.

Peter's jaw worked once. "You are a guest, Mr Hale."

Arthur poured anyway, steady hand, no spill. "Tonight I'm nearest the kettle, and my hands are steady. Let me pour."

Noreen sat back down and looked at the steam rising between them. She kept the thanks in her throat. Thanks made a debt. She already owed too many in this house.

Later, in bed, Peter said into the dark, "I do not want you encouraging familiarity with him."

Noreen stared at the ceiling crack shaped like a river map. "By letting him hold a kettle?"

"By performing solidarity against me."

She rolled onto her side to face away. "Maybe stop giving strangers a reason to notice your manners."

There was a long pause. Then the mattress dipped as he turned and his hand landed on her hip in that practiced claim men called gentle because it did not bruise, an ownership touch, a stamp.

"Do not provoke me, Noreen," he said softly. "You are not in a position to test boundaries right now."

She fixed her eyes on the ceiling crack and laid her own hand over his, easing it off her hip one finger at a time until it rested on the sheet between them. Then she kept both hands flat and her breathing even, because any sudden move would start a fight and she had no room left for one. Through the wall she heard Elsie and Tom beginning their evening argument, his voice loud with drink already, hers low and tight as wrapped string. Arthur's floorboard creaked once above, then quieted.

By Saturday morning Peter had moved the handbell to the kitchen sill where he could see it and left the curtain half open while he wrote at the table.

Noreen carried potato peelings to the bin and saw Elsie at her own step with a folded apron over one arm.

"Morning, Mrs Walsh," Elsie called, too bright and too loud, the way you spoke when you wanted windows to hear. "You still wanting that drippings tin back, or should I leave it at Mrs Bhatti's?"

Noreen felt Peter's gaze through the glass before she answered. "Leave it at the shop, thanks. Easier that way."

A beat passed. Elsie's mouth tightened and smoothed. "Right then. Weather's turning."

"Looks it."

Arthur came down the yard path with a coal scuttle, glanced between them, and looked away, giving the exchange the shape of ordinary neighbor business.

Peter rapped once on the window frame. "Noreen, inside."

She carried the peelings back in and hated how fast her body obeyed.

* * *

The blackout hit at ten past seven on Sunday evening with a sound like someone biting foil, a hard little crack from inside the walls, and then all at once the lane went dark.

Noreen had just lifted the pot from the stove. Steam ghosted up and vanished into black. Peter swore, formal even in anger. "Damn these incompetent schedules."

Arthur was already in the hallway, torch in hand. "Stay where you are. I'll check your board first."

"You are not on duty," Peter said.

"Electric doesn't care about duty hours."

Noreen set the pot down by touch and felt for the match tin. Peter took it from her hand and struck first, claiming the first flame for himself. The match lit his face from below, giving him hollows under the cheekbones. He handed the candle to her only after he had his own cigarette lit from it.

Across the wall Tom's voice came through, slurred and high. "Elsie? Elsie, where've you put the bloody lamp?"

A heavy thud followed, then another, cupboard doors or fists.

Noreen moved toward the back door. Peter caught her sleeve.

"You will not go over there in the dark."

"She's near term."

"So are you."

Arthur had the meter cupboard open now, torch clamped between his teeth, fingers quick on the fuses. "Main in this house is intact," he said around the light. "Looks street-side. Could be branch fault."

Tom shouted again, nearer now, and then the shared back gate slammed. Boots scraped on their step. Before Peter could answer, Tom banged on their door with the side of his fist.

Noreen opened it first.

Tom filled the frame with wet coat, red eyes, and the thick sweet stink of ale. Snowmelt ran off his cap brim onto the mat Arthur had scrubbed that afternoon. He had no gloves on. His hands were shaking, whether from cold or drink she could not tell.

"Your bloody lodger's nicked the power," Tom said. "Whole row's dark and you've got a torch like Christmas."

Peter appeared behind Noreen. "Lower your voice."

"Don't you tell me what to do in my own lane." Tom leaned past Noreen, peering into the hall. "Where's Elsie?"

"At your house, I assume," Peter said.

"She weren't when I come in."

Noreen felt the air leave her chest. "What do you mean she wasn't?"

Tom waved a hand, impatient at his own confusion. "She was there. Then she weren't. Lamps gone. Pan on boil. You think I can keep track when it's pitch-black?"

Arthur stepped into view, wiping his hands on a rag. "Mr Harrow, if she's come over, she's not here yet. Let's check your place and the lane."

Tom's eyes narrowed at him. "Who asked you, spark boy?"

Arthur did not flinch. "No one. But shouting won't bring the lights up."

Peter said, too quickly, "Mr Hale is board staff. He is here by council order."

"Yeah, and now we've all got to salute?"

Tom took a step forward that might have been toward Peter, might have been toward the room itself, toward any surface he could knock to prove he existed. Noreen smelled stale beer and cold tobacco and, underneath, fear sour as old milk.

"Tom," she said, keeping her voice level. "Where did you last see Elsie?"

"Kitchen. Then I went for lamp oil and she starts on at me about the money and then all this goes and—"

He broke off, looked at Arthur's torch, and seemed to remember an audience. "None of your business anyway."

Arthur crouched, unlatched his toolkit, and held up a coil of insulated wire. "Could use your hands if they're steady enough. Hold the lantern while I check the feed at your meter. If the tail's scorched, I can make it safe tonight but not tidy till morning."

Tom snorted. "My hands are fine."

"Good," Arthur said. "Prove it."

It was not a challenge in tone, but it landed like one. Peter shifted sideways to watch. Noreen saw the trap click into place and hated that she was grateful for it.

Tom looked from one face to another and found no easy exit. "All right then. Come on."

They moved as a knot through the yard: Arthur with tools, Tom with lantern, Peter with his coat thrown over pajamas and pride held high like a collar, Noreen behind because no one had told her not to and she was past waiting for permission. Wind cut through the passage and lifted her nightdress hem around her calves. The snow in the yard had crusted hard enough to hold footprints for days.

Elsie was in her own scullery when they got there, bent over the sink with a candle stub jammed in a saucer and a pan still rolling at full boil. She had a tea towel looped around one hand and was dragging open the wall cupboard with the other, looking for clean cloth in the dark. She looked up sharp as a trapped bird, then saw Noreen and let one breath out.

"Pan boiled over," she said. "I grabbed it wrong."

Tom shoved past Peter and came up on Elsie's shoulder before anyone could block him. "Don't start lying to cover yourself. Where's my wage packet?"

He caught Elsie's forearm and jerked her toward him. The baby's weight dropped hard in Noreen's pelvis. One shove from Tom could put her on the flags and she knew it; she moved anyway, bracing her hip against the table and driving herself into the gap.

"Take your hand off her."

Elsie twisted to get free. The pan handle skidded on the wet sink edge and a fan of boiling water slashed across the back of her hand. She hissed through her teeth and folded at the waist, clutching it.

Tom let go long enough to rear back, fist up at Elsie's face. She ducked on instinct. His knuckles smashed the wall above the breadboard with a crack of plaster and lath. The clock jumped off one nail and hit the tiles, glass popping inward. Two cups clattered out of the rack and burst at Noreen's feet.

For one beat everyone listened to Tom's breath and Elsie's ragged swearing. White plaster dust coated Tom's split knuckles; blood started through it in bright beads.

"Leave off," Noreen said, low now.

"It's my wife."

"It's her hand."

Noreen saw his shoulders bunching again, the look he got when shame needed an audience to hit. If she challenged him head-on, he would swing at whichever face was nearest. She kept her eyes on Elsie's hand and gave Arthur an opening. "Arthur, put him on the lantern before he breaks the whole kitchen."

Arthur was already at the meter box by the back door, lantern light cutting his face into planes. "Mr Harrow, hold this steady, at my shoulder height. Not there. There."

Tom held it too low. Arthur repositioned his wrist with two fingers, not rough. "If you wobble, I can't read the contacts."

"I ain't wobbling."

The lantern shook enough to make shadows jump.

Peter stood by the table, lips thin. "This scene is unnecessary."

"The scene is your lane in blackout," Arthur said. "I'd take unnecessary over fire."

He popped the cover, checked the cartridge fuse, then swore once, low. "Feed's iced on the branch. Not internal fault."

Tom made a triumphant noise. "See? Not my bloody wiring then."

"Not tonight," Arthur said.

Noreen caught the words and so did Tom. His face changed, a flare and collapse in one beat.

"What's that meant to mean?"

Arthur did not look at him. "Means I still need that lantern steady." He reached deeper into the box, fingertips numb and precise. "Peter, can you fetch the dry cloth from my bag? Left pocket."

Peter hesitated. It was the first time anyone had given him an instruction in that yard. Then he turned and did it, because refusing would look childish in front of the two women.

In the kitchen Elsie sat on the stool, jaw tight, injured hand in a bowl of cool water now. Noreen knelt beside her and checked the palm. Skin lifted in one narrow strip near the thumb pad. Red finger marks were already darkening around Elsie's wrist where Tom had grabbed her.

"Can you close it?" Noreen said.

Elsie flexed slowly, wincing. "Enough to work. Not enough to scrub pans tonight."

Noreen nodded and wrapped the towel tighter above the burn. "You'll need clean dressing."

Elsie kept her eyes on the water. "I know."

From the doorway Tom barked, knuckles split and chalked white with plaster, "I can hear you whispering."

"Then hear this," Elsie said without turning. "Shut your mouth and hold the light like the man told you."

The silence that followed was thick enough to chew.

Arthur said quietly, "Good level. Keep it there."

Noreen almost laughed, not because it was funny but because it was the first useful command Tom had obeyed in weeks and it had come from a stranger in a borrowed bed.

Five minutes later the branch feed coughed back and the bulb above Elsie's sink flared weak yellow, then steadied. A cheer went up somewhere down the lane. Someone banged a saucepan in celebration. Somewhere else, a child started crying at the sudden light.

Arthur closed the box and rubbed his hands together hard. "That'll hold if no one overloads. One heater per socket. No daisy chains."

Tom tossed the lantern onto the table too hard. "Right. You heard the man."

Peter said, "We all appreciate your expertise, Mr Hale."

Arthur slung his bag over his shoulder. "No expertise in not freezing. Just procedure."

Tom leaned in toward him, smile wet and ugly. "Think this makes you boss round here? Sleeping in Walsh's spare and telling men what to do in their own houses?"

Arthur met his eyes and spoke in the same calm he had used for the fuse. "No. It makes me tired and still on call." He nodded toward Elsie's bandaged hand. "And it makes me want fewer accidents."

Tom laughed once, sharp. "Accidents."

He patted his coat pockets, then his trouser pockets, faster each time. "Where's my packet then?"

Elsie closed her eyes. "Inside your cap lining. You stuffed it there at Mrs Doyle's and forgot."

Tom snatched off his cap, shook it, and a folded wage envelope slid onto the floorboards between his boots. No one moved to pick it up.

Peter made a small sound in his throat that might have been pity and might have been satisfaction.

Tom bent for the envelope, missed it on the first try, got it on the second, and stood with his face gone mottled red. He looked at Arthur, at Peter, at the women, seeing exactly what each of them had seen.

"Long day," he muttered. "Anyone could do that."

"Course," Elsie said.

The softness in her voice cut deeper than shouting would have.

Tom stuffed the packet into his inside pocket this time and buttoned the coat wrong, button into wrong hole, hands thick. "We're done here," he said to no one in particular, and pushed past Noreen, shoulder clipping the doorframe hard enough to shake the hanging cups.

When he had gone into the front room, Arthur crouched by Elsie. "May I?" he asked, indicating her hand.

She gave him the towel. He unwrapped it, checked the burn without fuss, and said, "Keep it cool ten more minutes, then dry dressing. No grease."

"You a linesman or a nurse?" Elsie asked.

"Brother to three sisters," he said. "You learn things."

There it was, the detail that made him a man and not just a coat on a peg. Three sisters somewhere who had burned themselves on pans and irons and kettle spouts. People who had taught him where to put his hands and when not to.

Peter had gone silent in the corner, watching Noreen and Elsie close together by the sink. Not touching, but near enough to feel. Noreen stood and took one step back, then another, widening the space on purpose.

Arthur saw it. Of course he saw it.

He packed his tools and said, "I'll check number six before I turn in. Their baby's night-light plug was sparking yesterday."

Peter recovered first. "Commendable diligence."

Arthur gave him a level look. "Nothing commendable. That plug either gets checked or it starts a fire."

Noreen walked him to the back step. The lane hummed now with restored current: radios crackling back to life, kettles relit, voices louder because fear had somewhere to drain.

"Thank you," she said, because this debt she would name.

He shook his head. "Don't thank me. Tom's meter tail is loose, and your gate-bell wire is stripped where it bends. I'll swap both in daylight."

She frowned. "Is it dangerous?"

Arthur adjusted the yellow scarf at his throat. "Not if you're careful. One heater per socket tonight, no daisy chains, and keep a bucket by the stove in case that wall plug spits again." He looked toward the two houses, mirror fronts with one shared spine of brick. "If he starts shouting next door, knock my door first. If I'm in, I'll come."

Before she could answer, he added, gentler, "I've got sisters. I know that kind of quiet."

Then he went down the step into the lane, torch beam low and steady, leaving Noreen with the cold and the truth of it.

Inside, Peter was already at the table with pen and paper, drafting terms before the door had shut.

"From tomorrow," he said, "there will be no yard chatter, no wall tapping, and no visits after dark. This lane lives on gossip and I will not have our name fed to it."

"Our name?"

"Do not play innocent."

Noreen looked at him, then at the shared wall, then at the doorway where Arthur's coat hook waited empty for his return.

"You can write rules all night," she said. "That bell on the back gate still rings, and making Elsie use the front door doesn't make us private."

Peter's mouth flattened. "Privacy is maintained when people follow instructions the first time."

"No," she said. "Privacy was when nobody looked. Now they hear the bell, they hear you tapping that wall, and they count who crosses which step."

He stood so fast the chair scraped. "You are becoming insolent."

"I'm not insolent," she said. "I'm saying we're on show."

He reached for her wrist and stopped short, hand open in the air between them. He had two kinds of witness now: the man in the spare room, and the woman he had married who had started saying things aloud.

She went to the stove, lifted the kettle, and poured out the water gone metallic from too much boiling. In the sink the steam rose and struck the window where frost was already feathering back across the glass from the edges in.

Across the wall Tom and Elsie were speaking in voices too low to catch words. The rhythm was wrong for peace. Noreen set the kettle down and listened anyway, because listening was what women did when doors were thin and consequences were thicker.

When Arthur came back in, he paused in the hallway and wiped his boots with slow deliberate strokes, twice each sole, a ritual meant to hold a line against mess.

Noreen went upstairs without speaking. She took the half-finished baby blanket from behind the wardrobe and sat on the bed with it spread across her knees, wool rough against her palms. She added twelve stitches, then had to pull back four where her count had slipped. From the spare room she heard Arthur turn a page in his damp detective novel. From the kitchen below she heard Peter fold paper and unfold it again.

She stayed put.

She kept her knuckles off the wall.

Before she slept, she placed the billeting order in the bottom drawer under her chemises and stockings, where Peter would not look because he assumed he already knew what mattered.

Elsie's blue-enamel saucepan sat on the draining board, carrot-and-turnip mash drying to a skin at the rim; Noreen left it there because sending it back too fast looked like a signal now.

In the morning she would walk to the shop by the long route again. Elsie would wave from her own gate with tidy neighbor distance, not as a co-conspirator in hunger and fear. They would talk about potatoes and paraffin and never say the names of things that kept them alive.

The stranger in the spare room would hear enough, and he would hear too much.


# Chapter 8

Smoke sat low over Barrow Lane, and the snow underfoot held every bootprint like a grudge. Noreen carried Elsie's blue-enamel saucepan wrapped in a clean tea towel, tucked under her arm as if enamel could bruise. The pan had only gone next door three nights ago with a mash of carrots and turnip, but she had made herself wait to return it. She had done the long route to the shop first so no one watching windows could call this a signal. She told herself she had been busy. She told herself Peter watched the clock every time she crossed the threshold. She told herself there was no reason for two women to stand shoulder to shoulder at a sink when one could hand a thing back and go home.

Somebody on the far corner had burned wet coal again and the smell dragged in the throat. Noreen tucked the towel tighter under her arm and kept one hand against the small, hard pull of her belly. A dense downward weight worked at her while the day pressed from all sides.

She knocked once on Elsie's back door. There was no answer. She knocked again and heard the slow scrape of a chair leg, then Elsie's voice from inside.

"Door's open. Mind the mat, it's soaked through."

Noreen stepped into the narrow scullery and shut the door fast to keep the heat. Heat was generous for what the house held: one stingy strip from the range and the damp breath off washing that would not dry. Elsie stood by the sink in a cardigan with two buttons missing, sleeves rolled over callused forearms, hair pinned up any way it would stay. Her face looked thinner than last month. Her belly did not.

"I brought your pan," Noreen said, lifting it like proof she had come for a single purpose.

"You didn't have to run it back in this weather."

"If I leave your things in my place, I start believing they're mine." Noreen made it sound joking and watched Elsie take the joke and fold it away.

Elsie wiped her hands on a cloth and nodded toward the lower cupboard. "Stick it under there, would you? I can't kneel today without needing a crane to get up again."

"You and me both."

Noreen crouched. The cupboard door under the sink had swollen at the bottom where the boards always drank drips, and the latch caught. She tugged once and nothing moved. She set the saucepan down, braced one palm against the frame, and pulled harder. The door came free with a wet crack and hit her shin. A small tin, two folded tea towels, and a sheaf of papers slid forward and spilled against her skirt.

"Damn thing," Elsie said behind her. "Leave it. I'll sort it later."

"You'll sort it with that back? Don't be daft."

Noreen gathered the papers first because they were already drinking the puddle near the pipe. Her fingers caught blue carbon, cheap cream paper, and one stiff card with a bookmaker's stamp in green ink. She froze long enough for the kettle to tick on the hob and for Elsie to take one step closer.

The top slip read:

HAYES & SON TURF ACCOUNT
T. HARROW
DUE: GBP 9 15s

Below it sat another slip. Then she saw a third with different dates and different stakes, but the same short name in the same blunt hand. A fourth had the corner torn, and at the bottom a pencilled note: settled part, see next week.

Noreen put the slips into a neat pile because her hands needed to do something precise. She kept turning pages. Under the betting slips sat a folded Post Office withdrawal docket with a purple stamp bleeding through from the back. The signature line read Elsie Harrow. The letters leaned right and heavy, the H climbing too tall. Noreen had seen that same upward H on the arrears authorisation in December, when the red-letter envelope had landed at her door by mistake.

She heard herself breathe through her teeth.

"What is it?" Elsie asked. "Has he left rubbish in there again?"

Noreen did not answer at once. She set the saucepan upright, moved the tea towels away from the drip, and spread the slips on the lid, pressing each damp corner flat while the kettle ticked too loud in the tight room.

Elsie came down beside her slower than she should have, one hand flat to the cupboard edge, face already wary.

"Tom's betting bits," she said when she saw the first line. "He says they're old."

"Old from last Tuesday?" Noreen tapped a date with her nail. "And Thursday. And yesterday."

Elsie took one slip, frowned, put it back exactly where it had been. "He keeps saying he's squaring it. Men do that talk, don't they. They posture." Her voice had gone airy, the way people spoke to children over cracked glass.

Noreen held up the withdrawal docket. "Did you sign this?"

Elsie squinted. "Looks like my name."

"That wasn't what I asked."

Silence thickened. From next door a cough came through the wall, then a heavy tread. Somebody dragged a coal scuttle across stone. The terrace kept no secrets if the houses decided to listen.

"Let me see." Elsie took the docket and read it with her lips moving. "Thirty-two pounds."

"From your savings."

"He told me he might need ten for the coal man in January. We talked about ten."

"This says thirty-two, and it says drawn in your name on the twenty-first. You were at the clinic on the twenty-first because you came back with the blood test bruise and swore at the stairs all afternoon."

Elsie's mouth opened, shut, opened again. She looked down at her inner elbow and felt the remembered needle-pinch and the yellow ache that had sat there for days.

Noreen hated that she remembered the date. Hated more that she remembered because she had wanted excuses to call next door. She kept her voice level.

"There's another one under it."

The second docket was for twelve pounds and change. It came from a different week with the same signature and the same tall H. It carried the same lie of a flourish at the end, like a tail trying to pass for elegance.

Elsie sat back on her heels with a sharp breath and pressed her knuckles to her mouth. "God." She said it flat, without prayer and without shock, as if she needed to place the word somewhere solid.

Noreen stacked the dockets with the betting slips and looked past them into the open cupboard. There were three onions softening in a net bag, half a sack of flour tied with string, two jars of dripping clouded white, and a chipped mug full of odds and ends: a hinge screw, two safety pins, a pawn ticket stub, a small prayer card gone greasy at the corners. They were ordinary things, and they were the things that held a life together. In the middle of them lay paper that could gut a household.

"How much is it in total?" Elsie asked, still staring.

"I haven't added every line yet. It's north of a hundred easy."

"You're sure."

"I'm sure enough to stop pretending." Noreen said it softly because she knew what came after a sentence like that.

Elsie braced one hand on the floorboards and pushed herself upright with a grunt. At the sink she filled a mug and let the water run over her knuckles until they numbed, then wiped a spoon that was already clean and lined three chipped cups along the sill by size. She kept rearranging them, listening to the small clinks, as if neat crockery could hold the shaking in her chest.

"Maybe he used it to clear the pram account," Elsie said. "Maybe there's a reason that's ugly but not ruin."

"Then where's the receipt? Where's one blessed note from him saying what he paid and when?"

"He wouldn't keep it in a wet cupboard."

"No. He'd keep his own name clean and let yours do the dirty." Noreen heard the edge in her voice and forced it back. "Listen to me. I'm not trying to make this worse than it is. I'm trying to stop you being the last one in the room to know what room you're in."

Elsie shut her eyes. Her shoulders dropped, then held there.

"I asked you once already to stay out of my marriage," she said quietly.

"You asked me to keep one letter quiet. I did."

"You looked anyway."

"Your cupboard threw it at my knees."

The line might have been funny in another kitchen. It died in this one.

Noreen rose, feeling the drag in her lower back, and set the whole stack of slips on the dry part of the table. The urge to bolt tightened her calves. Peter would mark the wet hem of her skirt, glance at the clock, and ask one mild question that opened into ten. She pressed her thumbnail into the side seam until the sting steadied her breathing.

"I'll go if you want," she said. "But if I walk out now, you'll have this in your head alone and he'll be through that door before dark with some story about bad luck and mates and one final flutter. You know how he says final like it has a lock on it."

Elsie did not look at her. "Don't quote him to me."

"Then tell me what you need."

A long minute passed. Outside, a cart rattled at the end of the lane and stopped. Someone shouted for salt. The range hissed as dripped water struck iron.

Elsie turned at last, and the politeness was gone from her face, replaced by arithmetic.

"Sit down," she said. "If I stand, I'll keep wiping that sink till we both freeze." She pulled two chairs to the table. "You're going to show me every date, and we're going to do the sums right."

Noreen sat.

She spread the slips by week.

The boundary she had set for herself in the lane melted on the floor with the tracked snow from her boots.

***

Elsie had always believed that numbers could be tamed if she wrote them in columns. She had not believed in luck; she had believed in lists: coal by the scuttle, bread by the week, and soap to the ounce. She kept money in envelopes with names on them, each folded twice. Even shame could be managed if she named it correctly and put it where no one would step in it.

The papers on her kitchen table would not stay in their column.

She watched Noreen's hand move from slip to slip, forefinger blackened with carbon smudge, and felt the house tilt two degrees to the left. The shift left the cups upright and made every plate seem to lean toward breaking.

"Read this one," Noreen said.

Elsie took the docket and read it again though she had already read it twice. The date matched the week before Christmas. The amount sat in thick numerals. The branch stamp bit through the paper. The signature read Elsie Harrow.

Her own name, written by a man who slept in her bed when he came home warm enough to sleep.

"Could be mine," she said, because defense rose before sense. "I'm tired half the time. Maybe I signed and forgot."

Noreen looked up with a face that held no pity and no impatience, which was somehow worse than either. "Then compare it."

"Compare what?"

"Your actual hand."

Elsie reached to the mantel for the ration book and a pencil stub, then sat again. She wrote Elsie Harrow on the back of a coal chit. Her letters slanted left when she hurried. Her H sat squat, practical, no decoration. Tom's version climbed and looped as if trying to look educated from a distance.

Noreen set the two names side by side.

"He's copied your spelling," she said. "Not your hand."

Anger sparked in Elsie at the calm in Noreen's voice. Elsie pinched one slip between finger and thumb until the paper bowed and creaked, ready to rip and feed the range. The motion stalled. She counted instead.

She counted nine pounds fifteen, then seven, then eleven, then fourteen and six. She added three and ten, then twelve and two, and then the lines marked settled part and carried over. By the time she reached the last slip, the total sat at one hundred forty-six pounds and some shillings. Noreen had said north of a hundred. Noreen had spared her the exact weight by less than a pound.

The amount did not land as a number first. It landed as rooms.

It was seven months' rent if the landlord stayed kind.

It was coal to March and then some.

It was baby clothes, blankets, pram instalments, doctor fares, bread, milk, and the cheap oranges Tom only bought when he wanted to be praised for trying.

It was money she had never held at once in her life.

She put the pencil down before the wood snapped in her hand.

"He can't owe that," she said. "No one gives him that much rope."

"Bookies do, till they don't." Noreen touched the edge of one slip where a thumbprint had blurred the ink. "Look here. This note says collect at house if missed again."

Elsie felt a small clean line of cold open between her shoulders. Blizzard fear had shape: road, ice, man in a ditch. This one wore paper: knock-at-the-door fear, signature fear. It was a different weather entirely.

"If they come," she said, "they come to me as well."

"Aye."

"If the Post Office asks, they'll say I signed these."

"Aye."

"And if I say I didn't, I'm calling my husband a forger in public."

Noreen's jaw tightened. "Yes."

The three yeses rang like spoons dropped in a sink.

Elsie stood, then sat again because standing made the child inside her press low and hard. She shifted in the chair, found no comfortable spot, tried anyway.

"I can hear him now," she said. "He'll say it was temporary. He'll say he was covering us till wages settled. He'll say if I make noise we'll both lose everything." Her voice went dry and precise. "He'll say a wife should not humiliate her husband in a winter like this."

"He'll say whatever keeps him one week ahead of the man he owes," Noreen said. "That doesn't make it true."

"True doesn't buy me a thing if I can't pay to prove it."

Noreen almost smiled at that, not from humor but recognition. "Aye. That's the trap."

Elsie pushed the slips into a tighter stack. The table had flour grit under her palms; she ground it back and forth until the skin burned.

"Why did you keep the first letter quiet in December?" she asked.

"Because you asked me. Because you looked like you might split at the seam if one more person knew." Noreen held her gaze. "And because I thought maybe it was one desperate thing. Men do stupid things in December and call them promises. I was wrong."

Elsie nodded once. She remembered that day too clearly now: red stamp on the envelope, Noreen standing on the step with snow in her hair, both of them speaking low so the lane would not hear. She had believed she could contain it then. One lie, one payment, one month and done.

Containment had lasted six weeks.

From next door came the thud of a door, then Arthur's voice through plaster, apologizing to no one in particular as he knocked a pan lid to the floor. Every scrape carried through the shared wall. Elsie had never seen a grown man so careful with borrowed space. He lined his boots heel to wall at the mat, folded his towel to a square, and thanked for tea in the formal tone he used when signing for tools. She did not dislike him. She disliked that careful men made careless men look like weather you could no longer call accidental.

Noreen caught the sound too and looked toward the wall. "He's in, then."

"Arthur?"

"Aye. Peter won't be far behind, and if he asks where I've been I'll need a short answer."

Elsie heard the warning under the words. She had heard Peter's short answers before and the long corrections that followed.

"Then we do this now." Elsie pulled her housekeeping tin from the shelf above the range. The lid squealed when she opened it. Inside sat coins wrapped in paper, two pound notes folded to soft cloth, and envelopes labeled coal, milk, rent in her compact hand. She counted out loud because the room felt less likely to spin if her mouth kept moving.

"Rent envelope: five pounds, ten. Coal: one pound, four and six. Milk: twelve and three. Emergency: two pounds even." She stopped. "That's what we have in cash unless Tom's wages come Friday, and I don't trust Friday anymore."

Noreen leaned forward, elbows on knees. "You need to keep enough where he expects it. If he sees empty envelopes, he'll start tearing boards up."

"He already checks the cupboards." Elsie gave a humorless breath. "Started last week as if tins have legs."

"Then move only part. Quietly."

Elsie slid one pound note from emergency and folded it into the lining seam of her cardigan pocket. She tucked another beneath the wax paper under the dripping jar. They were old cleaner's habits, to hide things in plain mess.

"I hate doing this in my own kitchen," she said.

"I know."

"No, you don't. Not this exact shape."

Noreen did not flinch. "You're right. Not this exact shape."

That answer, honest and unsoftened, made Elsie trust her more than any comfort would have.

She looked back to the slips. "What do we do with these?"

"If they stay here, he can burn them."

"If they leave here and he notices, he'll know someone's seen."

"He should know someone has."

Elsie gave her a sharp look. "That's brave talk from the house next door. I'm the one in the bed with him."

Noreen gave a short bark of a laugh that died halfway. "Don't I know it."

The room held that line between them, hot and bright, then cooled.

Elsie pulled one betting slip free and turned it over. On the back Tom had written a shopping reminder in pencil: baccy, boot polish, razor blades. She stared at the words until they blurred.

"He wrote this while owing us six months of air," she said.

"For fuck's sake," Noreen said, loud enough to strike the sink tiles.

Elsie set the slip back in the pile and arranged the stack by date again. If she touched each edge square, she could think.

"I won't throw it at him tonight," she said. "No, that's wrong, I won't hand him the whole bundle while we're both shaking. He smells panic and spends it."

Noreen snapped her fingers once, like she had caught a rhythm. "Aye. No row till you've got footing."

"Tomorrow I'll go to the Post Office before he wakes, if the road isn't glass. I'll ask for every withdrawal and tell them nothing moves off that account unless I'm standing there. Can I make them hold to it?"

"If you get a decent clerk, yes. If you get one who smirks, ask for the supervisor and keep your voice sweet as tea till he writes it down. Don't give him a crack to talk over you."

"You've had to do this before, haven't you?"

"With Mam's rent book, not savings. My brother kept 'collecting' it and drinking it. Different queue, same men behind glass."

Elsie nodded. The confession gave her a new detail, a piece of Noreen she had not asked for and now needed.

"I'll take the dockets," Noreen said. "Leave the betting slips in your tin. One paper missing, he'll call himself careless. Whole stack gone, he'll turn the kitchen out."

"Not till we copy them. I don't move a penny blind."

Noreen held up both hands. "All right, accountant. How?"

"I'll copy by hand now while there's light. Dates, amounts, names, the lot. You keep the list at yours. If he burns these, we still have record."

Noreen looked at her with something like respect and something like sorrow. "There she is," she said. "House could come down and you'd still line up the figures straight."

"If I stop doing sums, I'll start screaming." Elsie met her eyes. "And if I start, I might tell him I want you more than I fear him, and that would ruin us both."

Noreen reached across the table and put her hand over Elsie's wrist, firm and warm.
The want hit Elsie like standing too close to the range. She wanted to turn her hand and hold on.
Fear hit harder. One wrong touch in the wrong doorway and Peter would have gossip to weaponize, Tom would call her unstable, and every paper in the tin would still carry her name.
She pulled her hand back, opened the exercise book, and squared the page.

"Then don't scream yet," she said. "Write."

Elsie fetched the school exercise book she used for shopping tallies and sat back down. Her fingers cramped by the third line, but she kept going.

She wrote each line in order: date, amount, initials, notes.

At the bottom of the page she wrote a second heading: Withdrawals not signed by me.

When she finished, the kitchen had darkened to the blue hour that made all corners look colder than they were. She lit the small lamp and watched the flame find itself.

Noreen read the copied page, folded it twice, and slid it into her brassiere, the only place Peter never searched because he believed decency stopped at cloth.

"I'll keep this dry," she said.

A fist hammered the front door, three blunt knocks that shook soot from the lintel. Both women went still.
Through the narrow hall, a man's voice carried: "Message for Harrow. Len says tonight, or he comes through this door at dawn."
Bootsteps scraped off the flags.

Noreen exhaled once. "Hayes has started sending runners to your step."
Elsie closed the exercise book. "Then we haven't got till morning."

Elsie gathered the originals and returned them to the tin under the sink, exactly where they had been, tea towels over top, door pushed almost closed but not fully latched. She wiped the wet patch on the boards so no mark remained.

"If he asks," she said, "you dropped the pan and swore and left."

"I did swear, to be fair."

"Not loud enough for the lane."

Noreen rose slowly, one hand on the table edge. "Els."

"What."

"This isn't just him breaking your heart. If Hayes sends a collector, he'll shout your name on this step like you're the one dodging payment. If the Post Office checks those dockets, it's your signature they'll call forgery. Don't let him sweet-talk you out of that."

Elsie held the back of her chair until her knuckles blanched. The words "I know" rose first, then "I've always known," and both curdled into lies before she could speak.

"I won't," she said.

Noreen picked up the blue saucepan, now only an alibi in enamel, and moved to the door. Elsie watched her carry out copied evidence and a secret that could cost Noreen her marriage and hand Tom one more way to call Elsie unfit. Before stepping out she turned back.

"If he comes in rough or starts clawing for those papers, don't stand and argue. Knock once on the wall, twice on the pipe, then come through my scullery with your coat. I'll hear."

"Even with Arthur there?"

"Especially with Arthur there. Better one sober witness than none if this turns into police business."

"That sounds like trust."

"It sounds like a man who writes days on his tea bags so nobody wastes one." Noreen's mouth twitched. "I'll trust that kind of daft over a grand speech."

Then she was gone into the lane, boots crunching on old ice.

Elsie stood in the center of her kitchen and listened until the sound vanished.

She looked at the cupboard door, swollen wood, flaking paint, rusted hinge that never sat true. She had cursed that door for two winters and meant to fix it each spring. It had stuck, then given, then emptied its mouth over her knees.

By the time Tom came home, the tea had stewed to bitterness and the lamp glass had filmed with soot. He shouldered the door hard enough to rattle the hook, cap askew, cheeks wind-burned, breath sweet with ale over stale smoke.

"Don't fucking start on me, Els," he said before she spoke. "I need three quid now. Len from Hayes was at the depot. He said if I don't settle tonight, he comes here at first light."

He reached for the housekeeping tin on the shelf. Elsie caught the lid with both hands and yanked it to her chest.

"No. That money buys coal and milk."

"For us, love. I'm doing this for us." He clamped his hand around her wrist and twisted. Pain flashed from her knuckles to her elbow, and her grip broke. The tin's rim skinned the base of her thumb when it knocked her hip and dropped; coins skittered under the table and rang against the range.
"Fuck off, Tom. Leave it."
She bent for the pound note, but he shoved her aside with his forearm. Her shoulder hit the dresser edge, and her breath went out in a blunt grunt. He snatched the note she had left in emergency and stuffed it into his coat.

Then his eyes dropped to the sink cupboard, to the door sitting wrong in its frame.

"Who was in there?"

"The latch stuck. Noreen helped with the pan."

He crouched, yanked the swollen door, and pulled out the packet of betting slips and dockets from under the tea towels. Elsie stepped forward, hand out.

"Leave those."

Tom tucked the papers inside his shirt. "Nobody sees these. Hear me? You go to the Post Office or start gossiping over walls and they'll say you signed every line, and I'll swear to it. Keep your mouth shut till I square it."

He was gone before she answered, boots striking the flags, back gate banging once against brick.

Elsie stood with her palm flat on the pipe under the sink, ready to tap Noreen's code at the first sound in the lane, feeling it hum with next door's heat.


# Chapter 9

Elsie had her palm on the sink pipe, ready to tap Noreen's code, when the lights went without warning, not a flicker first, not a dimming, just a hard click and then the room lost its edges.

Elsie stood with one hand inside the flour tin and the other on the table, as if the dark might tilt and throw her. The clock on the mantel stopped in the same instant, and for a breath she could hear only her own sleeve brushing her apron. Then the lane answered: a child crying two doors down, somebody swearing in the street, a pan dropped hard enough to ring through brick.

She waited for the hum behind the wall from the Walsh wireless, but that had gone too. She waited for Tom's tread on the back flags, a boot scraping the step, his key missing the lock twice before finding it. Nothing.

The cold moved quickly in a dead house. Without the little electric bar in the front room, without the kettle's thin steam, the damp in the plaster came awake. Elsie found the paraffin lamp by memory, struck two matches before one held, and turned the wick low to spare oil. Its light made a yellow pool that stopped at her elbows.

She crossed to the front window and scraped at the inside frost with her thumbnail. Streetlamps were out all along Barrow Lane. At the corner the sky had a dirty orange glow where the main road still had power, or maybe only firelight from people opening grates and burning whatever they had left. Snowmelt from last week's brief thaw had frozen again into a skin over the cobbles. If Tom was at the pub, he would stay there where there was heat. If Peter was at the council rooms, as Noreen had guessed after supper, he might stay there too.

Through the shared wall she heard a chair dragged, then another. Noreen, still up.

Elsie put on her coat over her house dress and took the scuttle to the coal box in the yard. There were three shovels left. She could call it four if she lied to herself and counted slate dust as fuel. She tipped half into the scuttle and left half where it was. She had done too much arithmetic these last weeks to pretend this night would solve itself.

A knock came from the side door, not loud, just knuckles through wood.

"Elsie?"

Noreen's voice was muffled by scarf and dark.

Elsie opened the door onto a strip of frozen yard. Noreen stood in Peter's old overcoat, unbuttoned across her middle, breath smoking around her face. She held a candle in a saucer and had wax dripped down her thumb to the knuckle.

"You all right?" Noreen asked. "I could hear you moving and then it went quiet."

"I'm upright," Elsie said. "That's about the lot of it."

Noreen scraped cooled wax from her thumb with her nail until the skin went red. "Arthur's out with the board van. Peter's not back. I hate saying this, but if this sits till dawn, two grates are daft. We'll burn through both houses before midnight."

Elsie looked past her into the Walsh scullery, where another candle shook against tile. "Your front grate draws better."

"It does. Come in with what you've got. Bring bedding. We'll keep one fire and keep the pipes from bursting in at least one place."

They traded logistics in clipped turns, each answer landing before either of them had to name what it cost.

Elsie shifted the scuttle higher on her hip. "Give me ten minutes."

"Take five," Noreen said. "My toes are numb already."

Elsie packed in a hard sequence, counting under her breath as she moved. Two blankets, Tom's army-surplus one and the thin flowered one from her mother. A flat pillow. The tin with tea. Half a loaf wrapped in last Sunday's paper. She hesitated over the little packet of sugar, then took it too. On the dresser the photograph from their wedding sat in its cheap frame, Tom's grin broad, her own face careful as if she already knew cameras took more than likeness. She turned the frame down without deciding to.

When she stepped back into the yard with her load, Noreen was waiting by the shared gate with a hot-water bottle tucked under her arm and a rolled mattress dragging behind her.

"Christ," Noreen said softly, seeing the weight Elsie carried. "Here, give us that." She took the tea tin and bread, then the blanket bundle when Elsie shifted awkwardly around her belly. "You don't get points for martyrdom."

"No one has ever accused me of that."

"I've done it, at least twice." Noreen's mouth twitched. "Come on."

They built a camp in the Walsh front room like children in a game, except children did not count coal by handfuls. Noreen knelt by the grate, coaxing flame through cinders with twisted-up account circulars Peter had left on the sideboard. Elsie laid blankets over the settee and floor rug, wedged rolled towels along the bottom of the front door, and pushed a folded sheet against the letter slot to stop the draft. Each task made another corner of fear manageable.

The room took shape in lamp glow: the framed Sacred Heart print above the mantel, Peter's ledgers stacked with string tied precise around them, a cup with three knitting needles and no wool. Noreen's cardigan hung over the chair back, one cuff dark where she'd spilled tea yesterday and not had enough soap to scrub it through. Elsie noticed everything because not looking at Noreen's face was harder than looking.

When the fire found its breath, the coals settled with a sound like dry bones tapping together. Heat rose slowly, mean and precious.

Noreen sat back on her heels and pressed a hand to her lower spine. "There. If it dies now I'll throw Peter's records on top and let him call it civic sabotage."

"I'd pay to see it."

"With what money?"

"Exactly."

The joke landed and did not fix anything, but the air shifted.

Elsie poured water from Noreen's kettle into two chipped mugs and tipped in a pinch of tea leaves so small it looked insulting. They drank anyway. The tannin tasted of pennies and comfort.

Outside, wind shoved at the windows in uneven blows, then dropped. Down the lane somebody sang one line of a hymn and forgot the rest. The houses answered with coughs, a baby waking, the hard scrape of someone feeding a stove. Through it all the wall to her own house stayed silent, and that silence was worse than shouting.

"He'll not be back tonight," Elsie said, before she could stop herself.

Noreen did not ask which he. "No." She stared into the fire. "Peter left after tea with his satchel and his virtue. Said the council had emergency ledgers to balance at the hall and the city would collapse if he did not attend."

"Did you believe him?"

"I believed he wanted a warm room where no one asked him for kindness."

Elsie let that sit. She could hear the small crackle where a seam in the coal opened. "Tom said he was going to look for paraffin."

Noreen snorted. "Aye. In a pint glass."

"Likely."

Noreen leaned her head against the settee, eyes half closed. Candlelight made the hollows under her cheekbones look deeper. "I keep thinking if I can just get through one full night without something breaking, I'd count it as luxury."

Elsie pulled the blanket higher over Noreen's knees. "Then that's the objective, isn't it. Full night. No panic. No broken pipes, no broken bones, no ambulance."

"No husbands," Noreen said.

Elsie met her eyes then and looked away first.

Heat management took over the room. Elsie went across to her own house for the second lamp and came back fast because the empty rooms had a different cold now, the cold of abandoned places. In the scullery doorway, Noreen braced one hand under her belly and kept the other on the pipe, listening after each strike while Elsie banged metal with a spoon handle until the vibration ran clean through the wall. Near midnight, they spread the mattress on the floor close enough to the hearth to feel heat on their shins and cool on their shoulders.

Noreen brought down extra blankets from the spare room and a faded eiderdown that smelled faintly of lavender gone stale in the chest. "Arthur won't miss this one," she said. "He says it scratches his chin."

Elsie pictured him once, careful with his boots by the Walsh door, always tying the laces in a square knot as if his fingers had no other shape. "He's particular."

"He folds his socks into pairs like little sandwiches. It alarms me."

The detail should have been funny. It was funny. Still, the room felt too near and too narrow for easy laughter.

Noreen stood with the eiderdown clutched in both hands, suddenly solemn. "Elsie."

"Mm?"

"If your house drops colder than this one before dawn, you stay here. Not in the chair. In the bed. I'm not arguing in this weather."

Elsie opened her mouth to refuse on instinct, and Noreen lifted an eyebrow in that way she had when she knew refusal was pride dressed up as duty.

"In the bed," Noreen repeated. "We're both carrying. I'm not having you wake stiff and scared because you're trying to be proper for furniture."

"It's your house."

"It's a mattress on my floor and my husband is absent in service of municipal paperwork. I'd say propriety has had a bad week."

Elsie's answer came out rougher than she meant. "Don't joke me into it."

"I'm not joking." Noreen's voice dropped. "I'm asking. Stay where it's warm."

A coal popped and sent a fan of sparks against the guard. They both flinched, then both looked at each other and had to smile at the same time.

"All right," Elsie said quietly. "I stay."

Noreen held her gaze, then exhaled through her nose like a bargain struck.

Practicality was the story their hands told while they undressed in increments. Shoes first, then wet stockings hung by the grate. Elsie kept her slip and cardigan on, then took the cardigan off when the damp wool chilled her more than bare skin did. Noreen changed out of Peter's coat and into a thin nightdress with blue stitching at the cuffs, the hem mended in two places with darker thread. She rubbed her hands together briskly, blew into her fists, and slid under the blankets with a grunt.

"God save us from heroic women," she muttered. "Come on before I freeze to the rug."

Elsie lowered herself carefully beside her. The mattress dipped and made them roll together a fraction. Their hips touched, separated, touched again when Noreen shifted the hot-water bottle between them.

"Too hot?" Noreen asked.

"No, leave it there. I've got feeling back in my feet."

"Too close?"

Elsie swallowed. "Close, yes. Too close, no."

The fire snapped, settled. The room smelled of coal, paraffin, damp wool, and peppermint soap from the Christmas bar her mother had wrapped in tissue and called a luxury. Wind pressed a low note through the chimney and then released it.

* * *

Noreen kept her breathing slow and even and failed to fool herself. Sleep would not come. She could feel Elsie wakeful beside her by the way the mattress held tension, by the tiny adjustments that never settled into rest.

She had spent years learning how to read silence in a house where words got used as ledgers. This silence was not resentment, not fear exactly. It had heat in it.

Noreen spoke into the dark before she could lose nerve. "You awake?"

"Yes."

"So am I."

The answer sat between them, heavy and alive.
