Noreen rolled onto her side to face Elsie. In lamplight Elsie's eyes looked darker than usual, wide and guarded like someone waiting for a verdict. Noreen could not bear the thought of guessing wrong and harming what she wanted most.

"I need to say something before my courage packs up and goes home," she said.

Elsie stayed still, listening.

"If my hand lands wrong, you tell me plain. Now, in a minute, halfway through, whenever. I stop dead, and you don't owe me pretty words."

Fear flickered across Elsie's face, but she did not move away.

"I want you to," Elsie said.

Noreen forced herself not to rush, even as relief hit like drink on an empty stomach. "Because you're cold?"

"I'm cold."

"Because you're lonely?"

Elsie gave a sharp, almost angry little huff. "Yes."

"Because you choose it?"

Elsie held her gaze. "Yes. And because if I don't choose one thing that is mine tonight, I'll go mad."

The words went straight under Noreen's ribs. She pressed her lips together, then said, "All right."

She laid her hand on Elsie's sleeve first, over cloth, and left it there. Elsie closed the distance on her own, pressing her forehead to Noreen's mouth before turning and kissing her properly.

The first kiss was cautious, lips dry and careful. The second came with need. Noreen slid her fingers into the hair at Elsie's nape and felt Elsie shiver.

"Tell me if anything hurts," Noreen whispered.

"You too."

The rest of the room thinned to heat and breath as they kissed. Noreen felt the seam of Elsie's slip twisted under her palm and smoothed it. She pressed her hand to Elsie's lower back, then slid up under the slip to cup her breast, thumb rough over the nipple until it tightened. When her fingers moved lower, skimming the damp curl between Elsie's thighs, she stopped and waited.

"Yes," Elsie said, already reaching for her.

A breathless laugh broke out of Noreen, half disbelief, half surrender. "Right, then."

Buttons refused fingers gone clumsy with cold and urgency. Elsie fumbled at Noreen's nightdress, and Noreen kissed her wrist and did the last buttons herself. Air hit skin like cold water, then blankets trapped heat back around them.

Noreen touched Elsie with full attention, slow enough to keep asking and be answered without words. Stretch marks, taut skin, the curve and weight of her belly. She pressed her ear against it and grinned when the baby kicked her cheek.

"Bossy already," she murmured.

Elsie laughed, then made a softer sound when Noreen kissed the scar beneath her breast, the one Elsie once called ugly and then pretended she had not said anything at all.

Noreen felt Elsie's hand on her shoulder, then sliding down her thigh under the nightdress. Elsie's fingers parted her, thumb circling her clit before one finger pressed in. She opened for her and heard herself inhale like she had been running.

"Like that," she said, voice gone thin. "Don't stop because you think you should."

Elsie did not stop.

Pleasure rose hard and immediate, sharpened by fear of being overheard through the thin terrace wall. Noreen bit the heel of her own hand when a sound tried to break loose, then reached back one-handed to feed the fire and came straight back to Elsie without losing the rhythm.

They shifted positions twice, first too cramped and then side-on with Elsie's top knee propped on a folded blanket so belly and hips had room. Noreen cupped Elsie's face.

"This all right?"

"Yes. Better."

"Say it if it changes."

"I'll tell you if it changes."

Noreen's control broke into prayer and profanity in one breath when Elsie's thumb kept a hard rhythm over her clit and her fingers curled inside. "Sweet Mary, Elsie, right there. Christ."

Elsie kissed her to quiet her, and Noreen bit Elsie's lower lip and did not apologize. Their movements stayed imperfect, cramped by blankets and the little geography of the front room, but every pause was answered and every question got spoken.

"Here?"

"Yes, there. Don't lose it."

"Still?"

"Still. Keep that pressure."

"More?"

"More, but steady."

Then Elsie went rigid with a sudden low cramp. Noreen stopped instantly, hand flat over Elsie's abdomen.

"Pain?"

"Just a pull," Elsie said through her teeth. "Too fast, that's all."

Noreen counted softly under her breath in Irish, thumb drawing circles near Elsie's hip until the muscle released.

"We go slow," she said.

"We're going slow."

"You know what I mean."

Elsie blinked hard and nodded. "I know."

When Elsie came, it took her whole body with it. Her thighs locked around Noreen's wrist and her left hand seized Noreen's shoulder hard enough to sting. She bit the blanket to keep from crying out into the wall. Noreen watched, stunned by her and by the fact of being allowed this close. Her own release followed moments later, sudden enough to steal speech, rocking against Elsie's thigh with her face buried at Elsie's neck while the tremor ran through her.

When Elsie let go, four red crescents had already risen on Noreen's shoulder.

Afterward they lay sweating in a room that still needed coal to stay survivable. Their breathing settled unevenly. Noreen felt both babies moving, protesting the commotion in their own blunt language.

She laughed weakly into the pillow. "If Sister Agnes could see me now she'd set the convent on fire herself."

Elsie kissed the corner of her mouth. "She'd blame me."

"She'd be right in part."

"Only in part."

Noreen laced their fingers. The last of the joke drained out of her. "You still with me?"

"Yes."

"Any regret?"

Elsie stared up at the smoke-marked ceiling for a long beat. "No. Fear, yes. Regret, no."

Noreen shut her eyes, and a tear leaked sideways into her hairline. "Same."

The room had cooled while they were occupied. Duty came back in pieces. Elsie dragged herself up, tucked the blanket around Noreen's shoulders, and fed the grate twice. Noreen reached for the kettle and poured water into the hot-water bottle again, then tucked it at Elsie's feet with a muttered, "Your toes are like stones."

"You've checked that three times."

"I'll check six if it keeps you attached to your legs."

When they settled again, they faced each other with their knees drawn up, hands under each other's cardigans to share warmth.

Outside, around half two by guess, voices rose at the lane end, men arguing over a stalled lorry. Someone banged metal on metal. Then distance swallowed it and left only wind.

Elsie spoke first, because if she waited until daylight she might start lying to herself. "This wasn't the weather. I chose you."

Noreen searched her face, then nodded once.

Elsie swallowed. "If Peter or Tom starts asking, they get weather, pipes, and coal. That's all that leaves these bricks."

"Right," Noreen said. "Burst pipes and bad weather. Nothing for the confessional."

"And if either house goes sour, we knock once and cross the wall. We don't wait to be let."

Noreen squeezed her hand and reached for the stub of pencil Peter used for accounts.

She tore a strip from a circular, wrote in blocky letters by lamp glow, and held it out.

BANK FIRE AT FOUR. CHECK BOTH PIPES AT FIRST LIGHT. BREAKFAST IN ONE KITCHEN.

Elsie read it twice and gave a breath that almost counted as a laugh. "You made a rota."

"I panic better on paper."

She folded the note and tucked it into her bra. "I'm not vanishing when daylight comes."

"Neither am I."

The lamp burned lower. They dozed in short drops, waking whenever the fire needed feeding or a pipe groaned. Elsie dreamed once of walking through her own house and finding every drawer open, every spoon gone, and woke with her hand clenched in Noreen's nightdress.

Near four, Noreen rose to use the chamber pot behind the screen and came back swearing under her breath because her ankles had swollen against the elastic of her stockings. She sat on the mattress and massaged them, jaw set.

"Don't look," she said.

"At what?"

"At me with those nurse eyes."

Elsie moved closer anyway and took one foot into her lap, thumb pressing gently at the indentation above the sock line. "You should rest with them up."

"I should have won the football pools and moved to Brighton."

"That too."

Noreen watched her for a moment, then smiled tiredly. "You're doing it again."

"Doing what?"

Noreen caught Elsie's wrist before she could press another dent into swollen skin. "Turning me into a project."

Elsie let out a breath through her nose and loosened her grip. "Sorry."

"Don't be sorry. Be here."

She pulled Elsie down beside her and kissed her slowly this time, no hunger now, only claim and reassurance.

The dark thinned but did not break. Still no electricity. The fire had eaten most of what coal they had dared give it. Frost traced the inside of the window again where their breath had not reached.

Elsie sat up, yanked her shift straight twice, and started issuing instructions at the room. "Bank the grate now. One mug between us. Cut the loaf thin. Keep the wick under a thumbnail." The words ran out. She stared at the scuttle with her jaw locked and said nothing.

Noreen swung her legs down, tipped the coal scuttle to hear the thin rattle, then checked the lamp reservoir against her thumbnail. "Half's generous."

"Then call it less," Elsie said.

They made tea from yesterday's leaves boiled twice and stood in the kitchen doorway sharing the mug because both had cold hands and one cup held heat better than two thin ones. The yard beyond was a white crust over black ice. No footprints yet from the lane.

When the mug was nearly empty, Noreen glanced at the grate, then at the coal scuttle. "If we keep this fed till light, we'll have nothing left for breakfast."

Elsie nodded, set the mug down, and rubbed her palms together until they stung.

"We bank it now and take the back room. Bed keeps heat better than this floor."

Elsie hesitated just long enough to make the choice again. "All right."

With the lamp wick pinched low and the coals banked to a dull red, they carried blankets upstairs one armful at a time. The back room smelled of old lavender and damp plaster. The iron bedframe complained when they climbed in, but it held warmth better than the mattress by the grate. Noreen passed Elsie the mug with the last cold inch of tea in it, and Elsie set it beside the bed leg without drinking.

Noreen turned onto her side toward the window and caught Elsie's wrist under the blanket. "First light, one kitchen, remember."

"One kitchen," Elsie said.

The house settled around them. Elsie shifted once when the iron bit her left hip, then held still. Somewhere down the landing a floorboard gave one long complaint, and both women held their breath with their fingers tightening around each other's wrists until the noise passed.


# Chapter 10

In Noreen's back room, Elsie woke before the light had chosen a shape with her fingers still locked around Noreen's wrist from the night. The room was all one color, a thin iron gray, and the blanket over her had gone damp where two breaths had soaked it through. Her left hip hurt from the bed frame. Her mouth tasted of ash and salt and the tannin of tea gone cold in a cup she had never finished.

Noreen lay on her side facing the window, knees pulled up, one hand under her cheek. In the half-dark she looked younger and more tired at the same time. Her hair had come loose from its pins in the night, black against the pillow, and there was a red mark at her shoulder where Elsie had held her too hard and not let go. Elsie knew the exact shape of that mark because she had put it there with her own hand, with her own hunger. She had not been drunk. She had not mistaken a body for a blanket. She had known what she was doing each second of it.

The floorboard outside the room gave one long complaint. Then came a cough from down the landing, a man's cough trying not to be loud. Arthur. Billeted men learned to cough politely, as if lungs could be taught manners.

Noreen opened her eyes.

"He's up," Elsie whispered.

"I heard." Noreen's voice was rough from sleep. She did not pull away. She looked straight at Elsie. Her gaze dipped to Elsie's mouth and snapped back to her eyes. "You're shaking. That's cold, not regret, yeah?"

Elsie put her hand under the blanket and found Noreen's wrist. The skin was warm, pulse quick. "Both," she said, because anything else would have sounded like acting.

Noreen gave a breath that was almost a laugh, almost not. "Fair enough. Come on, then. We need the grate lit before he clocks we were dawdling." 

They got up in a silence full of small accidents: a sleeve inside out, Elsie's stocking twisted at the ankle, Noreen hissing when she bent to pick up her slip and her back pulled. Elsie saw the bite mark she had left on Noreen's collarbone and had to look away to knot her apron string properly. She tucked the sheet hard under the mattress, smoothing the middle where their weight had left a trough. Noreen flipped the top pillow and turned the lower one so both looked slept in from one side.

"Settle downstairs," Noreen said, practical now, fastening her cardigan at the wrong button first and fixing it fast. "If he asks, you took the settle after the fire went." 

"If he asks, I'll say I snored and kept you up," Elsie said.

"You did not snore. You did other things louder." Noreen's eyes flicked up, wicked and tired together. "Don't go pink now. We haven't got time." 

A kettle lid clinked in the kitchen below. Arthur moved like a careful lodger, never slamming, never claiming noise. Even so, every sound came through the house because the terrace walls carried and the cold made timber talk.

Noreen took one step toward the door and stopped with her hand on her belly. Her face changed. She swallowed twice.

"Bucket," she said.

Elsie got the enamel bucket under her just before Noreen retched. There was not much in her stomach, only sour water and yesterday's potato. Noreen spat, wiped her mouth with the back of her wrist, and said, "Well. Romance." 

Elsie rinsed the bucket with the little water left in the wash jug. "You've not kept breakfast down three mornings this week. That's not romance, either." 

"I know what it is. It's this child making a nuisance before it's got lungs." Noreen leaned against the dresser and closed her eyes for a second. "You all right?"

The question landed in Elsie like a hand pressed to a bruise. She nodded, then shook her head. "I don't know what to call it yet." 

"Don't call it a mistake. That's all I'm asking this minute." Noreen opened her eyes again. She was fully awake now, that quick brightness she got when she decided on a line and stood on it. "You can call it madness, bad timing, sin if it helps you argue with God. But don't tell me we slipped. We didn't slip. We climbed into it." 

Elsie looked at the frozen corner of the window where ferned ice had grown overnight on the inside glass. "I won't call it slipping."

"Good. Now move. If Arthur gets to the scullery first he'll see the lamp oil on your hem and ask why you were kneeling on my floor at two in the morning." 

They went downstairs together, not touching. In the kitchen the range was dead, ash white and soft. Arthur stood by the table with his sleeves rolled and his braces hanging, rubbing goose fat into his knuckles from a tobacco tin before he pulled on his gloves. The cracks across his fingers were deep enough to shine pink.

"Morning, Mrs Harrow," he said, nodding at Elsie with the careful courtesy of a man who slept in strangers' houses for a living. "You made it through then? Thought your side had gone black about midnight." 

"It did," Noreen answered before Elsie could, crossing to the range with the poker. "She came over when her fire dropped. Slept on the settle wrapped like a parcel. Look at her, still blue." 

Arthur checked Elsie's face, took in the dead grate, and seemed to make whatever ledger he needed. "Right thing to do. Board says we're on staggered cuts again tonight. I'll be out by five if roads hold." He rubbed his hands once, sharp. "I've left half my bread in the tin if either of you run short at dinner. It's stale enough to tile a roof, but it soaks." 

That was Arthur: offers instead of opinions. He said it as if leaving bread was a minor clerical adjustment, not kindness.

"Keep your bread," Elsie said. "We'll manage." 

"You'll do no such thing," Noreen said. "You'll keep your bread and we'll keep ours and everyone'll pretend this is normal. Sit down a minute and warm your hands. Your fingertips look like candles." 

Arthur did not sit. He stood with his mug and watched steam choose the least cracked path up the kitchen air. "Pipe outside's got a skin of ice," he said. "I can break it before I go. Save you wrestling it." 

"Please," Noreen said, and meant it.

Elsie knelt by the grate to lay paper, slack coal dust, two small lumps, one larger at the back. Her fingers worked by habit while her mind snagged on scraps of the night: Noreen's breath caught against her neck, the way her own name sounded in Noreen's mouth when she was close to sobbing and close to laughing at once. She pushed a match under the paper and watched flame take reluctantly, then commit.

Arthur drank fast and set the mug down. "If Mr Walsh asks, tell him I checked your outside pipe at six and Mrs Harrow crossed for heat. Better he hears one story than invents his own." 

He pulled a bent queen of hearts from his pocket, flattened it once with his thumb, and tucked it back. "My little girl called this the liar's queen. Keep your story matching and you'll be left in peace." 

"Right. Outside pipe." 

The back door shut. Cold came in around it anyway.

Noreen moved beside Elsie at the grate and fed in two more pieces of coal. Their sleeves brushed. Elsie felt heat in the place between her shoulder blades that had nothing to do with fire.

"He'll keep his own counsel," Noreen murmured.

"Maybe. People keep counsel until there's something to buy with it." 

"You're not wrong." Noreen crouched slowly, one hand braced on her knee. "You need to go next door before anyone sees your boots still here. Take the potato sack and my ash bucket. Looks like trade, not trouble." 

"You've got enough potatoes as it is." 

"You've got less. And don't start noble nonsense before tea." 

Elsie took the sack. It was heavier than it looked, dirt still on the skins. Noreen passed her the ash bucket and, under cover of the bucket handle, pressed two fingers hard into Elsie's palm, then let go.

"Tonight?" Noreen asked, barely sound.

Elsie stared at the blackening paper in the grate. "I don't know if tonight's safe."

"I asked if tonight. Not if safe." 

Elsie lifted her eyes. "We'll talk this afternoon. Properly. Terms." 

Noreen's mouth tightened, then eased. "Terms, then. Go on." 

Elsie crossed the yard with ash and potatoes and the small private ache of leaving a warm room for one where nobody had breathed beside her in weeks. Frost squeaked under her soles. Her own back door stuck; she had to shoulder it twice. Inside, Harrow House held the kind of cold that had edges. The sink had iced in a crescent where yesterday's rinse water sat. Tom's coat was not on the hook. His boots were still gone.

Tom was still gone, and there was no explanation and no relief either.

She set the potatoes in the pantry, emptied ash to the metal pail, and began morning inventory aloud because saying numbers pinned her mind down. "Coal: eleven full scuttles if careful. Paraffin: three quarters of a bottle. Oats: two and a half jars. Dripping: one crock. Bread: one loaf if no mold." She pulled the loaf from its cloth. One corner had gone green overnight. She cut it away and fed the spoiled bit to the stove.

From next door came the thud of Arthur's boot against the outside tap and his muttered, "Come on, you stubborn bastard," to the pipe. A man swore at metal differently than at people, with less performance and more patience.

Elsie scrubbed the settle blanket where her knees had left grime in case anyone checked Noreen's story against this room. She arranged the cushion with the faded rose print and put her own scarf over the arm. She hated that she thought in evidence now. She hated more that she was good at it.

A knock came on the shared wall: once, pause, once. Noreen's rhythm.

Elsie knocked back with the poker handle.

Noreen's voice came through plaster, muffled but clear enough if you knew the tune of it. "You lit yours?"

"Just now." 

"Arthur got the pipe free. He's nicked one of my good spoons to lever ice." 

"He can keep it if it keeps water running." 

Silence, then Noreen again, softer. "I meant what I said upstairs." 

Elsie leaned her forehead against the wall, paint cold as tin. "So did I." 

She heard Noreen move away; boards creaked under her weight. A moment later the kettle whistle rose and fell in two houses at once like an argument and an answer.

By ten the lane had woken into its freeze-day habits. Shovels scraped. A pram wheel squealed somewhere down by the corner. Mrs Lilley shouted at one of her twins for licking an icicle off the drainpipe. Elsie took in coal, shook out a rug, peeled potatoes for broth. After that she carried two jars of rendered fat and a strip of bacon rind next door in plain sight. They moved around each other in Noreen's kitchen with the ease of women who had worked side by side for months, only now every accidental brush had a second meaning under it.

No one would have called it dramatic. That was the trouble. A life could split without fanfare, right in the middle of peeling vegetables.

---

By afternoon the sky had gone the color of dishwater and stayed there. Elsie stood in her scullery with both sleeves rolled above her elbows, washing nappies in water she had boiled twice and still did not trust. Soap grated under her nails. The line in the yard was stiff as wire. When she pegged cloth up it froze into shape before she reached for the next one.

Noreen rapped at the back door and came in carrying the blue enamel basin between her hip and forearm.

"Can I steal your dolly peg? Mine's split," she said, then shut the door behind her with her heel and lowered her voice. "Peter's still not back. Arthur's gone to the substation. We've got half an hour." 

Elsie wiped her hands on her apron. "I said we'd talk."

"Then talk." Noreen set the basin down. Her cheeks were wind-burned from hanging wash and her hairline was damp with sweat. Pregnancy made every chore two chores. "Don't make me guess what face you'll wear tonight." 

Elsie glanced at the window. The frost she had scraped from the pane an hour ago had grown back in white veins. "In here?"

"No. Yard. If anyone hears, it'll sound like washing talk." 

They went out to the strip of backyard between privy and coal shed where the wall blocked half the wind. Elsie could smell damp brick, smoke, and the sharp ammonia of old snow where someone had let chamber water out too near the door.

Noreen pulled a basket of clothespins close and reached across it to catch Elsie's chapped hand. Her thumb dragged over a split knuckle and Elsie hissed. Noreen bent and kissed that knuckle once, quick and hard, before she let go.

"All right," Noreen said. "Terms. You first. You go listy when you're scared." 

Elsie pulled her hand back under her apron and looked at the wall where Mrs Lilley's line ran above the bricks. "Term one: no touching in open yard. Not by this wall, not by any window. One child on a milk crate sees us and we're finished." 

"Fine. Hands decent in public, sinners in private." Noreen tilted her head. "Next." 

Elsie rubbed the sting out of her knuckle against rough cotton. "Both houses still run. If your fire drops, I cross with coal. If mine goes, you bang the wall. We still show where we're due. Last night doesn't buy us a holiday." 

"Agreed. Fires first, faces straight." Noreen sorted pegs into neat little ranks and snapped a warped one clean in two. "What else?"

"Babies come first, always. If either pregnancy turns bad, pride can go hang. We call Keane, we call each other, and if the men object they can object while we walk past them." 

Noreen nodded once. "Yes. No heroics." 

Elsie swallowed. This was the line that would either keep her alive or ruin her. "And we stop calling last night a slip." 

Noreen closed her fist around the broken peg until stove polish marked her palm. She looked up slowly. "Good. Keep going. Plain, Elsie, not chapel." 

"I'm trying." Elsie took a breath that hurt her ribs. "I wanted you. I still do. That's true. Also true, if this lane gets a smell of it, we're finished. Peter'll call it duty and pin you in with a prayer book and a ledger. Tom'll sell the story for beer money if it clears one debt. So outside, we're neighbors. Inside, we keep our heads and we don't get careless near windows." 

Noreen held her gaze without blinking. "I know what they'll do. I live with one of them." She shook her head once. "But Christ, Elsie, you can turn wanting into pantry sums." 

"Pantry ledgers tell me who's fed by morning," Elsie said.

A half-smile pulled at Noreen's mouth. It dropped before it could settle. "You left out one term. Mine." 

"Go on." 

"Don't come to me only when you're freezing or frightened and then button yourself up by breakfast. I can't be your emergency stove." She tapped two fingers against Elsie's breastbone, right over the hammering beat there. Her voice roughened. "If we're doing this, we're doing the waiting too. The dull bits, the scared bits, all of it. Not just the parts that burn." 

Elsie felt the back wall at her spine, cold through wool. "I don't know how to do all of it. I know how to keep us fed and out of sight." 

"Neither do I. I'm not backing out." 

From two gardens down came the squeal of a gate and a child singing off-key about oranges. Both women turned their heads by reflex. When the singing skipped into a shout, they looked back at each other.

Elsie said, "All right. We don't lie to each other. We don't promise what we can't pay. If one of us needs space, she says it instead of vanishing." 

"Better." Noreen dug in her apron pocket and brought out three clothespins painted black at the tips with stove polish. "Now don't roll your eyes at me. I made code." 

Elsie stared. "You've already made code."

"I was awake at dawn, wasn't I?" Noreen held one peg up between finger and thumb. "One black peg on my line, left side by the drainpipe, means I need you after dark. No knocking till the top room curtain's shut. Two black pegs means don't come near, house is unsafe. If you need me, same on yours." 

Elsie reached for the peg. The polish smudged her fingertip. "And if someone asks why black pegs?"

"We say I ran out of proper ones and Arthur gave me these from depot stores. Men are always hauling home ugly bits of something and calling it useful." Noreen shrugged. "It's stupid. That's why it works."

Elsie turned the peg over, feeling the spring bite. "We need a spoken one too. For when we're at the front and everyone's looking." 

Noreen thought for a beat. "'Blue soap.' I ask, 'Have you got blue soap?' and it means I need you alone. You answer, 'Used the last of it,' and I know it's not safe yet. You answer, 'I've a sliver left,' and it means yes, later." 

"Is that too clever?"

"Maybe. But we're women on a lane talking laundry. We could run a whole conspiracy over nappies and no one would hear it." 

"Fine. One black peg for tonight, two for danger. Blue soap for words." 

Elsie put the peg in her apron pocket like it was hot.

Noreen leaned back against the wall and shut her eyes for a second. "There. Domestic espionage. My mother would cross herself, then ask for updates." 

"Mine would pray till her knees gave out." 

Noreen opened her eyes. "Do you want to pray?"

Elsie surprised herself by answering fast. "No. I want both babies breathing by spring, and I want us still speaking when we get there." 

"Amen, then." 

They stood with the basin between them and did not touch. The wanting sat in the yard like another person, patient, inconvenient, impossible to send home.

Mrs Lilley's voice cracked over the wall. "Nora? You got any bicarbonate? Little Alfie swallowed snow with soot in it and he's sick as a dog." 

Noreen's face changed in an instant to neighbor-bright. "Coming, love!" she shouted back. To Elsie, quieter: "See? No one lets you brood on this lane." 

She grabbed two nappies from the basket and walked to the fence with the easy sway she used in public, half flirt, half harmlessness. "I've got some, aye. Tell him if he licks gutter ice again I'll box his ears myself." 

Elsie took that cue and lifted the basin, headed for her own back door, and nearly collided with Peter Walsh coming through his gate from the alley.

He wore his office coat though it was Saturday, collar buttoned, hat rim rimed with sleet. His shoes were city shoes, wrong for this lane, thin leather already whitening at the seams. He had a packet of papers under his arm tied with green tape and a fold-out umbrella snapped at one rib.

"Mrs Harrow," he said, tipping his head exactly enough. He did not smile. "You are industrious." 

Peter always sounded as if he were dictating minutes to an invisible clerk.

"Trying to stay ahead of it," Elsie said, shifting the basin on her hip.

"Quite." His gaze moved from her face to the basin to Noreen by the fence and back again, calculating. "Noreen informs me there was a power failure of uncommon duration. I gather you sheltered here." 

Elsie kept her mouth neutral. "My grate died before midnight. Noreen had enough coal to keep one room warm, so I came across."

"Sensible in extremis." Peter's glove creaked as he tightened his hand around the paper packet. "I prefer prior notice when my household acquires overnight guests, but weather is untidy by nature." 

Noreen turned from the fence and came toward them carrying a little orange biscuit tin of bicarbonate for Mrs Lilley. "Peter, don't start accountancy with breathing people," she said, light as butter over hot bread. "Elsie would've frozen next door. You'd have done the same if it'd been Mr Hale with no heat." 

Peter looked at her for one long beat. His gaze dropped to the tin in her hand. "You are dispensing our supplies again." 

"Half a spoon for a child. We're not financing a revolution." 

His jaw moved. "You are always amusing when you are refusing instruction." 

Anger flared in Elsie, clean and dangerous. She tamped it flat under practicality. "I'll send over a pinch from mine tonight," she said.

Peter inclined his head. "Unnecessary. We are not destitute." 

Noreen's laughter thinned just a fraction. "No one said we were." 

He stepped aside to let Elsie pass but did not take his eyes off her until she had crossed into her yard. She could feel them between her shoulder blades like cold nails.

Inside her kitchen she set the basin down and braced both palms on the table edge until the tremor in her wrists eased enough to lift a kettle. Through the wall came muffled voices: Peter's measured baritone, Noreen's quicker answers, then a scrape at the dresser and the dry click of wood on wood.

"What is this black grease on the pegs?" Peter asked, every word neat.

"Stove polish. I marked the split ones."

"You will not mark household items like dock tags. Give them here."

A drawer thudded shut in the Walsh kitchen. The coded pegs were gone before dark.

Elsie peeled turnips for stew and nicked her thumb. Bright blood welled and looked black in the low light. She sucked it clean, wrapped it in rag, and went on. She kept the pot moving so it would not catch, banked the fire before it died, and stitched the hem she had torn on the coal scuttle. Frost furred the pane again. Every few minutes she paused mid-wring with a dish cloth twisted tight and bent her ear toward the wall, then forced herself back to work.

At dusk she took washing from the line with numb fingers and brought the pegs in by handfuls. Mostly plain wood, swollen from wet. One black-tipped peg from her own basket fell into her palm and left a dark stripe across her skin.

On Noreen's line, left side by the drainpipe, there was nothing but bare cord. Through the Walsh parlor curtain, Peter's shadow crossed once, then again, angular and slow.

She should have waited for daylight and kept to the terms she had set. Instead she slid the black peg into her apron pocket, poured tea into a chipped mug, and crossed the yard.

Noreen opened the scullery door on the second tap and pulled Elsie in by the sleeve. The room smelled of boiled cabbage and paraffin. Heat from the range had not reached the stone floor; cold climbed through Elsie's soles.

"He took all three," Noreen whispered. "Counted them like coins and put them in his desk. No more line code while he's in the house."

"Then tonight's gone," Elsie said. "No wall knocks, no back-door runs. We reset in daylight." 

"Tonight's gone," Noreen repeated, then caught Elsie's wrist and guided her hand under her cardigan to the hard warm curve of her belly. "Feel that? She kicks every time he starts on ledgers."

The kick landed once against Elsie's palm, sudden and intimate enough to hitch her breath. She bent and pressed her mouth to Noreen's knuckles, tasting soap and salt.

A floorboard in the passage gave a sharp crack.

"Noreen?" Peter called. "Who is in there?"

Noreen moved first. She shoved a chipped blue soap bar into Elsie's hand and raised her voice. "Mrs Harrow's run out. I said she could have a sliver."

Elsie forced her own voice steady. "Sorry to bother. Mine went soft by the stove."

Peter appeared in the doorway with the hall lamp held high, his glasses flashing. His gaze landed on Elsie's face, then the two women standing too close to the sink.

"You will ask in daylight next time," he said. "This house is not a corridor." 

Noreen smiled without warmth. "Then keep the roads passable and I'll keep perfect hours." 

His mouth tightened. "From this evening, the back gate is bolted at six. If Mrs Harrow requires anything, she asks at the front door and in daylight. I will answer." 

"You're posting office hours on my kitchen now?" Noreen said.

"I am setting order," Peter said. "Do not be insolent."

Elsie went out with the soap biting damp into her palm. In her yard she turned once. Noreen had not shut the door yet; she stood in the gap with one hand on the frame and the other on her belly, watching.

Elsie did not lift a hand. She closed her fist around the black peg in her pocket and went inside to lay out a lie she'd now have to keep.

In her kitchen she gave the shared wall one light knock, their old check beat. Nothing came back. A second later she heard Peter slide the Walsh back bolt home, metal on metal, final as a lockup gate.

Outside, the thin run of meltwater on the Walsh step had begun to glass over.


# Chapter 11

Overnight, the thin run of meltwater on the Walsh step had sealed into black glass.

Noreen saw it through the lace where the frost had not sealed the pane yet: a hard strip of shine from threshold to curb, no grit on it, no ashes, nothing the council promised in handbills and never managed on Barrow Lane unless a man from the council lived on that street. Peter had left heel marks from when he came in at half one, weaving and broad-backed in the hall, one shoe missing and his breath full of stout and peppermint. Those marks had frozen into pale commas in the dark.

She set his mug on the table anyway. Tea gone stewed and bitter because she had brewed it early and forgotten it while she pulled her stockings over swollen ankles that did not look like her ankles anymore. She had her clinic card tucked in the prayer book on the shelf, not for piety, for safety. Peter never opened it unless visitors came and he wanted the room to look decent.

The kettle rattled. In the spare room Arthur coughed once, turned a page, and coughed again. He had taken to reading railway timetables in the morning, stubbornly tracking hours trains no longer kept. Noreen loved him for that useless hope and pitied him for it.

Peter came down with the careful dignity of a man pretending he did not have a hangover. He had shaved a path through one cheek and left white stubble on the other. His glasses sat crooked. He put both palms on the table and looked at the tea like she had served him an insult.

"You let that stand," he said.

"I’ll pour fresh. Sit down before you tip over."

"I am not infirm." He drew himself up, then winced at the kitchen light. "Do not begin your fussing."

His words clicked into their proper sockets: the schoolmaster voice, the collar-button voice. If he had gone soft in the mouth with drink he would have been easier to steer. Formal Peter was a harder weather.

"Fine," she said. "No fussing. Drink this rot and enjoy it."

Arthur laughed into his cough in the next room. Peter ignored it with effort. Noreen saw the muscle jump in his jaw.

He drank half the mug to prove a point and grimaced. "I shall fetch the paper."

"You’ll fetch your death on that step. Wait till I throw ashes."

"I am capable of walking ten yards in daylight."

He reached for his overcoat one-handed because he did not want her help and did not trust Arthur to see him ask for it. He got his arm in wrong, cursed under his breath, and straightened the lapels like a clerk presenting himself at a window. Noreen came around with the coal scuttle anyway.

"Peter, just stand there two minutes."

"No."

He opened the door. January light hit the linoleum in a flat blade. She smelled iron air, chimney smoke, the binman’s cart three streets over where no binman should have been because the roads were still half shut.

"Peter."

He gave her that thin smile he wore when he meant to be obeyed. "Do not call after me like a fishwife."

He stepped down.

His foot shot out. One vicious skid took his leg from under him. For a second he hung in a ridiculous half-squat, coat flared, glasses flashing. Then he came down hard on his right side. The crack Noreen heard was sharp as kindling snapping under a boot.

Peter made a sound she had never heard from him, not even drunk: a raw bark that jumped to a high animal cry before he strangled it off. He curled around his wrist in the slush-dirted snow and tried to stand. He failed. Then he tried again.

Noreen dropped the scuttle. Ash scattered over the frozen step in a useless gray fan. She was beside him before she felt the cold through her slippers.

"Stay down."

"Do not touch me."

"You can order me later." She took his elbow and felt him shudder. "Let me see."

"It is a sprain."

The wrist was already rising, wrong shape, a hump where there should have been a clean line from hand to forearm. She did not need Mrs Keane for that one. Peter saw her face and looked away.

Arthur stood in the doorway in his vest, one boot on, one boot unlaced. Frost had turned his hair pale at the edges. "Need me?"

"Get his scarf and my handbag," Noreen said. "And my clinic card from the prayer book. Please."

Peter snapped, "No clinic. I have to report to the office by nine-thirty."

"You’re not holding a pen with that hand for six bloody weeks."

His eyes cut to Arthur at the swearing, then to the neighbor wall where Elsie could hear if she was near the sink. Shame moved through him quicker than pain.

He pushed himself upright with his left hand and nearly blacked out. Noreen caught his coat front and took his weight. She felt how heavy he was when he stopped pretending.

"Inside," she said.

"No hospital."

"You want this set crooked? You want that hand like a hook?"

"Do not dramatize."

She got him over the threshold inch by inch, his boots scraping the sill. Arthur came back with the scarf, bag, and card, and handed her a folded towel too.

"Wrap it," Arthur said softly. "Not tight."

Noreen nodded. Arthur’s own hands shook in the cold. She noticed, absurdly, that he had darned the thumb of his glove with bright orange wool, the wrong color entirely. He had done it neat as surgery.

Peter lowered himself into the kitchen chair and set his wrist on the towel with two fingers, staring at it like a limb bolted onto the wrong body. Sweat stood on his top lip.

"Get Dr Melling to come here," he said. "A home call is adequate."

"Home calls are for people with lungs full of water and babies coming out sideways," Noreen said. "You’re going to casualty."

"I am not sitting in that waiting room with drunks and children."

"You are a drunk with a broken wrist."

His face hardened so fast she heard the breath catch in Arthur’s throat. Peter spoke to Arthur, not to her. "Mr Hale, will you explain to my wife that language and hysteria do not improve outcomes?"

Arthur looked at Noreen first. "Outcome’s better if he gets x-rayed today."

Peter stared at him for three full beats, betrayed by grammar and class and pain all at once. Then he bent over his arm and breathed through his teeth.

Noreen moved to the telephone in the hall. The line gave her two dead clicks, then a tone, then nothing. She rattled the hook, dialed again. This time she got exchange and waited through a hiss of weather until a woman’s flat voice answered emergency control.

"Ambulance?"

"For a fractured wrist, no vehicle available," the woman said before Noreen finished. "Road priority is chest pain, collapse, labor, and children under five with breathing difficulty. Can family transport?"

Noreen looked at Peter, at his jaw shaking though he kept his mouth shut. "Yes," she said. "If I can find one."

"Then do that. Keep limb supported. No food in case sedation is required."

No food. Peter had eaten nothing but pride and stale tea. Good.

She rang Bhatti’s shop. No answer. Rang again and got Mrs Bhatti’s son, breathless, shouting over radio music. Yes, their cousin still had the Ford van. No, it might not start. Yes, he would try.

When she came back Peter had buttoned his coat wrong, left over right like a child dressing in the dark.

"Take it off," she said.

"Do not instruct me in dressing."

"Then dress yourself correctly."

He could not. His fingers fumbled at the buttons; he sucked a curse and dropped his left hand to his lap. Noreen rebuttoned him without asking. He sat rigid, jaw locked, flinching at each touch as if she were pressing on a bruise for spite.

A knock thudded through the wall first, then at the door. Elsie came in with no coat, just Tom’s old cardigan stretched over her belly and snow in her hair. She held a tin mug with hot water and salt, steam curling into her face.

"Arthur said he’d gone down," she said. Her eyes skimmed Peter, took in the wrist, the sweat, the carefully controlled mouth. "Christ."

"Mind your language," Peter said automatically.

Elsie gave him the mild look she used on men digging holes in the wrong place. "You can dock me a shilling later. Noreen, I’ve got bandage roll and sling pins if casualty’s crowded."

Noreen took the mug. "Thank you. Can you ring clinic for me? Nine-ten slot, Mrs Walsh, antenatal review. Tell them I’m delayed at casualty and I’ll come if we’re out in time."

Elsie glanced at Peter and then back at Noreen. They did not need more words. "I’ll keep trying till someone answers."

Peter said, "My medical business does not concern the neighborhood."

"It concerns your wife," Elsie said. "And she concerns me."

Peter’s left hand slid up the doorframe beside Elsie’s shoulder and gripped until his knuckles blanched, his smile gone flat and bloodless. Noreen felt a hard cramp seize low in her belly, sharp enough that she had to brace against the table edge before Arthur reappeared in the doorway with Peter’s hat and Noreen’s boots.

Outside, Bhatti’s cousin got the van started on the third attempt. He was a narrow man with one ear bitten short, maybe by frost years ago, maybe by a machine, and he kept tapping the dashboard twice before each gear, a habit meant to bully the gearbox into obedience. He told Noreen his name was Dev and asked no questions once he saw Peter’s arm.

The ride to Calderbank Infirmary took forty minutes for two miles. Dev crawled down untreated stretches while Peter swore at every skid and then apologized to Dev in courtly full sentences when a pothole jolted his wrist. Noreen braced one hand on the seat in front and held Peter’s forearm with the other, feeling each tremor that ran through him. Outside, people walked in the ruts buses had left days earlier, bent into the wind with milk bottles clinking in bags.

At the infirmary doors, Peter straightened and let go of her arm before anyone saw how hard he had leaned.

The casualty clerk had a mustard scarf wrapped twice around her neck and three fountain pens clipped to her cardigan like medals. She took Peter’s details without looking up.

Noreen stayed on her feet because standing felt safer than sitting. If she bent now, with the child low and her back burning, she was not sure she could rise fast when Peter barked her name.

"Name, date of birth, occupation, next of kin."

Peter gave each answer with clipped precision. "Municipal accounts clerk," he added, and the clerk gave one brisk nod, filing him under respectable and therefore urgent.

The wall clock sat above the hatch. Nine-ten was already gone. Noreen felt each lost minute like a thumb pressed into the sore notch under her ribs.

"Time of injury?"

"Approximately eight fifteen."

Noreen said, "He slipped on untreated ice outside our house. Landed right-handed."

The clerk finally looked up. "You the wife?"

"Yes."

"Any head knock? Vomiting?"

"No."

"Pregnant, are you?" Her eyes dropped to Noreen’s coat front. "You should sit when you can. We’re short on chairs, mind."

Sit when you can. Noreen tasted metal at the back of her tongue and kept her knees locked. She had carried him from step to van to desk. She could carry this part too.

Peter began, "My wife will remain with me," already braced for insult.

The wall clock clicked over and he lunged for command before anyone had challenged him. Noreen felt a hot, mean flicker of anger, then pushed it down before it showed on her face.

The clerk pushed a clipboard over. "Sign here, Mr Walsh. Or make a mark if hand won’t do."

He froze. Noreen saw the humiliation land, neat and exact.

"I’ll sign for him," she said.

Peter said, "No." He took the pen in his left hand and wrote with the fury of a man forging his own name. The letters looked like fence posts in thawing mud.

They waited among boys with split chins, an old woman cradling a teapot wrapped in newspaper, and a stevedore with his thumb tied in string. Peter refused to sit until his face went gray, then sat and pretended he had chosen to. Noreen watched the wall clock move toward nine-ten, nine-fifteen, nine-twenty.

At nine-thirty she found the pay phone by the lavatory and fed it her last sixpence. The clinic line rang, rang, rang, then died. She dialed again. A receptionist answered on the fifth try, voice flattened by static.

"Calderbank Maternity, hold please."

Noreen held. Men’s boots squeaked behind her. A child cried in thin, skinless screams.

"Maternity clinic." New voice, brisk. "Mrs Walsh? You were due at nine-ten."

"I know. My husband’s fractured his wrist. We’re at casualty. I’m trying to get across after."

Paper rustled. "After ten we log you as not seen. Sixteen booked, two staff off."

"Please, I only need blood pressure and urine done."

"And edema review, yes." The woman sounded tired, not cruel. "Your notes say swelling increased at last contact. I'm reading your alert line: pressure headache, flashing spots, pain under the ribs, baby going quiet. If any one starts, ring triage straight off. Do not sit on it."

Noreen pressed her forehead to the cold metal box. "Can you fit me later today?"

"No. Earliest routine slot is Thursday at twelve-forty, if roads hold. I can put a red flag on your file because this is second disruption in three weeks."

Red flag. Peter would hear it as accusation; she heard it as a thin line over a drop.

"Put it on," she said.

"Bring urine sample if possible. Rest your feet elevated in the meantime."

Noreen nearly laughed at that. Rest in whose house.

She hung up and stood still one extra second to keep from crying in a corridor that smelled of carbolic and wet wool.

X-ray showed what she had already known. "Distal radius fracture," said a young doctor with ink on his thumb and a wedding ring too loose for his hand. "Clean break, no displacement after splinting. We’ll plaster from knuckles to elbow. Six weeks, maybe seven. Keep it dry. No lifting."

Peter asked, "Can I return to office duties tomorrow?"

The doctor blinked, then smiled without humor. "Not if you’d like your bones to stay where I’ve put them."

By the time plaster set and discharge papers were signed with Peter’s left-hand fence-post signature again, the clinic had closed and snow had started in fine hard grains.

In the van back home, Peter rested his cast in Noreen’s lap without asking. She kept both palms around it to stop the vibration from the road. Each jolt went through the plaster into her wrists, her shoulders, the small sore place under her breastbone where panic had been living for weeks.

At the corner of Barrow Lane, Elsie stood under a shared umbrella with Mrs Bhatti, waiting. Elsie raised one hand when she saw them. Peter looked away.

Noreen did not.

She held Elsie’s gaze until Dev turned the van and the lane swallowed her from sight.

***

By evening the house had reorganized itself around Peter’s cast; he kept it lifted like a badge and made everyone move to suit it.

Noreen set up his chair near the range because cold made him "throb" every five minutes and because he claimed the draught at the table made healing impossible. She moved the mending basket, Arthur’s boots, her own stool. She shifted the medicine bottle to the mantel where Peter could watch it. She lined up spoon, cup, and folded handkerchief on a tray by his left side so he would not ask for each item separately and then blame her for delay.

He asked anyway.

"Water."

"Now tea."

"Not that cup, the chipped one catches at my lip."

"Where is the circular from Accounts? I had it in my briefcase."

"Do not overboil the cabbage."

Arthur came in at six with his hair damp from sleet and a parcel of cod wrapped in yesterday’s news. He set it on the counter and flexed his fingers over the range.

"Queue was out the door," he said. "Woman in front of me paid in coppers she’d warmed in her glove. Thought they’d stick less to her skin."

Noreen smiled despite herself. "Did it work?"

"No. She swore like a docker and gave the fishmonger two poetry lines about his mother."

Peter said without looking up, "Arthur, would you please not encourage profanity at table."

Arthur rubbed water from his eyebrow with his sleeve and gave a tiny bow. "Wouldn’t dream of it."

Noreen fried the cod in the shallow pan with the last spoon of dripping. Peter complained about the smell, then ate all of his portion and half the potatoes she had meant for tomorrow’s breakfast. When he finished he sat back, rotated the cast under the lamp, and held it out until Noreen stopped and looked where he wanted.

"You will write this week’s payment book entries for me," he said.

"I can copy numbers."

"Not copy." He tapped the ledger with his good finger. "You write what I say, exactly."

"Same thing if I do it right."

"When you are flustered, you tidy figures to make them prettier. Do not do that to mine."

He said it calmly, in the patient tone he used for children learning laces. She bit down on the answer that rose up: Accuracy began in not getting drunk enough to crack your own bones. Instead she took the ledger from the sideboard and opened to the right week.

Peter dictated household figures while Arthur washed his plate in the scullery, loud enough that neither of them could pretend not to hear.

"Coal, two sacks on credit, one pound six and four."

"Mm-hm."

"Do not say mm-hm while writing numbers. Repeat them."

She repeated. Her hand cramped halfway through and she flexed it over the paper. The baby rolled low and hard across her pelvis, a movement like a fish turning in a net.

Peter watched her stop. "Do not start that performance. If you needed to sit, you should have paced your effort."

Arthur came back in with a plate towel over his shoulder. "I can take dictation if that helps."

Peter’s smile stayed polite and sharp. "This is domestic accounting, Mr Hale, not a union meeting."

Arthur lifted both hands and turned toward the spare room. As he passed Noreen he set a small folded square of paper by the ledger, hidden under the edge. She waited until Peter asked for his second tablet, then slid it into her apron.

It was from Elsie, three lines in pencil:

`Heard from Bhatti boy: fracture set. I can come by after dark with broth if safe. Tap twice on wall if yes.`

Noreen kept her face blank and fed the note to the range, watching the paper curl black and vanish. Peter noticed the movement.

"What was that?"

"List of washing."

"You burn lists now?"

"Saves clutter."

He held her eyes a second too long. "I do not need the lane trooping through my kitchen while I am laid up."

Smoke from the burned note still ghosted above the grate; he had not seen the paper, but he smelled movement like a dog scents thunder.

"Elsie asked how you were."

"Mrs Harrow has grown very interested in my doorway."

"She heard you hit the step. Everyone heard you hit the step."

Color climbed his neck above the collar. He set his glass down with care that cost him.

"From this evening," he said, "there will be no unsupervised visits between you and Mrs Harrow."

Noreen stared, waiting for the laugh line that did not come.

"You can’t be serious."

"I am entirely serious."

"She is next door."

"Then keep it on the step, with the door open where I can see."

"Door open where you can see." Noreen gave a short, disbelieving laugh. "What are we now, two departments in a ministry?"

"Do not mock me in my own house."

"I live here too."

"You are my wife," he said. "You will not turn this place into a courting yard. The lane already has enough to say about you."

She heard what he did not dare say plain: her laughter with Elsie in the yard, doors closing at odd hours, the way she had started answering him directly after years of smoothing everything over. He did not know the shape of it yet, but he felt the ground move.

Arthur’s door clicked shut down the hall. Not loud, not theatrical, just enough to mark that he had heard and removed himself.

Noreen lowered her voice. "I missed clinic because you broke your wrist. I sat in casualty four hours. I hauled you in and out of that van. I held your cast over every rut in Calderbank and did not drop it once. Do not stand there and tell me who I can speak to."

Peter stood too fast, pain crossing his face before control sealed it.

"I did not ask to fall."

"No, you asked to be carried like a lord afterward."

"I asked not to be made a fool in my own house." He lifted the hall telephone from its table and set it beside his chair, pulling the cord until it went tight. "From this evening, the calls stay by me." Then he hooked her purse from the peg, tipped the coins into his left palm, and counted them into two stacks. "Bus fare there. Bus fare back. Nothing else."

"Your order put me off clinic."

"One appointment is not catastrophe."

"You do not know that."

"I know this: you do not drift next door whenever you feel like it. Not while I am laid up."

Laid up. He made the two words sound final.

She looked at his cast, then at his intact left hand, his intact legs, the mouth still perfectly capable of giving orders.

"You’re not laid up," she said. "You’re inconvenienced."

He stepped close enough that the plaster brushed her apron and planted his good hand flat on the mantel, boxing her against the range. His voice came low and exact. "Do not try me tonight." Sweat broke across her upper lip despite the cold, and the baby kicked once, hard enough to steal her breath.

Silence sat between them, thick as wet wool.

She thought of tapping twice on the wall and hearing Elsie tap back. She thought of not doing it and feeling that omission in her chest all night like swallowed ice.

Peter spoke again, mild now. "Tomorrow you ring the clinic where I can hear every word. You ask for another appointment and keep your tone respectful. Then you come straight home unless I am with you. Is that understood?"

Noreen could have said no. Could have thrown the ledger at him. Could have gone next door in her apron and slippers and dared him to stop her in front of Arthur and the whole lane.

She pictured the lane. Mrs Bhatti’s sharp kind eyes. Mrs Doyle’s front-curtain twitch. Peter, cast white as a flag, telling them all his wife had become unstable in pregnancy. In this year, on this street, men won that story first.

"Understood," she said.

He sat again, relieved enough to show it. "Good. Bring the nighttime tablets."

She brought them. He swallowed one, then rattled the bottle toward her.

"Second one."

"Label says every four hours."

"Label is not in pain."

She held his gaze, then shook one out and watched him take it.

Later she made up Arthur’s camp bed in the spare room because Peter said he might call in the night if his arm cramped and "a witness in residence" was sensible in case she needed help lifting him.

Arthur accepted the extra blanket and looked at her for a long moment. "You all right, Mrs Walsh?"

"No," she said before she could smooth it. Then, because truth felt dangerous in an ordinary kitchen, she added, "I’m tired."

Arthur nodded, practical and immediate, the way he took a tool from a workmate. "If you need me, knock twice on the wall. I sleep light."

The same code as Elsie’s note. Noreen almost smiled.

When she climbed into bed Peter was already on his back, cast propped on pillows like an honored guest. He did not touch her.

Near midnight his left hand clamped her shoulder. "Water."

She passed him the tumbler from the crate. He swallowed once and pushed it back into her palm. "Warm. Not this."

"Arthur’s asleep."

"Then move quietly."

She went to the kitchen, warmed more in the kettle, came back, and held the cup while he drank because he said his fingers were slipping. From the spare room Arthur coughed once and the bed springs answered; he had turned to the wall and made himself deaf.

At two Peter whispered, "Chamber pot."

"You can walk to the lavatory."

"If I fall again, you will explain to Mr Hale why you left me to piss myself."

He said Hale’s name just loud enough to carry. Noreen fetched the pot, knelt while he used it, then stood too fast and saw black sparks skate across her vision.

"Empty it," he said, handing it back without looking at her face.

By three he slept, mouth open, breath sour with medicine. Noreen scrubbed her hands in cold water until the smell lifted, then sat on the bed edge with her pulse hammering behind her right eye and made the decision clean: Thursday would happen without his permission.

Noreen lay awake and listened to the shared wall.

No taps came.

***

In the morning Peter insisted on being shaved before she went out.

"I cannot present myself at the surgery with stubble," he said.

"You’re not presenting anywhere. You’re on a chair by the fire for six weeks."

"Decency is not contingent on mobility."

She warmed water, set a basin, and shaved him with the old safety razor while he lectured her on blade angle. Her own hands were numb from rinsing in cold water. By the time she tied his scarf and settled his sling, the first bus to town had already gone, if it had run at all.

"I will accompany you to the clinic office," he said.

"No you won’t. You can barely get your boot on."

"Then Mr Hale can attend."

Arthur, buttoning his coat in the doorway, said, "I’m on emergency call till dusk. If I miss rotation, half the north feeder stays dark."

Peter gave a thin nod that meant offense logged for later. "Very well. Do not linger, Noreen."

"I won’t."

He held out his left hand, palm up, not to touch her but to receive. "Fare money. I will allocate."

She stared. "Allocate."

"You emptied your purse at casualty. I am controlling this week’s money until we understand the wage impact."

She wanted to laugh and spit in equal measure. Instead she watched him count coins one by one from the tobacco tin, each piece clicking table to table: bus there, bus back, two pence margin. No extra for tea, no extra for a call.

"If buses are off?" she asked.

"Then walk directly there and directly back."

"On this ice."

"You are not made of sugar."

Neither was he, and still he had cracked.

She tucked the coins into her glove and left before she said it.

The lane looked beaten, not cleared. Footpaths were rutted with frozen boot prints and cart wheels. At the corner she saw Elsie at her gate shaking a rug, breath smoking in little bursts. Elsie glanced up, saw Noreen, and touched two fingers to her own wrist in a question: him?

Noreen gave a short nod and a tiny twist of her hand to mime plaster. Elsie’s mouth tightened. She took one step forward, then checked herself when she saw Peter’s silhouette at the Walsh window.

Noreen walked on.

Halfway to town she turned back once. Elsie was still at the gate, rug over the rail, watching until Noreen rounded the bakery and disappeared.

The clinic office had moved winter triage to a side entrance because the main steps were iced solid. Women queued in coats over nightdresses, flasks tucked under arms, children clinging to hems. A hand-lettered sign on the door read: `IF YOU HAVE HEADACHE + FLASHING LIGHTS + SWELLING, TELL DESK IMMEDIATELY.`

Noreen stood twenty-three minutes before reaching the hatch. The receptionist today was a young woman with bitten nails painted red and a tiny silver thimble hanging from her necklace. She kept touching it between calls for luck.

"Name?"

"Noreen Walsh. Missed yesterday. Casualty with husband."

The woman flipped through cards, found hers, and frowned. "You were due for pressure, urine, and edema review. Mrs Keane marked urgent follow-up after prior bleed episode."

"I know. I need another slot."

"Earliest is Thursday twelve-forty, but that’s contingency clinic. If weather closes roads, it cancels first."

Thursday. Four days if the buses ran, six if they did not. She pictured her feet by Thursday, skin stretched over fluid till her shoes cut lines she could not press out.

"Is there anything sooner?"

"Not booked." The receptionist lowered her voice. "I can put you on the cancellation board. Means if someone doesn’t arrive, you must come within an hour when called."

Noreen almost said yes, then remembered the house telephone that worked when it wanted, Peter by the range answering before she could, Peter deciding which messages were relevant to household order.

"Can you ring Bhatti’s shop if our line fails?"

"We can try one alternate number."

Noreen gave it.

The receptionist wrote in red pencil: `ALT CONTACT: BHATTI STORES`. Then she looked up, expression changing from clerical to human. "Mrs Walsh, I have to log this word for word. Red-flag triggers are headache climbing fast, sparks in your sight, pain under the right ribs, sudden sickness, or less baby movement. If one starts, you do not go home first. You come to triage and say antenatal red flag at the desk. Say it back so I know."

Noreen nodded.

"Antenatal red flag. Straight to triage, no waiting."

"Good." The woman slid her a thin queue slip with the time scrawled in pencil. "Keep your feet up as much as possible."

Noreen tucked the slip inside her glove beside Peter’s counted coins.

As she turned from the hatch, she nearly walked into Mrs Keane herself, coming through the side door with snow on her hat brim and that heavy canvas bag dragging her shoulder down. Keane recognized her instantly.

"You missed yesterday."

"Peter broke his wrist."

Keane’s eyes went to Noreen’s face, then to her hands, then down to her boots where edema had made the laces bow outward. "How bad’s the swelling this morning?"

"Worse by night."

"Headaches?"

"A bit."

"How bad a bit?"

"Like someone tapping a nail at the back of my eye. Comes and goes."

Keane swore softly. "That’s not a bit. You needed seeing yesterday."

"I know."

Keane shifted the bag and stepped closer so other women could not hear. "Listen to me. If that nail-behind-the-eye pain starts again, you stop where you are, you sit, and you send word through Bhatti or Hale or anyone with legs. I do not care who gets offended. Don't walk it off. Don't wait for your husband to agree. This can tip to seizure in minutes."

Noreen felt heat flush up her neck despite the cold hallway. "He didn’t block. He broke."

Keane held her gaze. "And now he is home, and you are still the one carrying everyone. Do not confuse injury with innocence."

Noreen looked away first. Through the side window she could see two orderlies chipping ice from the ramp with shovels, one singing under his breath in a voice gone hoarse from smoke.

"Thursday," she said. "They gave me twelve-forty."

Keane set her bag on the bench, took the slip, and rewrote it on a stiff clinic card in heavy block capitals: `THURSDAY 12:40 - RED FLAG REVIEW`. She initialed beneath it, then pressed the card back into Noreen’s hand. "I’ll flag your notes again. Keep this on your body, not filed with his bills. If transport fails, send word through Bhatti and I’ll push for a lift from triage. If your vision sparks or the baby goes still, you come in and you come in now. No permission slips from husbands."

Noreen nodded, because speech had thickened in her throat.

She spent her last two pence on a call from the corridor phone, not to home but to Bhatti’s, asking the boy for two messages. The first was for Elsie: `Thursday 12:40. Red flag. Don’t come over while he’s watching.` The second was for Dev: van by the market cut-through Thursday twelve sharp, fare on Bhatti’s book till Saturday. The boy repeated both wrong once, then right.

By the time she reached Barrow Lane her calves burned from walking the final stretch where buses had stopped. Peter was at the table with his cast propped on the account ledger, reading one-handed and underlining with angry strokes from his left hand.

"You took one hour and forty-two minutes," he said without greeting.

"Queue was out the door."

"Appointment?"

She took off her gloves slowly, palmed Keane’s stiff card, and slid it into the prayer book on the chair by the door before she answered. "Thursday at twelve-forty."

"That is acceptable."

"If weather holds," she said.

"Weather will hold sufficiently."

She almost admired his confidence in the sky taking instruction.

He reached out. "Give me the card."

She hesitated, then gave him the thin slip from the reception hatch. He read it, turned it over, and set it under the salt cellar where he put bills due and things he wanted visible.

"You will rest now," he said. "After you prepare lunch."

Noreen stood by the range, one hand on the mantel to steady herself while heat climbed her shins and pain thudded behind her right eye in slow, deliberate taps.

Through the wall came the faint clatter of Elsie’s pans, the rhythm she used when she was worried and trying not to show it. Three fast, one slow, then silence.

Noreen listened to that private code while she peeled potatoes for Peter with fingers gone clumsy from cold and swelling.

Thursday, twelve-forty, she told herself.

Dev would be there at twelve.

Keane’s card sat in the prayer book, and Bhatti’s ledger held the van booking in another hand. Peter could watch the wrong slip all week; he could not unmake what she had already set in motion.


# Chapter 12

Thursday came exactly as bad as Wednesday had promised.

Noreen woke before the alarm because her left hand was numb under her cheek. The fingers were swollen into thick pale sausages, her wedding ring buried so deep the skin folded over its edge. When she tried to turn onto her back, a weight settled high under her ribs and stayed there, stubborn as a fist.

The house had not warmed in the night. Frost had feathered along the inside of the bedroom window again, and the blanket smelled of damp wool and Peter's hair oil. She sat up slowly and waited for the dizziness to pass. The kettle knocked against the hob downstairs, a dull iron tap each time the flame shook. Arthur was already up in the spare room, moving in his careful linesman way that never quite made a full thud. She could tell him from Peter even through the wall: Arthur placed his boots side by side before he stepped into them, and the soles hit the boards in matched beats.

Her appointment card was tucked into her prayer book on the chair, Thursday 12:40 at Calderbank Maternity Clinic in Keane's block capitals. She had smoothed that card flat three times since Tuesday night, rubbing the crease until the paper went slick under her thumb and willing the date to fade. Her ankles had doubled in size anyway. No amount of pretending shifted them.

At twelve Dev was meant to wait by the market cut-through with Bhatti's van booking in his coat pocket. If she missed that window, he would not linger where anyone could see.

Peter was at the table when she came down, cast propped on a folded tea towel, elbow cocked so the plaster never grazed the wood. He had shaved and put on his office shirt despite not going in. A man in a sling looked softer, she had once thought. The sling had done nothing to him except make him crueler with his good hand.

He had his little accounts ledger open beside the sugar tin. It had two columns, neat as parish notices, and each figure was pressed deep enough to mark the page below.

"You are late," he said without looking up.

"It isn't ten." She put the kettle straight and reached for the teapot. "I've clinic at twelve-forty."

"I know what day it is."

He said it like a correction from a headmaster. The cast turned his right sleeve into a stiff white wing. He moved the book with his left hand and shut it with a hard tap.

"Bus fares have increased again. We are not paying for two returns and your tea-shop gossip after."

"It isn't gossip. It's my blood pressure review."

"You were reviewed last month. Then you missed one. Then you claim emergency." He clicked his tongue and made another note. "If you require rest, rest here. It costs nothing."

Noreen set cups out in a row to keep her face level. One for Peter, one for Arthur, one for herself. The fourth cup, blue enamel chipped at the lip, stayed on the shelf where she could see it. She had not put it out since Peter's fall, not with him watching every movement and tallying each small kindness in his private ledger.

"Keane said I had to come in when they gave me this slot," she said. "It's not optional."

"You leave now and you are gone half the day." He pushed it toward her with his good knuckles. "No dinner on, no one at the door, no one to see who comes and goes. I will not have that."

Arthur came in from the scullery with his coat over one arm and the smell of outside still clinging to him, wet rope and metal. He set a jar of screws on the dresser before he spoke, automatically sorting them by size while he waited for tea. He did it even half-asleep, thumb and forefinger finding heads and threads, sorting by feel before his eyes caught up.

"Road's slick at the corner," he said to no one in particular. "Bus was ten minutes behind yesterday."

Peter gave him a look that could freeze paraffin. "Thank you, Mr Hale. We can manage our household timetable without commentary."

Arthur did not answer. He took his mug, nodded once to Noreen, and left with the jar in his pocket, screws chiming faintly against one another with each step.

Noreen poured tea and did not drink. Her mouth tasted of pennies and sleep.

"You said you would pay fare when I rebooked," she said.

Peter snapped it shut. "I said we would decide it. It is decided."

His voice had that careful municipal tone he used with complaint letters. It was not loud. It was worse than loud, a tidy refusal dressed as reason.

She hated that she knew his patterns well enough to hear the hinge turning. If she asked again, he would move from arithmetic to morality, then from morality to accusation. By noon he would be telling her she had endangered the baby by upsetting herself.

The back door rattled once. Two short knocks, a pause, then one. Elsie's knock, the one they had worked out months ago to spare themselves shouting through walls.

Noreen crossed the kitchen before Peter could stand. Her ankles throbbed inside borrowed boots, each step thick and hot at the joints. Elsie waited under the lintel with a shawl over her coat and her hair pinned anyhow, cheeks red from cold. Snowmelt darkened the hem of her skirt. She held up a tin measure.

"You left this with me," Elsie said, pitching her voice neighbor-bright. "Thought you might need it back."

Peter had followed as far as the doorway to the front room. He did not bother hiding his expression.

"Mrs Harrow," he said. "My wife is not receiving callers this morning. She requires quiet."

Elsie gave him a smile that showed no teeth. "It's one cup measure, Mr Walsh. I won't keep her from what she's got on."

"She has no errands."

"Clinic," Noreen said.

Peter turned his head toward her and did not blink. "No."

He gave her one flat word. He had discovered that plain command carried farther than shouting.

Noreen felt the room tilt for a second, not from fear but from that deep under-rib ache returning with bright edges. She gripped the doorframe and looked past him to the mantel where the clock ticked too fast, always three minutes ahead because Peter liked to arrive first and make people wait.

"I'm going," she said.

"On whose money?" Peter asked. "On whose authority?"

His cast brushed the chair back. Plaster dust came off on the varnish, a white crescent. He did not notice.

Elsie glanced at Noreen once, quick as striking a match. Then she reached into the inside seam of her coat and pulled out a folded bit of wax paper. Coins slid into her palm, silver and copper warmed by her body.

"On mine," Elsie said.

Peter laughed once through his nose. "You can barely keep your own house heated."

"Aye, and still." Elsie counted into Noreen's hand without looking away from him. "Four shillings and six. Return fare and a cup of tea if the clinic runs late."

The coins were heavier than they looked. Noreen felt each edge bite her skin as she closed her fingers.

"I don't need tea," she said under her breath.

"You need not be sentimental, Mrs Harrow," Peter said. "Favors between neighbors become gossip by supper."

Elsie shrugged. "Then chalk it on the scullery door if that helps you sleep."

Peter's mouth hardened. For a second Noreen thought he would step forward and block the threshold with his shoulder. Instead he lifted his cast slightly, the smallest reminder of his injury, and spoke with exquisite politeness.

"If anything happens on that road, you will not tell people I approved this outing."

"You don't let anything stand unless it kneels to you first," Noreen said, surprising herself.

His face went flat. He was most dangerous when he went quiet.

"Be back before five," he said. "Mr Hale requires his dinner, and this house does not run on sentiment either."

Noreen tucked the coins into her glove and stepped outside before he could add one more term.

The air knifed straight through wool. Elsie took her elbow as they crossed the patch of black ice by the drainpipe, not fussing, just steady pressure. They walked in silence past the Bhattis' shuttered shop, past the chapel steps drifted to half-height, past two boys breaking frozen puddles with a broom handle and shouting when the shards jumped. Every few yards Noreen had to stop and shift her boots because her feet no longer bent where feet should bend.

They cut through the market lane where Dev should have been waiting with Bhatti's van booking tucked in his coat. The lane held two greengrocers under an awning and Mrs Henshaw from number nineteen with her shopping net. No van, no Dev. He had either gone on the minute or taken one look at witnesses and kept driving.

At the bus stop, five people waited under the bent timetable board. A woman with a toddler wrapped in a blanket kept stamping circles into the snow to keep the child warm. A man in a porter cap smoked down to the paper and lit another from the stub.

When the bus finally came, it crawled in second gear and was full to the door. The conductor leaned out, cheeks raw, and shouted that he could take two standing at most. The porter and the blanket woman climbed on. The rest of them watched the rear lights fade into white grit.

Noreen checked the coins again. Without Dev's lift, they had bus luck or cab money and nothing in between. If they waited for the next, they would miss the appointment and lose the slot. Keane had said plainly on the phone that if Noreen missed again she might not be seen for another week.

Elsie flagged down the first black cab that looked alive enough to move. It skidded half past them before the driver caught, reversed in a spray of slush, and leaned across to crank the passenger door open.

"Clinic?" he said. "Road by the market's blocked. I'll take Chapel Street and you can pray." He had one knitted glove and one bare hand red as meat on the wheel. A canary pin hung from his rear-view mirror, tiny yellow paint chipped off the beak.

Elsie said, "How much?"

"Two and eight if we don't get stuck. Three if we do and I've got to dig us out."

Noreen made a sound she could not hide. Elsie squeezed her wrist.

"Get in," Elsie said.

They rode with their knees pressed together on cracked vinyl, the heater dead, windows filmed white at the corners. The cab smelled of stale tobacco and damp dog. Noreen watched terraced roofs slide by in jerks and thought how easy it was to disappear from one street to the next in weather like this. A missed bus, a delayed call, a woman deciding she did not want to start a fight at breakfast, and by afternoon blood pressure could go from numbers in a notebook to a body on a kitchen floor.

When they reached the clinic, Elsie paid from the same folded packet, adding two pennies tip and thanking the driver twice, forcing the scene into the shape of an ordinary fare. Noreen tried to hand half back.

"No," Elsie said. "You keep your hand shut. We'll sort it later."

"You've already fed my fire and my lamp." Noreen's voice shook from cold and something meaner. "I'm not standing in front of him with my palm open again."

Elsie looked at her properly then, eyes narrow with worry. "Then don't name it. Call it me getting you seen."

Noreen wanted to kiss her, hard, right there under the clinic awning with frozen grit blowing into their collars. Instead she tucked the coins away and followed Elsie through the door.

The waiting room was overheated in bursts, a radiator hissing and stopping like an asthmatic chest. Wet coats steamed on hooks. A pram stood by the wall with no baby in it, just folded nappies and a jar of talc in the tray. Somewhere down the corridor a woman was retching into enamel, the sound thin and furious.

Keane called Noreen in twelve minutes late, just before one, with her glasses sliding down her nose and a pencil behind her ear. She always smelled faintly of Pears soap and the clove lozenges she sucked to keep from smoking on shift. Today there was blood on one knuckle where the skin had split in the cold, sealed over with clear collodion that caught the strip light.

"Mrs Walsh, coat off, boots off if you can manage," she said. "Mrs Harrow, you stay if Noreen wants you. I'm not spending all morning repeating myself to husbands by telephone."
