"Stay," Noreen said before Elsie could pretend she should wait outside.

The exam room was small enough that Elsie's coat brushed the instrument trolley when she moved. Keane wrapped the cuff around Noreen's arm, pumped, listened, frowned, and did it again. The second reading took longer. She licked her pencil tip, wrote two numbers, underlined both.

"Any headache today?"

"Since dawn."

"Where?"

"Behind my eyes. And here." Noreen pressed the heel of her hand under her right ribs.

"Any lights in your vision? Sparkles, black spots, little flies?"

"Last night, when I stood from the chair. I thought it was the fire smoke."

Keane pressed two fingers into Noreen's shin for five seconds and lifted them. The dent stayed, a thumbprint holding in her skin.

"How long have your boots been tight?"

"A week, maybe two."

"Wedding ring?"

"Stuck."

Keane gave a short grunt that was not judgment, only arithmetic.

"Right. We're not playing at this anymore." She turned the chart toward them so they could see. "Six weeks ago you were one-forty over ninety. Last review, one-fifty-two over ninety-six. Today you're one-sixty-six over one-oh-eight. This is an upward trend, and it's steep." She tapped each line with the pencil in time with the words. "Urine last month had trace protein. Today's sample has more. You have edema to mid-calf and facial puffing. That constellation is preeclampsia. In my training notes they call it toxemia. Call it what you like. It can kill you fast if it tips."

The word kill did not echo dramatically. It sat in the room like another instrument on the tray.

Noreen stared at the chart and could not make the numbers mean flesh. On Peter's tongue, numbers turned to straps: pull them tight enough and you could not breathe. She wanted Keane to say pain scale, fever, something she could feel and report.

"I had pressure with my first," she said. "It settled."

"You had swelling," Keane said. "This is different and worse."

She turned to Elsie. "You asked for exact, last time we spoke in the lane. You can both have exact now."

Keane set the pencil down and counted symptoms on her fingers, each one clean and hard.

"If she gets a headache that doesn't ease with rest, tea, or tablets, call me. If she sees flashing lights or loses part of her vision, call me. If pain under the right ribs sharpens, if she starts vomiting, if her face or hands swell suddenly more, if she passes very little urine, if she grows breathless at rest, call me. If she has a fit, protect her head, turn her to the side, and send for ambulance at once. Don't wait for husband permission. Don't wait for weather to improve. Don't wait to see if she settles. Women seize and stroke on this trajectory. Babies detach placentas. I'm saying this in plain language because plain language is what keeps people alive."

Elsie's hands were clasped over her bag so tight the knuckles looked chalked. Noreen could feel the force of her listening like heat.

"What do I do now?" Noreen asked.

"You rest." Keane's voice softened by one degree and no more. "Strictly. Left side when possible. Feet up above hip level. No lifting coal scuttles, no carrying wet laundry, no scrubbing floors, no climbing up and down those stairs for every small thing. Salt moderate. Fluids steady. I want you seen twice weekly at minimum, home visits if roads fail." She glanced at Noreen's hands. "And I need that ring off before your fingers trap blood. Soap and string tonight."

Noreen laughed once, a dry broken sound. "Who is doing my house, then?"

Keane met her eyes. "Whoever prefers you alive."

Noreen looked at Elsie. Elsie looked back, fierce and frightened, eyes flicking once toward the door as if she were already counting Peter's dinner, Arthur's tray, the coal bucket, the laundry line, and the whole machinery of two households, all the invisible work that kept men warm and convinced they were in charge.

"My husband has a broken wrist," Noreen said finally. "He can't do much."

"He can do whatever men discover they can do when there is no alternative," Keane said. "Can he boil potatoes one-handed? Yes. Can he fetch coal in smaller loads? Yes. Can he stop spending your pulse on pointless arguments? Also yes, though that is advanced training."

Elsie gave a startled snort she tried to hide as a cough.

Keane pulled a fresh form from the stack and began writing in tight block print. She wrote with green ink, always green, because she said black disappeared in bad light and this town lived in bad light half the year. The nib scratched hard enough to hear.

"I'm filing high-risk note," she said. "If transport worsens, I'll push for day-bed observation at Calder Infirmary. Beds are scarce and we both know it. I still put it in writing. Documentation protects you when people say you're overreacting."

Noreen's throat burned. "Will the baby come early?"

"Maybe. Not always. Preeclampsia can force our hand, or labor can come on its own. Our aim is time without pretending we own time." Keane set the form aside and looked at Noreen over the rim of the chart, eyes steady on her face instead of the case number. "How many weeks now?"

"Thirty-one by my dates."

"Then every day matters."

Silence sat for a beat, broken only by the radiator's hiss and stop.

Keane picked up the cuff again and tightened it. "I'm taking another reading before you leave. I want a post-rest number. Lie down on the couch five minutes. Mrs Harrow, roll that blanket and wedge her ankle." She paused, then added, "And both of you listen carefully. Secrecy kills in obstetrics more often than blood does. If there is anyone blocking access, withholding fare, delaying calls, you tell me now or you tell me after in writing. I can't treat what I'm not allowed to see."

Noreen's mouth opened and closed. Peter's name sat there like a fishbone. Beside her, Elsie held utterly still.

"He didn't want me to come," Noreen said. "He said it was unnecessary expense."

Keane did not flinch. "Did he prevent you?"

"He tried."

"How did you get here?"

"Cab," Elsie said. "Paid from housekeeping."

Keane's gaze shifted between them and landed somewhere beyond both, in the space where legal facts and lived facts refused to line up.

"Right," she said. "Then we work with what exists. I will chart transport barrier and husband opposition to attendance. If he objects, he can object to my face."

Noreen let out a breath she had not noticed she was holding. Relief came with a sting of shame; wanting a witness still felt like betrayal.

Keane finished the second reading, a fraction lower and still ugly. She wrote it down and tore three slips from her pad.

"One for next clinic Monday, one for district check Saturday if roads permit, and one formal note naming this high-risk with transport barriers. Keep that note where your husband can see it. If roads don't permit, I still come unless I'm physically blocked. Keep a packed bag by the door from tonight: gown, nappies, towel, any papers. And put a list of emergency symptoms on the mantel where everyone can read it. Not hidden in a drawer."

"Everyone?" Noreen asked.

"Everyone who shares your roof." Keane capped her pen. "You are done pretending this is private discomfort. This is public risk."

Outside the room, the corridor had filled with the one-o'clock crowd. Women balanced toddlers on hips, husbands shifted from foot to foot near the payphone, a nurse wheeled a tray of sterilized instruments under a sheet. Life continued with terrible normality.

Noreen sat on the bench by the coat rack while Elsie wrestled her boots back over swollen feet. The leather scraped and stuck. Elsie did not rush. She warmed each boot mouth with her hands first, then eased the heel in inch by inch like fitting a child into a tight sleeve.

"Don't," Noreen said quietly. "You'll hurt your back."

"My back is not the point today." Elsie tied the laces and sat back on her heels. "Can you stand?"

Noreen stood. The floor rolled once and steadied.

When they stepped out under the clinic awning, the sky was a flat pewter lid promising more snow by evening. The road was a churn of gray slush and horse muck. Elsie stared at the symptom slip in her glove, thumb fixed over NO DELAY, reading the words again before the wind could snatch her focus.

Keane's capitals crowded the paper: Saturday district check, Monday clinic, and NO DELAY underlined twice.

The words were not theory. They were distance, door latches, and minutes.

Noreen touched Elsie's sleeve. "Say it."

"What?"

"What you're thinking."

Elsie watched a lorry throw brown spray across the curb, then said, very quiet, "I'm thinking we can't keep doing this like a secret errand. Not with that in your pocket."

Noreen closed her eyes briefly. The wind cut under her scarf and found the damp at the back of her neck.

"If he knows everything, he will lock the doors," she said.

"If he controls every penny and every call, you'll seize in that kitchen while he argues about fare." Elsie's voice stayed level, but the edge on it turned blunt and hard in the way it did when she crossed her own fear. "I won't have it."

They stood close enough that their sleeves touched, two married women on a public pavement making no gesture anyone could convict and all the decisions that would damn them anyway.

"I keep hanging off you like wet sheets," Noreen said, because plain fear had turned mean. "You'll put me on the bill between coal and paraffin."

Elsie shook her head. "Stop it. Cab was two and eight. Tea's a penny if the waiting room drags. Pay me when your boots fit again. Till then, we spend what keeps you upright."

Noreen gave a thin laugh that stung her throat. "Saints preserve us, we're pricing my collapse by the cup."

Elsie folded the symptom slip in half, then half again, and tucked it into the front of Noreen's coat where her hand could find it without searching.

"From now on," Elsie said, "if you get one of those signs, you knock my wall and you don't ask permission first. If you can't get to the wall, one long kettle whistle and Arthur comes. Two whistles and I run to Bhatti's phone for Keane. I'll keep cab fare in the flour tin. Keane said no waiting, and I heard her."

Noreen looked at her, really looked, at the pale strain around her mouth and the stubborn set of her jaw and the curve of her own late belly beneath the coat. Elsie was carrying life too, and still she was offering minutes and muscle she did not have to spare.

"This could ruin you," Noreen said.

"Tom already ruined enough. Peter can try."

She said it in the same plain tone she used for snow totals and bus delays.

The next bus groaned into view, windows fogged, steps slick. Elsie climbed first, turned, and took Noreen's elbow with both hands.

Inside, the conductor punched their tickets and moved on. Noreen held the rail and breathed through the ache under her ribs. Elsie leaned close enough that only Noreen could hear.

"Bag by your door tonight," she said. "Mantel list where he can see it. If he tears it down, I put it back up. If he bars that door, you come through mine."

Noreen nodded once. By the time the bus turned into Barrow Lane, she could see Peter's front window in her mind, bright with his waiting shape. She touched the folded slip through her coat and kept her hand there.

When they stepped off at their stop, she had already decided she would set the packed bag in plain sight and leave it there, even if he kicked it.
At the corner she and Elsie split for their separate doors; Elsie kept one hand in her pocket around the dwindled coins, lips moving once before her key found the lock, as if she were already counting rent against coal.


# Chapter 13

Elsie tipped the dwindled coins from her coat pocket onto the kitchen table and flattened three pound notes under the sugar bowl. She counted the notes a third time before she even unbuttoned her coat.

The bus cold from bringing Noreen home still clung to her coat hem, and frost had climbed halfway up the kitchen window from the inside, feathery and white as cheap lace. A little hill of silver sat to one side, sorted into shillings and sixpences, and the copper had gone green at the edges where damp had got in. She kept a stump of pencil near her elbow and wrote figures on the back of last week's fish wrapper because she had used the clean page from Tom's old notebook for Noreen's clinic times.

Rent first. Coal second. Mrs Bhatti before Saturday, even if it was only a part payment and a promise. She wrote 

`rent 2-6`  
`coal 1-4`  
`Bhatti ?`

and then she stopped, because there was no arithmetic symbol for a man who came home with empty pockets and a grin that wanted to pass for charm.

The house sounded wrong without his tread. She could hear next door clearer than her own hallway. Noreen's kettle lid clicked and settled, clicked and settled again. A muffled male voice followed, Peter's clipped schoolroom tone even through the wall. Then silence, and the kind that meant someone had decided speaking cost too much.

Elsie tucked the money into the tea caddy and shut the lid. Her belly shifted hard to the left under her dress, a slow drag as if the baby had rolled to listen too. She rubbed the side where the heel had pressed and went to stir the pot on the hob, mostly cabbage and two rashers cut thin enough to lie about themselves.

When Tom came in, he banged the front door too loud, as if noise could stand in for confidence. Cold rode in with him, and the wet-wool smell from his overcoat, and ale, and tobacco ground into his scarf. He stamped his boots once and coughed into his fist.

"You should've seen North Bridge," he called, voice bright in that practiced way. "Buses crab-walking like old ladies on Sunday shoes. Whole route was a circus."

Elsie did not answer from the stove. She listened for the jingle of wage coins in his trouser pocket. There was none.

He came into the kitchen, grin set, hair flattened by his cap. "Smells decent in here. You put meat in? Christ, that's luxury."

She set the spoon down. "Post came."

"Oh aye? Anything nice?"

"From Calderbank Depot office. Brown envelope with your name on it. It's on the mantel."

He looked past her toward the front room, then back. The grin stayed but his eyes moved fast, quick as a boy checking whether his hand had been caught in the biscuit tin.

"Probably rota nonsense," he said. "They keep sending paper because they love the sound of themselves."

"Open it."

"Later, Els. Let me sit down first, I'm frozen through."

"Open it while I'm here."

He scratched his jaw, eyes flicking to the mantel. "You'd make a decent foreman, you know that?"

She did not move.

Tom sighed and went through to the front room. She heard the envelope tear, then nothing, then the paper fold once, carefully. He came back with it in his hand and an expression he tried to keep blank.

"Administrative thing," he said. "They've made a fuss over a short bag on Tuesday."

"Short by how much?"

"Not much. Bits. A few quid that'll turn up in some bloody drawer."

"How much, Tom?"

He slid into the chair by the table and spread the letter as if the paper itself might defend him. "They're saying fourteen pounds and ten from remittance, and another six and change from a late shift float. It's nonsense."

Elsie stared at him. "Twenty pounds is not bits."

"I didn't say twenty, did I? I said they're saying it. Auditors count one way in the morning, another way by tea."

"Did you take it?"

"I moved money around." He held up a hand before she spoke. "Listen to me first. I moved money because I needed two days to square a private matter. Two days, Els. It got caught because that jobsworth Martin came in early and did his little hero routine."

She could hear her own breath pulling sharp through her nose. "What private matter?"

"Nothing you'd thank me for dragging indoors."

"Try me."

"Bookie pressure." He said it quickly, like spitting out a fish bone. "There. Happy now?"

The kitchen seemed to tilt for a second and then set itself right again. The pot lid rattled and she did not reach for it.

"How much do you owe?"

Tom rubbed both hands over his face, as if he could warm them that way. "One hundred and forty-seven to Billy Cragg. That's the hole."

She sat down opposite him because her knees had stopped trusting the floor. "And you took depot money to feed it."

"Don't say stole. Christ."

"What word do you want?"

"Borrowed against Friday. I had a line coming in."

"You had a bet."

"A line," he snapped, and then softened at once. "Love, I know how it sounds. But you didn't see the board that day. It was a banker. Anybody would've taken it."

"Anybody with my name on his pram papers too?"

He held his breath.

She had not planned to bring it in yet, but the line had come up out of her before she could choose it. She watched his face rearrange itself around the new calculation: how much she knew, who had told her, which lie still had room to breathe.

"That again," he said at last. "We sorted that."

"No. You told me not to fuss and I wanted the room to stay standing, so I let you walk away. That's not sorting."

"You're carrying enough as things stand. I kept the uglier bits off you."

"You put the uglier bits in my name." She leaned over the table until the edge pressed hard into her stomach. "Dates, Tom. When did you start taking depot money?"

He swallowed. "Three Fridays back. First time was five quid. Paid it Monday. Then twelve, then..."

"Then?"

"Then it got away from me."

"How many times?"

"Four."

"And who knows?"

"Depot office. Maybe transport police if Martin keeps barking. Nobody's gone to a station yet. This is still internal if I make it right before Monday hearing."

Elsie looked at the letter. The words jumped around, but she picked out enough: `disciplinary suspension`, `pending inquiry`, `attend 8:30 Monday`. At the bottom, typed clean and merciless, `Failure to account for public monies may result in police referral.`

She could taste metal from the kettle steam. "You're suspended."

"Temporary."

"Which means no wages this Friday."

He flinched. "Half pay while they look. Maybe."

"Maybe is not rent."

He slapped his palm flat to the table and forced cheer into his voice again. "All right. That's why we're talking now, eh? That's why I'm straight with you. We fix it before Monday and they can't prove intent. That's the game."

"Game."

"Figure of speech. Don't start hanging me with grammar." He leaned in, palms open, matey grin trying to crawl back. "I've got a route. I need a little bridge money, just enough to put back what they're shouting about. Then I show up clean, say records got crossed in storm overtime, and we're done."

Elsie heard next door's wall clock strike four, muffled and officious. The baby kicked once, a hard knock under her ribs.

"Where does this bridge money come from?"

He hesitated for a beat. "Your ring."

The word landed heavier than the coins in the tea caddy.

Tom rushed on. "Only for a week or two. Alfie Dent gives fair rates if he knows it's me. You pawn it Friday, I clear the short, I buy it back once wages restart. Nobody sees anything."

She turned her left hand over on the table. The wedding band sat loose now because her hands had thinned despite the swelling everywhere else. The gold was worn white at one point where it rubbed against the bucket handle when she scrubbed floors.

"Not that ring," she said. "You don't get to spend it."

"You haven't even heard the full plan."

"No, Tom. Not for your patch job."

"Elsie, don't be stubborn for pride. It's metal. It's a circle."

"It's mine."

"It's ours." His voice sharpened. "Unless that changed and nobody told me."

She held his gaze. "You want the truth? It changed when you signed my name and called it protection. It changed when you took fare money from a public bag and came home smelling like stout."

He pushed his chair back hard enough for one leg to scrape and shudder on the stone floor. "I came home, didn't I? That's more than some. I came home and told you what was what."

"Because a typed letter did half the telling first."

Tom's mouth twisted. Shame looked like anger on him; she had learned that years ago and still found herself checking for the bottle when he raised his voice.

"You don't get to stand there pure," he said. "You knew cash was tight and still you handed bits over next door every other day. Soup, tins, coal. You think I didn't notice?"

"I kept a pregnant woman fed."

"You've got one in your own house." He jabbed a finger toward her belly and then dropped his hand, as if embarrassed by it. "I'm trying to keep us out of court, Els. Help me do that."

She stood, slow and careful, hands on the table to lever herself upright. "Tell me everything before you ask me to bleed for it. Names. Amounts. Who's pressing you."

He stared at her, breathing through his mouth.

"Billy Cragg runs the book for this side," he said finally. "His runner's little Len with the cracked front tooth. I was in deep before Christmas and kept chasing because I thought one win would pull me clear. Cragg wanted twenty by Thursday night or he'd start calling here. I took depot money to keep him off the door. Lost again on Saturday match that never should've turned. There. That's everything fit to print."

Elsie pressed her fingers against the table edge until the varnish bit into her skin. The numbers lined up now. They made a shape she hated because it looked solid.

"You put us under a man who sends boys to doors," she said.

"He sends reminders, not knives. Don't make it East End melodrama."

"And the depot hearing?"

"Monday morning."

"And if you can't replace it?"

He looked away first. "Then it's transport police. Maybe court. Maybe prison, if they fancy an example."

The kettle hissed dry enough to scream. Elsie reached past him and lifted it off the ring.

"Tea?" she asked, voice flat.

Tom blinked. "What?"

"You're shaking. Tea won't fix theft, but it'll stop your hands rattling long enough to listen." She set the kettle down and faced him again. "We're not done."

*

She did not put milk in either cup. There was half an inch left in the bottle for morning and she had made that decision before he asked.

Tom drank too hot and burned his tongue and swore at the cup. By the time he slammed it down, the window had gone dark and the cold had thickened in the corners.

"Right," he said. "Here's the bit you won't like."

Elsie sat opposite with her cardigan buttoned to her throat, hands in her lap so he could not see them tremble.

"You've already done that bit."

"No, listen. Ring gets us maybe twenty-five. I can raise another five off Stan."

"Stan?"

"My brother."

"The one who still owes you from Easter?"

"He'll cough if it's family disgrace on the line."

"He'll cough if you tell him a version with you as victim."

"Call it what you like. Between that and a story at Monday hearing, we can contain it." He leaned forward, lowering his voice as if the wall itself took notes. "If they ask where short money went, you say you had me hold housekeeping cash because you were worried about thieves in the lane. You say I mixed bundles by mistake with depot takings during overtime. With weather chaos, they might buy that."

Elsie felt heat rise up her spine, not from the stove. "You want me to stand there and lie for you."

"I want you to keep your husband out of a cell," he said. "Out of court. Out of something the whole lane chews on."

"By putting me in it." She tapped the table once. "I've got three pounds twelve and six in that caddy. Rent's two-six. Coal's one-four if Langley still has sacks. There's no mystery housekeeping bundle for your Monday story."

"Then don't give figures," he said quickly. "You keep it broad. You say you were scared after those lane thefts and shoved notes at me in a hurry. Snow week, overtime, everyone mixing things up."

"Mine aren't mixed."

"Els, come on. You're my wife."

"I'm your wife, and I'm the one they'll question if you use my name."

Tom dragged a hand across his mouth, buying a second. "All right, different route." He pointed toward the front room where their wedding photograph sat on the mantel, silver frame gone black at the corners. "Ring goes to Dent's till payday. One week, maybe two. You keep the ticket in your apron. If anybody asks, you needed coal money."

She leaned in, voice low. "You're not taking this ring, and I'm not feeding another lie into your depot file."

"Don't talk like you're clean in this if I go down," he snapped. "Barrow Lane won't split hairs. They'll call you thief's wife, and they'll put it on the baby too."

"They already count every potato in my basket when I buy on credit. A police statement is new trouble, and you know it."

The urge to throw the cup at him came so suddenly she almost smiled at it. She set the cup down instead, carefully, and laced her fingers together until the knuckles hurt.

"No," she said. "I kept this house warm when there was no coal. I kept food moving when your wages wandered. I took shame in spoonfuls because I thought we were on the same side. You made me collateral without asking, and now you want my ring and my mouth as well."

He rubbed his hands over his scalp, flattening his hair and lifting it again in the same motion. "Christ, Els, don't make me beg in my own kitchen."

"Then stop bargaining with things you've already spent." She held up her hand and folded one finger after another as she spoke. "No pawning the ring, and no statement with my name in it. Tomorrow you go to Stan and raise what you can without forging, gambling, or touching this house's coupons. Monday you tell the depot hearing what you took and when."

"You're insane." He jabbed two fingers at the tabletop. "You just wrote my conviction speech."

"I wrote the only version I can repeat when they put me under a light."

"You want me to confess intent?"

"You took it four times. Call it what you like."

"Don't use that word at me again."

"Then give me another word that isn't a lie."

The silence that followed had weight. Outside, someone dragged a dustbin along the frozen flags and the metal shrieked all the way to the corner.

Tom stood and began pacing between stove and sink, three strides each way. "All right. Alternative. I pawn it myself on my name, not yours. Hear me out. I bring back the slip, you lock it in the tin, done. If everything settles, I buy back before anyone sees."

"No. If that ring leaves this house, Dent takes his cut and I have nothing left to show for ten years except the gap on my hand."

"You'd rather nurse a principle while transport police knock?"

"I'd rather stay out of the lie that gets typed."

She felt her pulse in the scar line under her wedding band, a hot quick beat that had nothing to do with the stove. He was trying to make panic sound like common sense again.

"You think truth protects you?" He turned, face blotched with cold and rage. "Truth's for men with solicitors. People like us survive on stories that pass inspection."

"Your stories are why we're here with no wages on Friday."

He stopped pacing. "Say the line," he said.

"What line?"

"Say you'll stand with me Monday. Sit there, look worried, keep it simple. That's all I need."

"I'm not sitting in that depot office nodding through a story about cash I never gave you."

She pictured Monday's office in one hard flash: wet coats steaming on chair backs, men pretending not to stare at her belly while they waited for her to lie cleanly.

"Don't give me chapel talk."

He stepped closer until she could smell stale ale sweetening on his breath. "Then say it plain. You with me or not?"

Elsie met his eyes and felt the room narrow to the width of the table between them.

"Not like this," she said. "Not through my ring. Not through my mouth lying for you."

He stared as if he had misheard a foreign language.

"After all this," he said, quiet now, almost gentle, which frightened her more than the shouting had. "After years, you'd choose a piece of gold over me."

"I'm choosing rent paid Friday and my name off your betting mess."

He gave her a look she had seen only once before, when the stillbirth had ended with clean sheets and no baby cry and he had stood at the foot of the bed unable to speak. Hurt and fury braided together until they were one rope.

"Fine," he said. "You want lines? Here's one. If I walk out tonight, I don't carry this rent next week. I don't carry coal. I don't carry your little social smile with the neighbors either. You can explain my absence however clever you like."

"That's Friday rent gone and next coal sack gone. Say threat if you mean threat."

"It's arithmetic." He snatched his cup and hurled the dregs into the sink. "You think you've been doing sums alone?"

She got to her feet too fast and had to catch the table edge. Pain pulled low across her abdomen, a hard belt, then eased.

Tom saw it and softened for one beat. "Sit down," he muttered. "Don't start anything with the baby because of this."

"Don't point at my pregnancy to win this." Her voice dropped until it almost vanished. "You want to leave, go. You want to stay, you stay under terms I can live with."

"Terms." He showed his teeth with no humor in it. "You've turned into a solicitor overnight."

"No. I turned into the person who has to remember exactly what happened when men start asking questions."

He flinched as if she had slapped him.

"Jesus," he said. "Who are you now?"

She thought of Noreen's swollen ankles in clinic shoes, of Keane's dry voice in the hallway yesterday: `If she seizes in this weather, you may not get a second chance.` She thought of the pram receipt with her forged name. She thought of the baby inside her and how little room was left for pretending.

"I'm the person left counting coins and answering doors when you've spent your charm," she said.

For a moment he looked ready to strike the table again, maybe the wall, maybe anything that sounded louder than shame. Instead he grabbed his coat from the hook, shoved his arms into it, and headed for the front room.

Drawers opened, shut, and opened again.

Elsie followed, one hand on the hallway wallpaper to steady herself. "What are you doing?"

"You said go. I'm going. Stan's got a settee."

"Take your own clothes. Leave the ration book."

He turned with a shirt half-hanging from his bag. "Don't be ridiculous."

"Ration book stays here."

"I need my coupons."

"You can get replacements through your brother if you're out of household. You don't take this house's food with you."

His mouth opened, shut. "You're serious."

"About food? Always."

"Fuck this, Elsie. Fuck your bloody terms," he said, not even trying to keep it down. The words smacked the plaster and carried straight through the wall; next door, a chair scraped and then stopped dead. The baby jolted hard under Elsie's palm, and heat climbed up her neck.

"Happy now?"

"No."

He jammed socks and a shaving tin into the bag, grabbed his bus cap from the peg, and paused at the door with his hand on the latch.

"When they come asking," he said without turning, "remember you had a chance to help me make this small."

Elsie's hand went to her ring by reflex. She pressed the band into her palm until the edge marked her skin.

"When they come asking," she said, "I'll tell what I know and no more."

His throat worked around something he did not say, then he yanked the door open.

Cold blew in sharp enough to sting her teeth. His boots pounded down the two steps, suitcase knocking against his knee, and then the gate banged once, twice, like punctuation.

The house settled around the silence he left.

*

For five full minutes Elsie did not move. She stood in the hallway with one hand on the peeled banister and listened to every sound she had not noticed while he was filling them: the tick of the clock, the soft simmer from the kitchen, a cough through the shared wall, the steady drip where thaw had started to find a crack above the front window.

On the sixth she lurched to the sink and gripped enamel hard enough to squeal it against the wall. Her heart kicked at her throat in fast dirty thuds. She bent over the basin and retched up pepper broth and spit, strings of it hanging from her lip before she wiped them with the heel of her hand. The baby drove one blunt heel into her side and held it there until her breath came back in thin pulls.

Then she went to the table and opened the tea caddy.

Three pounds, twelve and six in silver, one and nine in copper. Not enough for rent and coal together. Not enough if Monday brought a police knock and a landlord with opinions. She drew Tom's letter toward her and laid it beside her own scrap list.

She dragged the scrap closer and wrote if no Tom wage down the side. The pencil bit so hard it tore the wrapper and kept going. She came up fourteen shillings short on rent before she even counted coal, with only half a scuttle left and milk for maybe two days if she stretched it thin. A drip from the thaw crack above the window hit her wrist as she wrote clinic fare for Nora?.

She stared at the last line until the word blurred, crossed out `Nora`, and wrote `Noreen` back in, firmer this time, as if the full name might make the problem sturdier.

From next door came a faint knock on the shared wall: two taps, pause, one tap. Their new code since chapter ten's quiet mornings, a question dressed as plaster.

Are you there?

Elsie tapped back once.

In a minute the back latch lifted and Noreen slipped in through the scullery door, wrapped in a grey cardigan worn inside out so the seam would not bite her swollen wrists. She carried an enamel mug and a folded dish towel under one arm. Her hair was pinned up badly, escaping in damp curls at her neck, and one slipper heel had been crushed flat from being stepped on in a hurry.

"I heard the gate," Noreen said quietly. "And him saying Christ knows what in the lane. You all right?"

Elsie nodded, then shook her head because there was no useful shape for the truth.

Noreen set the mug on the table. "Barley broth. I put too much pepper in because Peter won't eat it that way. Lucky you."

Even now, even with her ankles puffed above her socks and a bruise-yellow tiredness under both eyes, she found room for mean little jokes like that. It was one of the things Elsie trusted most in her.

"Sit," Noreen said. "You're white as the sink."

"I'm fine."

"You're vertical. That's not the same."

Elsie sat.

Noreen lowered herself onto the opposite chair with both hands braced on the table, breath hissing once as her back protested. She glanced toward the front hall, then back. "Did he hurt you?"

"No." Elsie touched her ring again before she could stop herself. "He wanted this pawned. Wanted me to lie for him at depot hearing."

Noreen's face hardened, then flickered with something like pity for Tom before she smothered it. "And?"

"And he stole from work to pay gambling debt. About twenty from depot short bag so far. Owes bookie one-forty-seven. Hearing Monday."

Noreen let out a breath through her teeth. "Sweet Jesus."

"He said if I stood with him we'd make it small."

"By making you an accessory." Noreen rubbed her lower belly absentmindedly, fingertips tracing slow circles through the wool, the way she did when worry climbed too high. "What did you say?"

"No ring. No lie. Confess or don't, but without me in it."

Noreen gave a tiny nod, then reached into her cardigan pocket and pulled out a wrapped eucalyptus sweet. She always kept one there because the iron tablets made her gag and mint made it worse. She unwrapped it, sniffed, and set it on the table without eating it.

"So he left," she said.

"Settee at his brother's."

"Then he left." Noreen's voice went flat on the word, no soft edges. "That's the fact."

Elsie felt tears sting and hated them instantly. They came anyway. Her chest hitched twice, then locked; she set the mug down too fast and broth slopped over her fingers as a cramp cinched low across her belly.

Noreen was up before the mug stopped rocking. She cupped Elsie's jaw with one hand and pressed the other into the small of her back. "Breathe with me. Slow. In through your nose. That's it. You're here."

"I told him to go," Elsie said when she could force words out. "I wanted him gone for months, and when the latch went I wanted to run after him and drag him back by the sleeve. What's wrong with me?"

"Nothing's wrong with you. He made home feel like a fire and a blanket in the same hour for years. Your body doesn't drop that habit because he slammed one door."

Elsie scrubbed her wet face with both hands. "I'm furious at him. I'm scared for him. I'm scared I'll carry his mess in my name till this child's grown."

"You can be scared and done," Noreen said. "Both fit in one body. I've been carrying both since Peter started counting out my bus fare like parish dole."

"I keep hearing the gate."

"You'll hear it all night." Noreen pushed the mug back into Elsie's hands. "Drink first. We can bully tomorrow into shape when it shows up, not marry what your heart wanted tonight to be."

Elsie swallowed broth that tasted of pepper and bay and cheap pearl barley, thick enough to feel like food. Warmth spread into the hollow behind her ribs where panic had sat all evening.

"I don't know what to tell people," she said.

"Tell them what keeps you safest." Noreen shrugged, grimacing when the movement pulled her side. "Night rotations, brother ill, depot overtime. Pick one plain story and wear it threadbare till spring."

"If police come, story falls apart."

"Then you don't start with lies you can't carry. Keep it mean and dull." Noreen rubbed her thumb against the eucalyptus wrapper, eyes on Elsie. "You know he had debt. You know he walked after a row. After that, you've got nothing to add. That's true, and true is lighter to haul."

Elsie stared at her. "You're good at this now."

Noreen barked a laugh. "Practice."

For a moment they sat in the kitchen's thin warmth, shoulders hunched against drafts, the wall clock ticking them forward whether they were ready or not.

"Peter knows?" Elsie asked.

"He knows there was shouting. He pretended not to hear words because hearing words means obligations." Noreen reached for the eucalyptus sweet, then put it back untouched. "He'll ask in the morning and I'll tell him as little as possible. Arthur heard the gate too, likely. He was in the spare room mending that ridiculous cuff of his where sparks burned it again."

Elsie pictured Arthur with his frost-cracked knuckles and patient quiet, stitching work shirt cuffs with electrician's tape on the table because thread kept snapping in the damp. Another set of eyes she had not chosen.

"Social cover's gone," she said. "It's one thing when a husband vanishes for a night. Bag in hand is another."

"Aye." Noreen looked at her directly. "And cover isn't food."

Elsie let that sit.

Noreen leaned back carefully, one hand at her lower spine. "Let's do sums. What have you got cash and coin?"

"Three twelve and six, plus coppers."

"Rent's two-six."

"And coal's one-four if Langley still has sacks."

"Then rent gets paid first, so nobody's got an excuse to nose into your rooms." Noreen lifted one shoulder in a tired half-shrug. "I'll stretch ours for a week and walk to Bhatti with you Saturday. She'll carry tab if she can see your face and not just Tom's promises."

"You need your own food. Keane said salt down, rest up, no drama."

Noreen rolled her eyes. "Keane says many correct things in one breath. Rest is the one this street never stocked."

"Nora."

"I know." She pressed her lips together and nodded once. "I know. I've been seeing little lights at the edge again when I stand too quick. I won't pretend." She gave Elsie a look half-defiant, half-weary. "But I'm not leaving you alone in this kitchen tonight with that face."

Elsie reached across and laid her hand over Noreen's. The skin was warm and dry, knuckles rough from washing nappies in water that never stayed hot long enough.

"Thank you," Elsie said.

Noreen's thumb moved once under her palm, a private stroke gone as soon as it happened.

From next door Peter called, voice formal even in irritation. "Noreen? Where have you gone?"

Noreen closed her eyes for a second. "There he comes, as reliable as tax." She stood, using the table as leverage, and winced. "I'll come back first light. We'll get your words straight before milk rounds."

At the door she turned. "Hide the ring somewhere stupid, not obvious. Men always check sewing tin first."

Despite everything, Elsie gave a breath of laughter. "Where then?"

"Flour bin if you've got any flour left. Nobody digs there unless they're saints or starving."

When Noreen had gone and the scullery latch clicked, Elsie took the ring off for the first time since her wedding day.

The pale line beneath it looked indecent, skin that had not seen air in ten years. She held the ring between finger and thumb, felt its heat fade, then walked to the larder and pushed it deep into the last three inches of flour in the tin, under a folded tea cloth so she could not see the spot and make herself mad by checking every minute.

She washed the cup, banked the fire, and laid Tom's depot letter flat under the breadboard where damp would not curl it.

At half six, Mrs Pritchard from the end house knocked to return the enamel pie dish from Christmas and let her eyes travel once over the hallway where Tom's boots were missing.

"Quiet in here," Mrs Pritchard said.

"Tom's with his brother tonight," Elsie said, voice neutral. "Family matter."

"Oh." Mrs Pritchard held the dish a little tighter. "Right. Well. If you need paraffin, our lad got an extra can."

The offer carried curiosity like a second handle.

"Thank you," Elsie said. "I'll remember."

After she shut the door, she leaned her forehead against the cold wood and counted to twenty.

Before she got back to the table, three hard knocks hammered the front door, too square for a neighbor and too sober for Tom. A narrow lad in a wet cap stood on the step with his collar to his ears, cracked front tooth showing when he spoke: Len, Billy Cragg's runner.

"Tom Harrow in? Cragg says twenty by dinner tomorrow or he stops being polite."

"He's not here."

Len leaned to look past her into the hallway. "Then I'll ask next door. Folks answer faster when everyone's listening." Through the shared wall came a thump of movement from Walsh's side, chair leg or boot heel, and Elsie caught the door before he could turn away. If he started naming numbers at that wall, Peter would hear every one, and Noreen would pay for it in blood pressure and questions.

"Wait." She went to the tea caddy, peeled one pound note from under the sugar bowl, and folded it twice before pressing it into Len's gloved hand. "You give Cragg this and tell him no more doorstep theatre."

Len rubbed the note between thumb and forefinger. "One quid buys you till Sunday noon. After that he comes himself."

He left with his boots loud on the flags, no hurry in him at all.

By eight the street had gone muffled again, snow under fresh crust, distant shouts from the pub corner blown flat by wind. Elsie sat at the table with the ration book open and a list of meals for seven days written in her careful hand: cabbage broth twice, potatoes and onion if Bhatti had any, barley stretch, bread heels toasted, egg only if she could trade for one.

Under it she wrote, hard enough to score the table through the paper, tell no lies you can't keep straight.

She started a fresh scrap and wrote, "He left after a row about money," then stopped when the front latch jumped under a hard shove.

Tom's voice came through the wood, thick with drink and cold. "Open the fucking door, Els. I left my key."

She stood so fast the chair legs screamed on stone. The baby rolled hard under her ribs. She slid the bolt across, palm slick, and kept her mouth near the panel. "You'll come back tomorrow sober. Not tonight."

"Don't you make me a spectacle out here." His fist hit the door again. Across the lane a curtain twitched. In Walsh's front room floorboards creaked once, then twice.

"Go to Stan's," she said. "You're not coming in shouting."

He cursed, kicked the bottom panel, then went quiet enough for her to hear him breathing.

"You lock me out now, it's done," he said.

"It's done because you made it done."

Something metal scraped through the letter slot and clattered across the hallway tiles. His house key spun once, rang, and stopped against the skirting board.

"Keep it then," he shouted, the words already moving away toward the gate. "Keep your bloody house."

Bootsteps slipped on the frozen flags, then the gate slammed and the lane swallowed him.

Elsie picked up the key. It stayed warm from his hand for one second and then went cold as sink water.

In bed, the mattress felt too wide and too cold on the side where he had slept. The springs complained every time she turned. Through the wall she heard Noreen vomiting quietly into a basin, then the soft murmur of water, then Arthur's low voice in the hallway offering to fetch more coal bricks from the shed. Peter answered with a curt no that meant yes if no one named it that.

Elsie lay on her left side and watched the moonlight make bars across the ceiling through frost-scratched glass. Her baby moved once, then settled.

She did not pray. She tucked Tom's key under the mattress seam and listened while pub shouts thinned to coughs and the drain outside began ticking with meltwater, one drop, two, then a run.

By morning she would be a woman with a husband elsewhere. By Monday she might be a thief's wife in every mouth on Barrow Lane. By next week, if Tom's wages stopped fully, she would be choosing between coal and clinic fares with a child pressing down in her own body and another neighbor's blood pressure rising next door.

She put her hand over the hard curve of her belly and spoke into the dark, not loud enough for anyone else to hear.

"We'll keep the door," she said. "We'll keep the truth as near to plain as this street allows."

In the kitchen, her torn sums sat under the sugar bowl: cash 2-14-3, rent 2-6, coal 1-4. Fifteen and nine short before milk, before clinic fare, before morning.
Somewhere after midnight, rain turned to the dry hiss of ice against the glass.


# Chapter 14

On the landing, the Harrow clock struck two and Arthur coughed once in the spare room. Noreen sat on the edge of the bed with both palms under her belly, waiting to see whether the tightening would pass. It did not.

The coal in the range had sunk to a red eye, and ice gritted against the front window with little clicks like teeth. Her feet were already swollen before she put them on the rug, the skin stretched shiny where her slippers rubbed. She moved slowly because the headache from the clinic warning had come back behind her right eye, a steel pin turned one quarter at a time.

The next pain crossed her belly while she reached for the chamber pot. It did not rise and fall. It took hold low and deep and dragged her breath out of her like someone pulling wet linen through a wringer. She stayed bent over the mattress until it eased. Then she counted. Keane had told her to count anything she could trust: clock ticks, kettle whistles, the distance between pains. Numbers did not panic.

She got to six minutes. Then five. On the third pain she stood and warm water spilled down her calf, clear at first, then clouded pink when it hit her heel.

"Elsie," she said to the wall before she remembered a wall did not answer.

Arthur was in the spare room on the other side of the landing. She knocked once and he was up at once, boots in his hand, shirt half buttoned. He slept in a wool cap with a rent at the crown because the back room chimney drew wrong and dropped soot on the pillows.

"It's started," she said. "It's not practice. It's proper." 

Arthur did not ask whether she was sure. He looked at the water on her nightdress hem and nodded once. "I'll fetch Mrs Harrow and ring Keane. Phone at the substation still works if the line hasn't iced." He jammed his cap down and glanced at her feet. "You want the fire up now?"

"Yes. And the kettle. Two kettles if they boil." She gripped the bedpost until another pain climbed through her spine. "Where's Peter?"

"Town Hall side. He said he'd walk back after the late ledger shift." Arthur's mouth flattened. "No one walks quick in this."

He was gone before she could answer. She heard his tread on the stairs, then the front latch, then the storm swallowing him. Noreen braced both hands against her belly and breathed the way Keane had shown her, in through teeth, out in a slow thread that made room for the next one.

Elsie came through the back door in Tom's old duffel, her own coat hanging open over her stomach because nothing buttoned now. She had wrapped a scarf over her hair and forgotten to tie the ends, so the wool tails swung when she moved. Her face was pale in the firelight, the color of skimmed milk, but her hands were steady.

"I'm here," Elsie said. "Arthur's gone for Keane and ambulance. Sit. No, don't sit that far back, you'll trap your tailbone. Here." 

Noreen laughed once at that and the laugh broke in the middle because the next contraction had started. Elsie crouched in front of her and put both palms on Noreen's knees, grounding them. She smelled of smoke, Pears soap, and the sharp iron scent that came when snow hit hot coal.

"You're shaking," Noreen said when it passed.

"Course I'm shaking. Doesn't matter. Tell me how far apart."

"Five. Then maybe less. My water went." Noreen touched her own mouth, trying to keep words from spilling too quickly. "My head's bad again. I've got lights on the edge."

"Shit," Elsie said under her breath, the word small and hard. "Keane said headache with lights means no waiting about. We'll set the front room and keep you warm. Have you bled much?"

"Only streaks so far." 

Elsie nodded and stood with one hand braced at her back. The movement cost her; Noreen saw it in the pause at the top, that tiny freeze while she caught her own breath around her own child. Then Elsie was all task.

She pulled sheets from the airing cupboard, rejected the first set for damp, took the older coarse pair that had dried nearest the grate. She sent Noreen to the privy before pains grew stronger, then changed her into Peter's old clean shirt because buttons were easier than a nightdress knot with wet hands. She brought towels from both houses, counted them out loud, and folded them by size on the sideboard.

"These small ones are for the baby. The large ones are for you. Clean rags go in this basin. Dirty ones go in the coal scuttle once it's empty. Don't argue."

"You're bossy when frightened," Noreen said, breathless.

"I'm bossy when there isn't time."

Another contraction hit and Noreen leaned into Elsie's shoulder. Through the wall, the Harrow clock struck two. Somewhere on Barrow Lane a bottle crate blew over and clattered down the cobbles until it smashed.

Arthur came back twenty-five minutes later white to the brows, eyelashes crusted with frost, carrying Keane's canvas bag and a paraffin tin under his arm.

"She'll be five minutes behind me," he said. "She made me take these because she said I'd drop her and not the bag. Ambulance knows. They said maybe an hour, maybe more. They've got a jackknifed lorry near the viaduct."

"Did you get through clear?" Elsie asked.

"Clear enough. Operator had to patch me twice." He put the paraffin by the range and flexed his fingers one by one, checking each one still answered him. The knuckles were split and dark where old skin had opened again in the cold. "I primed the spare lamp. Want it in here or landing?"

"In here," Noreen said before Elsie could answer. "I don't want shadows tonight."

He nodded and lit the lamp. His hands stopped shaking only when flame steadied in the glass.

Keane arrived as promised, cheeks raw from wind, hair escaping her pins in silver loops that had gone damp and then stiff. She carried her own boots in one hand for the last step over the threshold and came in stocking-footed so she would not track slush to the bed. The socks were men's grey work socks darned three times at the heel.

"All right," she said, not loud, not soothing. "Tell me what happened and when."

Noreen gave her the times between breaths. Keane listened with the brass watch she wore pinned upside down on her bodice, then warmed the stethoscope head inside her sleeve before setting it on Noreen's skin. She always did that. Noreen had noticed it first at clinic and nearly cried from gratitude at that tiny refusal to make a woman flinch.

Keane examined her quickly, efficiently, eyes narrowing only once. "You're in established labor. Early for dates, yes, but labor all the same. We don't move you unless I lose either heartbeat. Roads are a hazard and you've got pressure signs. We deliver here."

Noreen nodded because there was nothing else to do. Elsie asked, "How early?"

"Likely thirty-four weeks, maybe a shade less. Small baby expected. Cold is our second enemy, bleeding is third. First enemy is delay." Keane snapped open the bag. "Arthur, boil everything that can be boiled. Knife, scissors, enamel bowl. Keep a rolling kettle, not a polite one. Elsie, I need you gloved and ready with me."

"I've got washing gloves," Elsie said.

"Good. Wash your forearms to the elbow with carbolic. Nails now. Noreen, pass urine if you can. Empty bladder gives me room."

Another pain took Noreen and she gripped the mattress ticking. She could smell carbolic soap, paraffin, wet wool, and beneath all of it the old sour mattress heat of the room. Keane spoke over the contraction in short steps she could hold onto.

"Breathe down low. Keep your jaw loose. Don't waste it in your throat. Save it for the push when I ask." 

From the front room Arthur fed the fire until the grate glowed orange-white. Coal was low. He split two broken chair rungs and fed those too, muttering apologies to the furniture like it might forgive him. Every few minutes he stepped to the back door and listened for engine noise that never came.

By three o'clock pains were under four minutes and biting hard. Noreen labored kneeling over the back of a dining chair because lying flat made her dizzy and made the pin behind her eye drill deeper. Sweat ran down her spine though the room stayed cold at the skirting boards. Elsie stood behind her, fist pressed into the small of her back through the shirt, counting breaths in a calm voice that thinned only at the ends.

"Three in, six out. Again. Three in, six out. You've done this all week with the stairs. Same lungs."

"Stairs don't split a woman in two," Noreen said.

"No," Elsie said. "But you swore at those as well and beat them."

Keane checked the fetal heart between contractions, shifting the horn and frowning at the rhythm. "There are dips on the tail. It's not catastrophic yet. Keep going."

Outside, wind hammered the guttering so hard that soot rained down the chimney breast. Arthur set a pan lid over the nursery basin to keep grit out and went back to the door. He had moved from question to routine, and routine was how he managed fear: check lamp, check fire, check road, check kettle, repeat.

At half past three, Noreen vomited thin tea into the basin and immediately apologized. Keane ignored the apology.

"Good," she said. "Body's working. You're near transition."

Noreen wanted to strike her for the word good. She wanted to sleep standing up. She wanted Peter there and not there at once. Another contraction cut through thought and left only noise, a low animal sound she did not recognize as hers until Elsie said, "That's right, use it." 

The front door banged. Arthur went to it and came back with two ambulance men, broad-shouldered and red-faced from the cold, equipment wrapped in army blankets and banging against their knees as they squeezed through the narrow hall. One of them had half his moustache singed off, a clean crescent over his lip where he had leaned too near a heater some other night. He said, "Road's shut at the top. We parked by the chapel and hauled this lot on a handcart." 

Keane did not look up from Noreen's knees. "Good. Set oxygen by the hearth. Bring your transport cot in and warm it. Baby will likely need transfer at once." 

"And mother?" the other man asked.

"Mother stays till she's stable. You don't get both in one trip with those roads." 

The man glanced toward Noreen and then away, not from squeamishness but from arithmetic. He was counting distance, snow depth, fuel, and time.

Noreen was already past embarrassment. She clenched the chair back and pushed when Keane told her, not before. The room narrowed to six commands: breathe, hold, curl, push, pant, rest. Elsie's hand never left her shoulder.

She smelled singed wool and realized belatedly that Elsie had leaned too close to the fire, coat sleeve smoking at the cuff. Arthur slapped it out without comment and moved the coal shovel farther from her foot.

"Don't you dare catch fire in my house," Noreen gasped.

"Bad timing, that," Elsie said, and Noreen laughed again, then bore down into another contraction so fierce it seemed to pry her pelvis apart with iron bars.

Keane's voice sharpened. "Head's there. Slow now. No heroics. Pant."

Noreen panted, cursed, panted again. She felt the burn and then a sliding release that emptied her in one hot rush. Keane's hands moved fast. The room went quiet for half a second that lasted a year.

Then a small wet cough.

It was not a cry. It was a cough like a kitten bringing up milk.

"Baby's out," Keane said. "The baby's tiny, very tiny. Come on, little one, breathe for me." 

She rubbed the baby's back with a rough towel, suctioned the mouth and nose with a rubber bulb, rubbed again. The baby's skin was blue at the lips, red-black at the knuckles, the limbs no thicker than Elsie's wrist. Keane clamped and cut the cord, handed the child to the singed-moustache ambulance man at the warming cot, then changed her mind and took the child back to give two measured breaths through a tiny mask, counting under her breath.

"One and two. Pause. Again." 

A weak cry came then, high and reedy, and Noreen's whole body jerked toward the sound.

"Let me see." It came out hoarse, less command than plea.

Keane brought the baby close enough that Noreen could touch with two fingers. The child's foot was no larger than a matchbox, heel translucent. Noreen slid a fingertip along the sole once, with the careful pressure she'd use on wet paper.

"You're here," she said. "Stay here."

The ambulance man fixed an identification band around the tiny ankle and another around Noreen's wrist, a strip of stiff paper inside plastic, numbers written in blunt pencil because ink froze. The band was too big on her from edema gone to slack. It rattled when she moved.

"Special care unit," Keane said. "They're set for prem babies tonight. You won't be with the baby straight off. Hear me? That's not abandonment. That's treatment."

Noreen nodded, tears and sweat and hair all the same wet now. "Tell them I sing off-key," she said to nobody and everybody. "Tell them the baby can complain about that later." 

Elsie bent and kissed her forehead, quick and fierce. "I'll tell them worse than that."

The ambulance men wrapped the baby in warmed blankets around a hot-water bottle tucked safe at the feet and lifted the transport cot. The weak cry faded down the hallway and out into storm. Noreen turned her face toward the door until she could not hear it at all.

Then she felt blood pour out of her.

It was warm at first, then cooling as it spread under her hips. It was too much, and it came too fast. Keane's hand was between her legs instantly, pressing, assessing.

"Placenta not complete," Keane said. "And we're flooding. Elsie, gloves now. Arthur, fresh boiling water and every clean towel left. No one panics."

No one panics, Noreen thought, and began to shake so hard her teeth knocked.

Keane's voice dropped lower, more deliberate, the cadence she used when systems failed and only hands remained.

"Noreen, listen to me. You're hemorrhaging. I need your permission to do painful things quickly."

"Do it," Noreen said.

"Say yes clearly."

"Yes. Do it."

Keane gave an injection into Noreen's thigh, ergometrine from a glass ampoule she snapped with her teeth because her fingers were wet. She massaged Noreen's uterus hard through the abdomen, knuckles pressing deep until Noreen screamed and nearly bucked off the bed.

"Stay with me," Keane said. "Pain means muscle's waking. This is good pain now. This is good pain." 

Elsie climbed onto the mattress behind Noreen, bracing her shoulders with her own body despite the weight of her belly. "Bite this," she said, pushing a folded towel to Noreen's mouth. "Not your tongue."

Noreen bit and tasted bleach and old sun from line-dried cotton. She felt pressure, pulling, another hot gush. Keane worked with relentless hands, not cruel, not kind, exactly enough.

"Placental piece retained," Keane said through clenched teeth. "I'm going in. Hold her steady."

"Fuck, Noreen, stay with me," Elsie whispered, and tightened her grip. Arthur stood at the door with a stack of towels and looked ready to run through brick for them, furious there was no wall he could break that would help.

He set the towels down and turned his back without leaving, giving privacy where he could in a room that had none. He stared at the wallpaper roses and recited transformer fuse sizes under his breath, each number tapped out to keep his hands from shaking.

Noreen left her body for a few seconds at a time. She was on the rug in her mother's kitchen at nine years old, kneading bread with a chipped mug for a cutter. She was sixteen and laughing in the rain outside the dance hall. She was nowhere. Then Keane's hand dragged her back with brutal certainty.

"Look at me," Elsie said into her ear. "Look at my coat button. The blue one. Count the holes."

Noreen fixed on the button until it became a lighthouse lens. It had four holes, and its rim was chipped. The thread was doubled because Elsie never trusted a single line to hold. She counted through another contraction of pain that was not labor now, only survival.

Blood soaked two towels, then three. Keane packed, pressed, waited for tone, pressed again. She asked Arthur to fetch the paraffin lamp closer and then cursed when the flame guttered.

"Fuel's nearly gone," Arthur said.

"Then warm bricks in the grate and wrap them. Keep her from crashing." 

He did it at once, using tongs and old pillowcases, elbows locked and wrists angled away from his coat as though heat could jump. The room smelled of hot clay and blood and paraffin smoke thick enough to sting eyes.

At last the flow slowed from gush to heavy seep. Keane kept one hand on Noreen's abdomen, feeling for firmness, willing muscle to lock down and hold life in place. She checked Noreen's pulse at the neck, counted silently, checked again.

"You're still here," Keane said.

Noreen opened her eyes. "Would've annoyed you if I wasn't," she whispered.

"Very much," Keane said.

Elsie laughed once and then had to sit down hard on the chair because dizziness washed over her. Keane saw it immediately.

"You all right?"

"Just stood too quick." Elsie pressed a palm to her own belly until it passed. "Baby kicked. Didn't like the shouting."

"Your baby can file complaints later," Keane said, echoing Noreen without smiling. "Drink this." 

She handed Elsie sweet tea gone lukewarm. Elsie drank and went back to Noreen's side.

It took another forty minutes for a second ambulance to claw its way into Barrow Lane. The men brought plasma and a stretcher; one wheel jammed at the threshold and the taller one barked, "Bloody hell, lift the bastard."

Keane reported while they worked, each fact tied to a hand already moving.
"She delivered prematurely at four eighteen." The shorter medic tightened the transport strap across Noreen's hips.
"The infant left cyanotic and only started breathing after assisted mask breaths." Noreen turned her head toward the door as if the cry might still be in the hall.
"Postpartum hemorrhage is estimated over a liter, and I removed retained placental tissue manually." Fresh blood warmed through the pad beneath her and Elsie swore, then bit it back.
"I gave ergometrine. Her pulse came from thread to usable, but only just." The taller medic spiked the plasma bag and held it high while his partner searched for a vein in Noreen's cold hand.

They shifted Noreen carefully onto the stretcher. The mattress beneath her looked butchered. Elsie stared at the spread until her own belly cinched hard around the baby and she had to unclench her jaw to breathe. The copper stink sat at the back of her tongue. Noreen's blood had crawled to the bed edge in branching fingers.

Keane snapped her fingers once near Elsie's face. "Stay with me. Help me with blankets." 

They layered Noreen in wool and two coats. The plastic transfer band knocked against the stretcher rail each time the men lifted, a hard dry clack that sounded indecently small after all that blood.

At the front hall, the door burst inward with another gust and Peter stumbled in, glasses fogged, overcoat crusted white to the thigh. His cast was wrapped in a grocery bag tied with string. He took in the stretcher, the blood on Keane's apron, Elsie's coat, Arthur's face, and his own house reduced to triage.

"No one moves her," he said, and shoved the door shut behind him with his shoulder. The bolt shot home under his good hand. The click landed in Noreen's chest like another clamp; her pulse banged under the loose transfer band. "Not until I know where my child was taken and who signed this transfer. You do not shove my wife through that door on guesses."

The stretcher team stopped dead in the narrow hall. One cursed under his breath. Noreen tried to answer and only coughed; cold air scraped her throat raw, and panic flickered when she felt another thin run of blood over the sheet edge.

"Open that door," Keane said.

"I am her husband," Peter said, cast braced against the jamb as though he could hold back weather and law by posture alone. "You will give me names and destination before transfer proceeds, and you will stop talking over me in my own house."

"Then watch what waiting buys you." Keane pulled back the top blanket. Blood spread again, quick and bright. "Set her down. Start plasma now."

The men dropped the stretcher legs and worked in the drafty hall, fingers clumsy from cold. It took two tries to seat the needle. Noreen hissed through her teeth and grabbed Elsie's sleeve hard enough to hurt, fixing on Elsie's blue coat button whenever the hall tilted.
Arthur reached for the bolt. Peter blocked him with his shoulder, and they hit the umbrella stand hard enough to send canes rattling across the tiles.

"Peter, move," Elsie said. Her voice cracked into command. "You're spending her blood to win an argument."

Keane did not look up from the line she was taping. "Obstruction logged. Witnesses present."

Peter turned to Noreen. "Nora, tell her to stop speaking to me like I am some thug hauled in from the yard."

Noreen looked at him through half-lidded eyes, skin grey with exhaustion. She lifted her wrist with effort and showed him the transfer bracelet.

"You're the one in the doorway," she rasped. "Open it."

Peter stared at the band, then at the plasma drip and the blood creeping toward his shoe. His jaw worked once. At last he drew the bolt and stepped back, flattening himself to the wallpaper so the stretcher could pass.

Keane shoved two folded sheets into his good hand as the medics rolled by. "Transfer destination, neonatal identifier, and your report copy. If you want to challenge the timeline, file it at the clinic."

"This household has been managed in my absence by people who do not understand consequence," Peter said, grammar tightening with rage. "When she is stable, I will deal with every one of you."

Arthur spoke before Elsie could. "Consequence is they're alive. Kettle stayed on, fire stayed on, and they're alive."

Peter wheeled on him. "You are a lodger under emergency order. Remember your status."

Arthur shrugged once, tired. "Assigned status tonight was heat, water, and hands where Keane pointed. That's what I kept to."

"You are overstepping," Peter said to Keane.

"No," Keane said. "I'm stepping exactly where your wife needed somebody to stand."

The stretcher moved. Noreen caught Elsie's sleeve as they passed, fingers weak but insistent.

"Don't let him scrub this clean," she whispered. "Don't let him start giving orders like none of this happened."

Elsie bent close. "I won't. I'll go to the hospital as soon as roads open. I'll bring you news if they won't let you travel." 

Noreen's mouth twitched, an attempt at her usual crooked smile that only half arrived. "Bring proper tea. Hospital tea tastes like mop water." 

"I'll bring leaves, not bags," Elsie said.

The ambulance men carried Noreen into wind and white grit. The door shut behind them on a sudden silence that felt obscene after hours of commands and cries.

Peter stood in the hallway holding papers that shook in his hand despite all his effort to appear still. On the table beside him lay the basin of bloodied towels, the clipped cord, Keane's empty ampoule wrappers. Proof refused to become polite.

Keane stripped off her gloves and dropped them into the scuttle. "Mr Walsh. Keep that fire hard. If it drops, the pipes freeze and she comes home to a broken house. Boil water. Carbolic on the bedframe and latch. Don't let neighbors in to gawp. Do you understand?"

He looked at her like she'd insulted his lineage. "I understand instructions perfectly well."

"Good. Then follow these."

She turned to Elsie. "You need to lie down before you fall down. Your face is paper." 

"Not yet, I won't." Elsie was already gathering clean cloths and a bucket. "Not while he's standing there counting sheets like a bloody inspector."

Peter made a small disbelieving noise. "Mrs Harrow, I would advise restraint in your tone, and discretion in what you repeat outside this address."

Elsie did not look at him. She wrung out a cloth in boiling water and laid it over the mattress stain. Steam rose pink. "I'd advise you to heat your front room, boil nappies, and stop spending words on my tone," she said. "And stop trying to edit witnesses."

Peter opened his mouth. Keane cut him off.

"Enough. She's not your employee."

"I am trying to protect this household from malicious interpretation," Peter said, voice tight as wire. "And I will not have you three turn this night into lane gossip."

"You bolted the door on a hemorrhaging woman." Keane was already writing on the ruled card. "That delay is in the report, with time and names."

For a long second no one moved. Wind rattled the letterbox. Somewhere down the lane a child cried and was hushed. Then Peter stepped away from the table so Keane could leave the card in plain view, and because he had just watched three people do work he had always claimed as his right to direct.

Arthur carried the coal bucket in from the yard and set it down with a grunt. "Last of it," he said. "I'll queue at Bhatti's when she opens, if she opens." 

Keane nodded. "Put it on Peter's tab and mine if needed." 

Peter flinched at that, tiny but visible. His thumb went straight to the edge of Keane's report, flattening one crease and then the next until the paper sat square in his hand. Elsie marked it: even now he needed paper straight while blood cooled on the floorboards.

Keane washed her hands in water gone rust-red and wrote one final note on a ruled card, pressing hard enough to tear the paper at the corners. She left the card weighted under the sugar tin where Peter could not pretend he had not seen it.

Before she left for the clinic she crouched by Elsie's chair and spoke low.

"You did well. You followed direction under pressure. If anybody questions that, send them to me."

Elsie nodded. Her hands had begun to tremble now that there was time for tremor. "Will the baby make it?"

Keane did not offer kindness she could not guarantee. "The baby reached special care alive and breathing. That's where certainty ends for tonight." 

She stood, pulled on her boots, and lifted the canvas bag that seemed heavier now though half its supplies were gone. At the door she paused.

"Sleep in shifts," she told Arthur and Elsie. "One ear on the phone if line returns. He'll start correcting everybody by breakfast. Don't let him talk you out of what you saw."

Then she was out into the white dark again.

Arthur banked the fire. Peter went into the front parlor and shut the door with measured care, pretending gentleness with hinges might repair the rest. Elsie stayed in the bedroom alone a moment longer, cloth in hand, staring at the transfer bracelet number copied on Keane's scrap: tiny digits, blunt pencil, proof of a life carried out before sunrise.

She pressed the cloth harder against the mattress until her shoulders hurt. Through the wall she heard Peter on the telephone at last, voice low and formal, giving names and dates, trying to build the night into a ledger he could survive.

In the bed, the hollow where Noreen had labored was still warm.

Elsie laid her palm there and kept it there until the heat went thin.


# Chapter 15

By dawn, after the transfer out of Barrow Lane, the ward smelled of boiled sheets, sour milk, and bleach. Noreen woke with her tongue thick as felt and reached for the jug on the locker. Her arm only got halfway there before her stitches pulled and a hot wire of pain ran under her belly. She stopped, breathed through her teeth, and watched the water tremble in the jug from footsteps in the corridor.

Someone was crying two beds down, not loud, just regular. A baby was making a thin lamb sound from behind the nursery doors, and it was not hers. She knew her own child now by absence, by where the noise did not come from.

The plastic bracelet they had clipped on her at transfer sat against the bone of her wrist: WALSH, FEMALE INFANT, SPECIAL CARE. The letters had rubbed grey where she thumbed them through the night. Her hand still smelled faintly of iodine and old blood. When she lifted the hem of her gown to check the pad, the blood was dark rust now instead of bright, but there was plenty of it. She was still leaking life onto institutional cotton while her daughter lay in a different building under a heat lamp.

A trolley squeaked past the end of the ward carrying metal cups and toast in a covered tin. The woman in the next bed, Mrs Dunn from Sowerby, turned over and pushed her plait off her shoulder with a practiced flick.

"You should ring for tea before they run out," Mrs Dunn said. She had a face full of freckles and a housecoat with tiny blue anchors on it, ridiculous and stubbornly cheerful in March mud weather. "If you wait till matron does rounds you'll get the sad pot."

"I don't want tea," Noreen said.

Mrs Dunn gave her a look over the edge of the blanket, not unkind. "Then ring anyway. Keeps your hand occupied."

Noreen pressed the bell.

Staff Nurse June Firth came in with her cardigan half-buttoned and a men's watch strapped loose over her cuff, the cracked glass mended with clear tape. She always smelled of peppermint because she chewed mints to cover cigarettes between rounds. She took one look at Noreen sitting up and put a palm out, flat and absolute, the same gesture that halted beds, visitors, and arguments.

"Easy," she said. "You're not on your feet yet."

"I need to go to special care."

"No."

Noreen swallowed. "You can wheel me."

"No." Firth moved to the end of the bed, checked the chart, and tapped the page with her thumbnail. "You lost a dangerous amount of blood. Your pressure dropped again at four. You fainted trying to stand for the commode. I'm not sending you on two buses and a corridor walk in this state."

"She's there on her own."

"She's not on her own. She's with nurses and a registrar and a machine that's doing exactly what your body can't do this morning."

"She's mine."

Firth softened for half a breath, then hardened again into ward voice. "I know who she's for, Mrs Walsh. I know it every time I write her number. But policy is policy and weather is weather. No maternal transfers until stable mobility, and no exceptions while beds are doubled up. Half this ward's from blocked roads and half special care's from blocked roads. We're all running short."

Noreen looked at the chart without seeing it. "When can I go?"

"If your pulse behaves and you keep food down, maybe forty-eight hours. Maybe longer."

"Maybe," Noreen said, and hated the word.

Firth put two fingers on the bracelet around Noreen's wrist and turned it so the label faced her. "You can send milk on the eleven o'clock run. We can get you started after breakfast. That's not the same thing. I know it's not. It's still something."

She went away before Noreen could answer.

The toast came. Noreen chewed one corner and could not make it pass. Her breasts ached in a heavy, spreading way, skin hot and tight under the gown. Her body had not got the message that the baby was three miles and three locked doors away. Milk wanted to happen in one room; the child who needed it was happening in another.

By half past nine the ache sharpened. Every small movement dragged. She rang again.

A younger nurse came with a tray: two thick glass bottles, rubber stoppers, labels, and a chipped enamel bowl of hot water. Her name badge said C. MERCER. She had ink on the side of her left hand where she had tested a fountain pen and forgotten to wash it off.

"I'll show you," Mercer said, pulling the curtain. "It's awkward at first. Everybody hates the first go."

"Did she feed at all?"

Mercer shook her head. "Tube feeds. They gave donor for now. Don't look at me like that. Donor keeps tiny ones alive. Your milk will go with the next lot if we can get any."

Noreen sat higher, wincing. Mercer folded a towel, wedged it behind her back, and talked her through it with mending-basket patience: warm cloth first, hand position, press back then forward, do not pinch. The first drops were yellow, sticky, and so scant they barely counted as drops. Noreen cried anyway, silent, while Mercer pretended not to notice and handed her the bottle.

By the time Keane arrived near eleven, Noreen had filled barely a finger's depth in one bottle and nothing worth capping in the second. Keane shook rain out of her coat onto the lino and set her canvas bag on the chair.

"Do not apologize for quantity," she said before Noreen could speak. "The first day is often this. It comes in proper later."

Keane's chapped hands were pink at the knuckles. Her scarf had come loose and one end was pinned with a thistle-shaped brooch, dented from years of catches and doors. She looked older than she had in the Walsh kitchen, or maybe hospital lighting scraped everybody down to bone.

"I want to see her," Noreen said.

"I know."

"They keep saying policy like that word should make me grateful."

"It's not for gratitude. It's for queue management and infection control and fear of lawsuits nobody here can afford." Keane pulled the chart closer and read, lips moving. "Your blood pressure's still unstable on standing. Your haemoglobin is very low. If you collapse in a corridor, they lose both of you for an hour while people fetch people. They're trying to avoid that." She looked up. "I'm explaining, not blessing it."

Noreen wiped her face with the heel of her hand. "How is she?"
